THE RED PAGE .
A CLERGYMAN’S WIFE.
SHE has lived thirty-two years in Australia —
Thirty Years in Australia is Che title of her book
(Methuen; 2s. 6d.)— and “we have been home-
sick practically all the time—good as Australia
has been to us. At any moment of these thirty
odd years we would have made for our native land like
homing pigeons, could we have found the means ; it was
only the lack of the necessary ‘ sinews ’ that prevented
us.” So Ada Cambridge writes of herself and her hus-
band, G. F. Cross, Anglican clergyman at Williamstown,
near Melbourne.
The persistency of England in her is the chief reason
why Ada Cambridge’s novels, written on a plane of
moderate merit, have been commercially profitable. She
has two audiences. Her books are written in Australia,
more or less about Australia ; but they are characteristi-
cally English. The Australian element is sufficient to
give them a pleasant novelty for English palates ; yet it
is not obtrusive, it is not foreign. Typical Australian
writers are foreign for English readers : they write of
things that the average Englishman has not before heard
of, in a way that the average Englishman cannot compre-
hend. .The typical Australian writer takes Australia
for granted : he does not explain things ; and
thus he misses the English audience. But Ada Cam-
bridge is not a typical Australian writer : she
is an English writer resident in Australia, pre-
serving always the English point of view. In Aus-
tralia we can 'still appreciate the English point of view,
while relishing the Australian landscape ; so that our
authoress makes the best of both hemispheres. Novels
of the same quality as hers would be scarcely profitable
if sold in Australia alone : we have not yet population
enough.
Mrs. Cross is 5S years of age ; she has been married for
82 years ; she has published nearly a score of books ; and
the last, Thirty Years in -Australia , is the book- of her
life. The book of her heart may not be written by a
clergyman’s wife. Here, as in Unspoken Thoughts, A
Marked Man, and elsewhere, we are given glimpses
behind the decent veil—glimpses of revolt, sad glimpses.
But a clergyman’s wife must march with the regiment.
**
Thirty Years in Australia can be read with
pleasure, though it is concerned chiefly with the
trivial details (to her so momentous) of Ada Cambridge’s
married life. The book is singularly shallow, considering
how deeply run the currents of the writer’s character.
Only two or three pages have literary value. But the style
of the whole is so simple, so sincere, so natural and
womanly, that one cannot help being charmed with
it. We read how Mrs. Cross was “ the most sea-
sick lady they had ever carried ”to Australia; how
she “ never hired a sewing - woman—having made
all my own clothes as a girl, even to the wedding-
gown, I made all my children’s, until the boys
grew beyond their sailor suits, and the girl put her hair
up how “ housework has all along been the business
of life ; novels have been squeezed into the odd times” ;
how “ the Murray wriggles between its two colonies like
a length of waved dress braid” ; how if a son had lived
“he would have been a man of thirty now—married
doubtless, and perhaps to some woman who would have
made him wretched ” ; how Archibald Forbes fell so
deeply in love—“ in a strictly platonic fashion, of
course ” —with “ the gifted lady who painted flowers
so marvellously”; how Mrs. Cross used to walk
about Melbourne at night with the governess—
“ in about three garments, the most delightful costume
that I ever wore in my life,” and was never molested or
annoyed ; how Mrs. Cross went to a private hospital in a
black hour, and was overcharged by the chloroformist;
how “ within one year [lBB6] 270 new companies were
registered in Melbourne, having an aggregate nominal
capital of fifty-two millions ” —and Mr. Cross, by hurry-
ing, just managed to get his stipend deposited before his
bank closed (for months) ; how Mrs. Cross boomed with
the rest, and received £197 for the serial rights of A
Marked Man “ where now shillings are hard to come by ”
(in The Australasian office !) ; how Mrs. Cross travelled
with a Governor’s wife in a saloon railway carriage, and
the Governor’s wife was prostrated by the heat, and the
Governor held a wet cloth against the window, and a
Minister was asked to get the carriage hosed, and
couldn’t because “It would blister the paint!”—how’
“ the father of the family likes his bed, and is not in the
habit of exciting himself”; how Mrs. Cross had one boy
at Melb. Grammar School, and one at Geelong, and at the
schools’ boat-race used to wear a breast-knot of light-
blue and dark-blue ribbons, “mixed in exactly equal pro-
portions”; how she, the rector’s wife, surreptitiously
sent her puddings to a priest’s table when there was
company, “ for our puddings, 1 may modestly state, were
good”; how “When Edward Wilson of The Argus
bequeathed the charge of his interests in that paper to
the friend who for thirty years conserved them so
well . . . the last instructions of the dying man were :
‘ Keep it gentlemanly, and never let them be mean.’ ”
All that, and much more—the whole work-box.
Then, there are some stories—too few. Why
did not Mrs. Cross give us more stories? It testifies to
her sweetness that she never lost a servant-girl until
marriage, and that she has been petted by the best society
of Melbourne. But she has not “made copy” out of
her society—or not in this book. She did not wish
to —very well. This is her book, not a book for you—
all right. Then, what have we ? The story of the verger
who stood for congregation at a marriage service, but
could not read responses, so was instructed to say
“ Amen.” “ Every time G. paused to take a breath, no
matter where, a loud ‘ Amen ! ’ was shot into the breach.”
“ Who giveth this woman to be married to this man?”
“ Amen ! ”
The story of the bush concert where “ our priina donna,
a young married lady, confessed to riot feeling very well.”
“However, she went through the two hours programme
unflinching! j r .” “The situation had grown desperate
before she was nearly through with her last number.”
“ When the rest of us got home afterwards, it was to
hear that our prima donna had become a mother rather
less than two minutes after gaining the shelter of her
own house.’’—And “ I think,” says Mrs. Cross, with a
charming little air of reflection, “I think that was the
most interesting concert I was ever at.” (Note the adj.)
The story of the fashionable Melb. house-party, in-
cluding the Boseberys, which one afternoon went out to
their host’s tennis-ground. They found a crowd of cheap
holiday excursionists in the middle of an exciting game.
“ Did they seurrv away, scared, on the appearance of the
smart folks from" the house ? Did anybody order them
off, or even request them to desist ? Not a bit of it.
They calmly continued their game, which took along
time, while we sat down meekly and waited. When they
had quite done they trooped away without a word, and
then Lord Rosebery wearily took up his racquet and
started to play.”
O brave democracy 1
Jt
Ada Cambridge has had eight homes in Aus-
tralia—a clergyman’s wife I Pathetic clergymen's wives :
in these very domesticated thirty years in Australia
we hear all about their babies and their troubles.
Mrs. Cross’s own new baby pops up at every fortieth page
or so ; and (tears of mothers !) by-and-by she tells us of
this death in babyhood, that death in boyhood, the death
of “my eldest son, in the prime of his fine young man-
hood—which for me has altered the whole face of the
worl'd and of the future.”
A h me / the hidden griefs
Watered with tears :
Centuries, Time the thief's
Stolen from years !
This wonderful woman! one thinks. Husband and
family; births and deaths; seventeen books or more;
and withal an invalid for long, yet making her own
dresses, her own furniture, helping and supervising her
household, breaking-up her home seven times, and, in
the earlier years, assisting in the work of the parish !
The tragedy of clergymen’s wives !
Even Mrs. Cross glows when she thinks of it, though
she says “I, however, have not been a victim.” Yet “it
is not surprising to find a pious man killing his wife by
inches without having the slightest notion that he is
doing so.” And she cites a horrifying example.
Amongst my colleagues of those [earlier] days was a
lady of exceptional culture and refinement. Her hus-
band, a Bush clergyman like my own, was poor, of
course, and they averaged a baby a year until the
baker’s dozen was reached, if not passed. The wav she
“ kept ” this family was such that I never saw a dirty
child or a soiled table-cloth, or a slatternly touch of
any sort in her house. She taught the children as they
grew old enough ; I know that she did scrubbing and
washing with her own hands. In addition, she did
“ the parish work.”
One day, when she was run down and worn out, her
husband told her that the organist, from some cause,
was not forthcoming, and there was no time to procure
a substitute. “ So, my dear, you will have to play for
us.” He knew that she could do it, for she had often
done it before ; it was the merest trifle of a task, com-
pared with those she hourly struggled with ; but it
was the one straw too many that breaks the over-
loaded back. She looked at him in silence for a
moment, flung out her arms wildly, and, exclaiming
“ I can do no more ! ” went mad upon the spot.
Happy clergyman’s wife ! she marches with the regiment
no longer.

The Newspaper Press Directory of C. Mitchell
and Co., London, now in its 58th year of issue, includes
simply the best Australasian Press Directory extant.
(Nevertheless, as to minor newspapers, it needs revision
to date.)
A new cookery book is called Please , M’m, the
Butcher !
“ Boondi's Book,” “by ‘ Boondi,’of the ‘Sunday
Times ’ and ‘ Referee ’ —AOareful Selection of the Author’s
Most Entertaining Writings,” is published by the Sunday
Times Newspaper Company, Limited, Sydney. The con-
tents are referred to in a preface as “ journalistic jottings
(original and otherwise).” Readers will find the “ other-
wise ” more prominent than the “ original.” We cannot
see that “ Boondi ” has added anything of value to the
long extracts that he has scissored or paraphrased from
J. M. Bailey, J. K. Jerome, and sources unacknowledged.
The staple of Boondi’s Book is supplied by J. M. Bailey,
the American humourist famous as “The Banbury
Newsman.” “ Boondi” usually introduces his quotations
with “My friend Bailey says ” ; but there is no further
acknowledgment, and probably not one Australian reader
in a thousand is aware that the passages following this
introduction are “conveyed” from Life in Danbury.
Indeed, it is likely that some readers think that “My
friend Bailey” is merely a peg ui)on which “Boondi”
hangs his humourous observations, in the same way that
F. P. Dunne makes use of “ Mr. Dooley” and “ My fri’nd
Hinnessv.” Colour is given to this idea by the insertion
of local allusions (such as “ like carrots at Paddy’s
Market,” p. 108) in “ The Danbury Newsman’s ” matter.
Bpondi’s Book contains twenty-five sketches reprinted
from The Sunday Times. In the first sketch, “House
Hunting,” some two columns are credited to “ a man of
bitter experience” (unspecified). “The Joys of a Jam-
boree,” out of eight columns, has six columns credited to
J. K. Jerome. The other two columns include references
to “ King Gygees ” and “ Heretodus” that are certainly
original. In “ Why Wear Clothes?” we strike “My
friend Bailey ” and scraps from various sources. “ The
Worried Woman and the Hatching Hen ” is made up of
“a good story I once read” and J. M. Bailey’s “ Driving
a Hen ” —not acknowledged, and not improved by para-
phrasing. “ The Awfulness of Arithmetic” is eqmposed
of “a little tale of the Tyrol” (two columns), Jerome
(two columns), scraps, and commentary. “ Winter Wag-
geries” contains a paraphrase of Max “ Adder’s ”
story of the man whose house-door slammed on
his nightshirt. “Figure Facts and Fancies” has four
columns of “ My friend Bailey,” scraps, and commentary.
“The Sorrows of Spring”—“my friend Dailey”—four
columns. “The Helpfulness of Hobbies”—“my friend
Bailey ’’--four columns. “ Our Wintry Woes ” —“ a gifted
and much-admired friend named Bailey”—three columns.
“ Love’s Young Dream”—“l have read,” two columns;
“my friend Bailey,” two columns. “The Business and
Burdens of a Boy”—“my old friend Bailey,” seven
columns ; J. K. Jerome, two and one-half columns. “A
Sultry Subject”—“my friend Bailey”—four columns.
“The Problem of the Pocket” —“my friend Bailey”—
two columns. Of ten other sketches, at least five are
more or less made up of newspaper scraps strung
together from various sources.
The most remarkable example of “ Boondi’s ” talent is
the sketch entitled “ Slaves : Past and Present.” Three
years ago Edwin Pugh, a clever English writer,
published in To-Day an unforgettable picture of
the life of an English labouring woman married
to a selfish husband. The Sunday Times (Sydney)
reprinted this with due acknowledgment—“ The
Woman at Home. By Edwin Pugh, in To-Day.”
‘ 1 Boondi ” has copied or paraphrased this sketch, with the
addition of an introduction and some Australian refer-
ences, and it now appears in Boondi’s Book without a
word of acknowledgment to Pugh. Some juxtaposed
passages will show how close is the travesty—
Edwin Pugh.

G. H. Ellwanger, author of The Pleasures of the
Table , an account of gastronomy, reminds us that
Sardanapalus, last of the Assyrian kings, offered a guerdon
of a thousand pieces of gold to him who would i induce a
new dish. “Eat, drink, amuse thyself: all else is
vanity,” was his maxim, and the precept he desired to
have ‘engraven on his tomb. It is recorded that Mark
Antony, more than usually pleased with his dinner, sent
for the cook and presented him with a city of 35,000 in-
habitants.
In England 400 or 500 years ago, people took four
meals—breakfast at seven, dinner at ten, supper at four,
and livery at eight.
Since then, from an early hour in the morning the
principal daily meal has advanced equally in France
and England through every hour from ten in the fore-
noon until ten at night. In France in the thirteenth
century nine in the morning was the dinner-hour.
Henry VII dined at eleven. In Cromwell’s time one
o’clock had come to be the fashionable hour, and in
Addison’s day two o’clock, which gradually became
adjourned until four. Pope found fault with Lady
Suffolk for dining so late as four, saying young people
might become inured to such things, but as for himself,
if she would adopt such unreasonable practices be must
absent himself from Marble Hill. Four and five con-
tinued to be the popular dining-hour among the better
classes until the second decade of the century, when
dinner was further postponed, from which period it has
steadily continued to encroach upon the evening.
The clergy have been always bulwarks of good living.
Ellwanger tells a story of a French wedding dinner at
which the officiating pastor was present. After every
course, he raised his glass and exclaimed, “My children,
with this you must drink some wine.” The turn of
dessert arriving, he repeated his injunction for the tenth
time, again setting the example himself. “ Pardon,
Monsieur le Cur 6,” one of the guests interrupted, “but
with what do you not drink wine?” “With water, my
son! ”
Of Bignon’s famous Paris restaurant these capital
legends are revived :
“Fifteen francs for a peach?" inquired Prince
Narischkin ; “they must be very scarce.” “It is n’t
the peaches that are scarce, mon prince ; it is the
Narisehkins.” “ Monsieur Bignon, a red herring at
two and a half francs ! It seems to me that is exces-
sive.” “ But these prices are marked in your interest,”
rejoined the restaurateur. “It is the barrier I have
established between my clients and the vulgar. Why
do you come here ? To be among yourselves, to avoid
embarrassing or compromising surroundings. If I
changed my prices the house would be invaded, and
you would all leave.”
Another patron who complained of a sauce was
asked, “ Did you dine here last evening?” “No,” he
replied. “ That is the trouble, then ; you spoiled your
taste in the other restaurant,.” Still another guest
objected to the charges on his bill, comparing it with
an identical breakfast of a few r days previous, which
amounted to eighteen and a half francs, whereas the
breakfast in question was charged twenty-one francs
eighteen centimes. “ I will investigate the mistake,”
said Bignon, who, with the two bills, proceeded to his
desk, returning shortly afterwards. “It is very true,
Monsieur, that a mistake was made in your favour last
Monday ; but I make no claim for restitution !"
The new “ library edition ” of Ruskin is to
include “about thirty-two” vols., each 21s. net. For-
tunately it will not be necessary to read them all.
BOOKFELLOW'S MEMORANDA
Whitewashing Julia, H. A. Jones’s new play at Lond.
Garrick, has been capitally advertised by Walkleying
Jones. Jones complained to his manager, Arthur Bour-
chier, that for four years A. B. Walkley, The Times
dramatic critic, had attacked him personally and had
criticised his work unfairly.
In recent years the notices in The Times upon me
and my work have passed the limits of criticism, and
have degenerated into personalities and hints that
scarcely stop on the safe side of libel.
When in place of criticising my play the writer sug-
gests that I am a “ viveur ” ; when he goes out of the
way to comment upon how and where I spend my time
and money ; when the original notice is followed by
numerous after-thrusts ; when these ingenious little
attacks are insinuated into notices of other plays ;
when they are repeated week after week in the Friday
literary supplement of The Times ; when this is con-
tinued over a space of years, I do not think we can be
charged with over-sensitiveness to criticism if we ask
the editor that on Monday The Times shall be repre-
sented by someone whose mind is in a less distracted
condition to judge of my work.
To give one instance : A few years ago I produced
The Manoeuvres of Jane, at the Haymarket. The
play, its story, its motives, its characterisation, are
doubtless fresh in the memory of playgoers. They
shall tell me whether I am wrong in describing it, as
what I intended it to be, a harmless piece of fun. This
little piece was described in a weekly journal by the
author of the recent Times notices as a “ com6die
rosse ” [Ang. coarse, suggestively improper, and worse.
—B.], the most offensive epithet that can be applied
to a play. I think we may Submit to English playgoers
that the writer who could describe The Manoeuvres
of Jarte as a rosse must have “a curious
mind,” and that he is not quite competent to taste for
them in these matters.
So Bourchier turned up a Times file, read and re-read
Walkley’s criticisms for four years, and agreed that Jones
had been the subject of “bear-baiting.” He wrote to
The Times; received no reply ; and when Walkley
arrived as usual on the first night of Whitewashing
Julia he was refused admission. The Times noted the
fact, and nothing more ; and that is the history to
March 14.
L. J. Brient, writing from London to Sydney
Telegraph , notes the new respect paid to Australian
political precedents by Englishmen. “ Speakers not only
impress their audiences, but carry conviction with th
declaration : ‘This is what they do in Australia Brieni
says both Barton and Wise were more effective speaker
in Knsrland-than he torn d them in Australia. Contrast
ing Rosebery with Wise, he says the latter commits »
fault common to most Australian after-dinner speaks™
of the higher order—he crowds too much matter into a
speech. “Lord Rosebery usually makes one point and
usually coins one phrase, in an after-dinner speech ’ The
point is remembered, and the phrase sticks.” Brienr’.
letters grow more interesting. But he must not write
“ very analogous to.”
Mark the continuous novel in Eng. Review of
Reviews, with a hint of wounded snake about it. It began in
January, but it is to end only when Pitt would have laid
down his arms if he had been an American. Current
topics are to be the warp of an eternal woof, unless Stead
tires of it. For our own part, we are tired of it ahead,.
We are also tired of
Bernard Shaw’s monotonous pose,
Dana Gibson’s monotonous drawings,
Andrew Ower-Lang,
Mrs. Humphry Ward.
And, since C. Wragge has a refuge on Mt. Kosciusko
we do wish he would crawl in, with a proper medicinal
provision of whisky and tobacco, and nail up the door
from the inside.
(Though Bernard Shaw was serious enough the other
day, when he told a London audience that he had lost
faith in nearly all his old ideals, including socialism and
democracy, and retained little more than a hope that
somehow good would be the final goal of ill. Moral for
reformers : Marry a rich wife, and retire from business.)
Dramatic-writer Augustus Moore (brother of
George) wrote the scenario of a musical piece based on
Charles O'if alley, and offered it to theatrical-manager
George Edwardes. Edwardes handed it to dramatic-
writer Tanner, who recommended refusal. Then
Edwardes produced The Country Girl, written by Tanner
and embodying (Moore alleged) ideas taken from Moore's
MS. Moore claimed damages for infringement of copy-
right. Case heard before Eng. Chief Justice and a jury
on March 2 ; verdict for plaintiff, iilOO.
Bulletin’s London correspondent adds a gloss: This
case was a great triumph for the comparatively poor
playwright over the theatrical autocrat, and there is
rejoicing in the ranks of the perennially-despoiled “ ori-
ginal and unknown ” authors. It is a glaring warning
to every Australian dramatist, novelist, and story-teller
not to submit brain-wares to Lond. market unless they'
or a trustworthy representative, be on the spot to safe-
guard their interests. Ideas are very gold and diamonds
here, and the law of copyright protects them poorly. There
are theatrical managers, publishers, editors, whose theatres
and publications shine with the filched gems of
fresh, bright intellects. They know a “good idea”
when tney see it, and they are skilled in the art of
lifting and resetting. These men grow wealthy and
prominent on “ submittals.” No man with a new rifle or
gold-saving invention would submit it to a capitalist
until patent protection had been obtained. The play-
wright or author, with no safeguard but the “honour”
(forgive us !) of the possible purchaser, lays before him
fully the cherished products of his brain and experience.
Sometimes he is robbed so cleverly and through dummies
that he never realises his injury ; and if he have enough
experience to realise the theft he has also sufficient to
know his impotence and the hopelessness of successfully
vindicating his claims. Most of the “brain-sneakers”
referred to have “ hacks” in their employ to whom stolen
ideas are tossed to be cunningly dressed-up for the
public’s digestion, while the MSS. are returned to the
authors with thanks —for the gift-ideas—and regrets—
that there were not more of them.
D. G. Rossetti buried poems and things: W. M.
digs them up. Rossetti Papers, 1862-70, is a fresh (?) collec-
tion to be published shortly. My own Rossetti glow has
not faded (though I found his pictures thin and weak at a
first glance, and disappointing—the work of a man who
knew what he wanted [and that always the fine thing]
but had not technical skill to attain it, lacked the sure,
strong touch, wavered and hesitated. Jacob had to
wrestle hard with the angel before he was blessed, and
“I fight my pictures,” says Verestschagin. Rossetti
could n’t hit hard enough : his painter’s muscle was
flabby. But, at the second glance, going half-way to
meet the emotion, the emotion came, and stayed. I
agree with R. that his poems are his best expression, as a
result, a thing accomplished ; and think his pictures hold
more potency of result.) but Brother Bill’s beer is get-
ting a little too far from the parent malt.
Lond. Times spent nearly £60 to have Kipling’s
last piece of verse cabled from Capetown; and the
advertisement waswocthit. “The Settler” is written
for the occasion of African peace, and Kipling, who
knows what is expected to be said, what ought to he
said, and how it ought to be said, says it as well as
Kipling can. An effective mask of strength and dignity,
and the author peering behind it “Well?” “The
Settler” is to turn swords into plough-shares, as per
formula. Some stanzas—

I have great pleasure in informing you that I carried out your instructions as near as possible from the beginning of January up to the beginning of
of this month, by wearing the belt when going to bed. After the first three weeks I wore it I began to feel better in myself, and was able to walk better,
and now lam pleased to say that the Belt has entirely cured me. Up to the time I called on you in Melbourne, the week before Christmas, I had been under
the doctor’s treatment for some time, and nearly physiced myself out of existence, so much so, that, between the physic and the want of sleep and rest, I
was almost run down, and had lost in flesh one stone six pounds. Immediately the Belt began to operate my appetite improved, and my strength also, and
now, thank God, through your advice and the Belt, lam quite well again. I weighed myself last week, and have again put on some of my lost flesh, to the
extent of over a stone.
When I was taken ill with the Sciatica I had just been returned to Parliament. I became so bad I could not walk, except under the most excruciating
pain, and had to hire a cab to take me to and from the House for over three weeks. I can honestly and fully recommend your Belt to anyone suffering from
this complaint.—l am, sirs, yours respectfully,
HAY KIRKWOOD, J.P., Member of Parliament for Eaglehawk.

Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Penitent.
I have done for ever with passion and wine*
Gold and glory and man’s applause ;
Ihe thrill in the marrow i 3 no more mine,
And mine no more is the hush, the pause
Of a heart spell-bound by a love divine.
I have wantoned right well in the joys of life,
And yielded myself without restraint
To the flowing bowl and the pleasures rife
In this age of ours; but, growing faint
And weak with years, I would quit the s rife.
I am grey and weary, and fain would tread
In ways far foreign to those I trod
When eyes invited and lips were red :
In youth to sin, in age to God—
That’s how the soul of man is wed.
I kneel to a white-faced Christ; I knelt
To red-lipped wantons a score of times:
He gives no sign that He hears ; they felt
My words make music, like golden chimes
That mingle with midnight stars and melt.
I eat dry crusts for my own soul's sake
Who fed and feasted on earth’s best fare ;
Drink pale, poor water, who used to slake
A kingly thirst in a vintage rare—
Red wine from the shores of some sunny lake.
The friendships fine and the fellowships—
Where are they now ? Gone, like the light
Of glowing youth. All gone the quips,
The jests, the songs ; and here to-night
I mumble a prayer with nerveless lips.
But grace is mine, and my faith is sure,
And the gates shall ope as I enter in ;
And I shall find (may it long endure)
A sweetness watching that first, fierce sin
When she who yielded with tears was pure.
N.S.W.
RODERIC QUINN.
Inspiration.
There’s a wind that sweeps through the Eay and
Night,
And like the lightning goes,
But none have heard the sound of its wings,
And none know whither it blows ;
But where’er it comes the thoughts of men
Are like clouds together hurled,
As they are carried with mystio speed
Over the crazy world.
We see no waving of leafy boughs,
Nor heave of the purple sea;
When this wind its fiercest blows, the Earth
May be still as the dead men be.
But the spirit feels its fiery breath,
And the souls of men are stirred,
As o’er the electric lines of Life
Is flashed the magic word.
The gale from the Spirit-land blows in,
And they who feel it glow
With an ecstasy and ardour like
The seers of long ago—
The vital and inspiring breath
With which ideas are sown,
Like visioned seeds, in the mystio soil
Where the spirit-flowers are grown !
N.S.W.
R. CRAWFORD.
The Queen Quest.
A wonderful, sweet and unwon bosom
In a glow where the sun shrinks and sta;g grow
dim,
With pale lips starved and a fainting blossom
Of love untended by lip and limb,
In lyric marches thro’ long dream-valleys
I sought; for far, in her blazing palace
A Queen bides, holds the cup, the chalice
For lips and blossom still kept a brim.
A Queen all gracious and gold and glowing,
Fairer than all men have deemed most fair !
I followed the harsh path and met in going
A pale-faced, tremulous wayfarer ;
He cried : “Your journeying once was mine;
Ere your feet be weary, forego the wine
For lip and blossom ! ” But still the shrine
Of my Queen blazed far, and the wine was there.
My blossom of love spake : “ Forget her towers :
Let me fall. As your hopes pass, let me pass.”
But came in my path in a place of flowers
A keen-lipped girl thro’ the still, green grass ;
And my soul flamed out to her scarlet lips,
And my blossom blazed to its petal-tips,
And the splendid Queen, in a far eclipse,
Paled, and melted, and no more was.
M.L.
M. CORMAC.
Nightfall.
The sun has set behind the western hill;
The sky glows crimson, and the shadows fall;
The twilight deepens o’er the sea, and all
Is softly still.
The day is passed ; and calmly comes the night.
The wind is hushed ; the billows surge no more ;
Save when they break along the sombre shore,
And glimmer white.
No echoes float across the rolling deep ;
The noise, the strife, the murmur die away ;
Hushed are the countless voices of the day—
The world’s asleep.
And now the night tide, rising, sweeps away
The last day’s footprints with a gentle hand ;
Scatters new shells and smooths the golden sand
For the new day.
So, when we reach the sunset of life’s day,
And from its cares and perturbations sleep,
The waters of forgetfulness shall sweep
Our tracks away.
M.r..

Wasted Vitality
Totally Incapacitated.
“A Cheerful Mind and a Vigorous Body.”
The Case of Mr. C. W. HACK.
(fob the “times.”)
With the view of obtaining some idea as
to his opinions on the general trend of events
in which he was once the principal party
concerned, Mr. Charles William Hack, of
23 Eveleigh-street, Redfern, courteously
granted an interview to our representative.
The Reporter : “If you will carry your
memory back to three years ago I would
like you to tell me exactly how you stood
from a physical standpoint.”
Mr. Hack : “If it will render a service
to anybody else, I will tell you with plea-
sure, although the subject recalls such a
wretched period of my existence that I
would like to forget it altogether. My
trade is a carpenter, and I am glad to say
that I could always get plenty of work ; but
three years back I was so thoroughly run
down that I could not do it. I had been
in an indifferent state of health for
some months previously, being generally
‘out of sorts,’ as people term it, and
I thought I had caught a chill and re-
quired a rest. On the Monday morning
I felt so fatigued that I dropped a note
to my employer, stating that I was not
well, and that I hoped to be able to go to
work at the commencement of the following
week, but when the time came do you think
I was able? Ho * I had less energy then
than ever. If 1 thought about my work
it distressed me, for I knew that my
vitality had wasted away to such an extent
that I was powerless to perform it. My
condition fluctuated a little, as some days I
fancied I was slightly better, and that I
would start next Monday, but when the
day came I could not get up steam. Occa-
sionally I did a little about the house,
and then I was so prostrated with ex-
haustion that I had to go to bed for three
or lour days. This went on for three
months, by which time you may be sure I
was very downhearted.”
Reporter : “ Were you away from work
all that time ? ”
Mr. Hack : “ Every day of it, and if
everything did not go straight I became
exceedingly irritable. The least tiling ex-
cited me, and as I could do nothing but
brood over the helplessness of my condi-
tion the unhappiness of my life can be
easily understood. I resorted to medicines,
but what good were they to me ? None at
all. First one kind and then another I tried,
till I gave them up in despair. I then
visited a hospital, but the medicines dis-
pensed to me from that institution were
of no more assistance than if I had never
seen them. Those disappointments made
me more low-spirited than ever, and at
last my mind was harassed by the awful
conviction that I would never .get strong
again. My body was drained of all its
vital force, and there were no means of
replenishing it, because the medicines did
not help me, my appetite had turned
traitor altogether Some days I fancied
1 could relish something nice, but when I
got it I could not eat. At other meals I
was more successful in taking a little food,
but when it was down then the-misery
began. My stomach filled with wind and
made me very uncomfortable, while the
feeling of heaviness in my chest was almost
unbearable. The shoulders were also the
seat of much trouble, as between them
there came a pain which was very dis-
agreeable ; and as for aches—well, those
across the small of my back were so acute
that they depressed me with their intensity.
Life wjs getting more bitter and more un-
desirable every day, as up to that time I
used to keep a diary and take great
interest in most matters, but now all happi-
ness was gone, and I, could not apply my
mind to anything. My head ached to dis-
traction, so no wonder I was suffering
from loss of memory. There was a dull,
misty feeling about my eyes as if they had
swollen, but, rightly or wrongly, I put that
down to having so many sleepless nights.
The coating on my tongue and the bitter
taste in my mouth were most disagreeable,
though perhaps not such a serious matter
as having my nervous system in such a
shattered condition. Noises set my nerves
shaking at once, while even the conversa-
tion of people was annoying, so I had no
desire to mix in company. My brain was
so unsettled that one minute I would make
up my mind to do a thing and the next
minute I could see no necessity for it ;
but, losing weight and strength so rapidly,
caused more anxiety than anything else,
especially as nothing could check my infir-
mities.'’
Reporter : “ But you are not an invalid
now ? ”
Mr. Hack : “Thank Heaven, I am not 1
I am also grateful to Clements Tonic, for it
was the only medicine which restored my
lost energy. I was anxious to get better,
and did not care what I tried, but I can
safely say that Clements Tonic did me an
immense amount of good from the start.
Its invigorating effects shed a brightness
over my gloomy surroundings, and gave me
hope when everything seemed lost. From the
first couple of bottles of Clements Tonic my
appetite improved greatly, and what pleased
me more still was to observe that the same
medicine was putting my digestive organs
in good working order. Before taking
Clements Tonic I remember one morning in
particular getting up as usual, and shortly
afterwards I fell senseless on the floor
through experiencing violent and over-
powering pains in the stomach ; but a few
weeks’ use of Clements Tonic drove all
pains and flatulence away, and about my
chest and shoulders the freedom from tor-
ture was equally marked. So distinctly
did Clements Tonic strengthen my worn-out
system that I felt its beneficial influence
with each dose, my nerves in the meantime
getting as steady and true as they had ever
been. To know that I was no longer a
slave to an incurable complaint was a fact
which Clements Tonic alone could set at
rest, for my headaches and backaches simply
drifted away, and in course of time there
was not an ache in any part of me.”
Reporter: “Were you sleeping better,
too ?”
Mr. Hack: “ That was one of the prin-
cipal features of Clements Tonic. It made
me sleep splendidly, and its toning effect on
my brain was so grand that I did not
dream at nights, and I have not since been
inconvenienced with a bad memory. The
flesh I had lost was regained, and nobody
could have been more thankful than I was
at again being able to go back to work with
the cheerful mind and vigorous body which
nothing but Clements Tonic could restore.”
“ Reporter: “ Can yourstatements appear
in the press ? ”
Mr. Hack: “You can publish them as
you please, because Clements Tonic made a
thoroughly healthy man of me, and to all
delicate people its true worth should ba
male known.”
STATUTORY DECLARATION.
I, Charles William Hack, of 2S Eveleigh-street,
Redfern, in the State of New South Wales, Common-
wealth of Australia, do solemnly and sincerely declare
that I have carefully read the annexed dooument,
consisting of four folios, and consecutively numbered
from one to four, and that it contains and is a true
and faithful account of my illness and cure by
Clements Tonic, and also contains my full permission
to publish in any way my statements which I
give voluntarily, without receiving any payment;
and I make this solemn declaration conscientiously
believing the same to be true, and by virtue of
the provisions of the Oaths Act, 1500.
Subscribed ind declared at Redfern, this twel
day of April, one thousand nine hundred and t\
before me,
T. D. P. HOLDEN, J.F

SATURDAY, APRIL 25, 1903.
“Empire Day.”
Premier Barton was gravely disappointing
in his reply to the foolish deputation which
asked him, the other day, to have an
“Empire Day” proclaimed as a public
holiday in Australia. The request might
have evoked from a politician, with no claims
to statesmanship but only to a little plain
common-sense, a very decided negative ;
from a man claiming to be a statesman it
should have drawn a scathing satire upon all
such flummery, especially when suggested
with so bad a motive. But Mr. Barton
did not conceal his approval of the proposal;
he was almost enthusiastic as to its merits.
True, he did not take up a firm attitude and
promise to establish the holiday. But then
Mr. Barton rarely does take up a firm atti-
tude nowadays on any matter which does not
affect his own personal comfort. Provided
that his political position is not threatened,
nr the meal hours unconscionably delayed,
Mr. Barton’s attitude is usually one of
placid serenity, and his disposition is to
be easy-going and non-committal. He is
content to allow his splendid abilities
to lie dormant; his magnificent capacity
for work to rust in disuse ; and his enthu-
siasm for Australia to be smothered by
the cozening flatteries and worthless gauds
of Downing-street. Australians might have
reasonably expected from him, on the oc-
casion of this silly request of silly people, a
flash of common sense, if not of Australian
nationalism. Mr. Barton, however, gave a
lazy acquiescence to the idea of an Empire
Day in Australia, and, if any reliance is to
be put upon his promises of industrious
effort, the absurd proposal will in time come
into effect.
• • * *
The setting-up of another public holiday in
Australia for any cause whatever is in itself
objectionable. There are already quite
enough holidays in Australia, possibly too
many in view of the serious financial
problems which will very shortly have to be
faced. The need is rather for a day of extra
hard work to clear up some of the long-
neglected tasks and to clear off some of the
loiTg neglected debts, than for another
excuse to take a day off to lean up against a
wall. And to suggest a holiday for the pur-
pose of instituting processions, and making
exposure of oratorical diseases, and dressing in
liveries, uniforms, star-spangled regalia, and
other trappings of slavishness and of vanity,
is from all points of view a most pestilent
idea. That instinct in man which urges him
to seek to become a K.C.M.G., and a
Mason, and wear a gaudy apron ; or to be a
Royal Dejected Cat, or a United Gladsome
Buffalo, and deck himself out in robes,
skirts, frills and petticoats ; that instinct
which suggests to him to walk in procession
with a great blaring noise fore, and a great
glaring banner aft ; which leads him to
consort with his fellow man in public
halls for the purpose of exchanging
meaningless signs, winks, passwords,
and inanities of flowery speech, is of all
instincts the most mentally degraded
and the nearest to the painted and be-
feathered mumbo-jumbo worshippers of the
Central African swamps. To deliberately
encourage such an instinct by setting up
another special holiday for its display and its
gratification would be to deliberately push
back the intellectual development and the
growth of reason in the people, and to decoy
them wilfully on a path of garish folly lead-
ing back to savagery. The desire for
feathers, red-ochre markings, tinsel decora-
tions, processions, and pow-wows marks the
aboriginal instinct in man, the elimination
of which is civilisation ; and it is that desire
which an “ Empire Day ” celebration would
chiefly serve.
• * • •
A further holiday of any sort being in the
present circumstances of Australia unneces-
sary and undesirable, and a holiday for the
display of feathers and flummery being under
any circumstances impolitic and mischievous,
Mr. Barton had ample opportunity to give
a vigorous refusal to the deputation which
asked for an “ Empire Day ” without involv-
ing himself in any suspicion of loyalty to
Australia and disloyalty to King Edward
and Mr. Chamberlain. He might have
founded his negative on economic grounds
alone. Or he might with gentle irony have
pointed out that when chests are narrow it
is well to try to develop them, not cover
them with sashes and bullion ornaments ;
that where men are weak in individuality,
they should seek to strengthen their minds
rather than form societies for leaning up
against one another; that national self-respect
and self-knowledge, and therefore national
progress, are only to be won by the progress
and the elevation of the essential man, not
by any wearing of gaudy trappings, walking
in gaudy processions, mouthing of gaudy
rhetoric, or any other setting up of a
standard of ornament in place of one of solid
worth. Mr. Barton’s hearers would not
have understood any of these parables, and
he might have gone home, after such a
polite refusal, and pondered over the ques-
tion of how much better and greater a man
he had been made by his “Sir” .and his
G.C.M.G.
• • * *
But had Mr. Barton been Australian
Premier in deed as well as in name, he
would not have dallied long with the lighter
questions raised by the deputation. He
would have stripped them off to come
down to the naked issue : Should Australia
celebrate “the Empire” and Imperialism
with any glad holiday 1 Should a free,
democratic-aspiring people rejoice in the idea
of becoming part of an Imperial system
built upon the ruins of freedom with the
bones of patriot victims ; make festival over
the subjection of India and Ireland and
Africa, and the future possible subjection of
Australia ? Imperialism, understandable
enough in the days of Charlemagne when
the murder of a people’s freedom could be
ascribed to the Divine inspiration of a ruler,
is in these days of rationalism, of democracy,
of political philosophy, an absurd anachron-
ism. It has been well defined as a system
in which nations, trespassing beyond the limits of facile
assimilation, transform the wholesome stimulative rivalry
of varied national types into the cut-throat struggle of
competing empires. ... A depraved choice of national
life, imposed by self-seehing interests, which appeal to
the lusts of quantitative acquisitiveness and of forceful
domination surviving in a nation from early centuries of
animal struggle for existence. Its adoption as a policy
implies a deliberate renunciation of that cultivation of
the higher inner qualities which, for a nation as for an
individual, constitute the ascendancy of reason over brute
impulse.
Its necessary doctrine is aggression ; its
necessary accompaniment militarism. It is
a combative ideal of national life. It main-
tains it to be the business of a nation not to
develop its own life and its own industries
and its own intellect, but to expand its terri-
tory, to take up the burden of governing
other peoples, to conquer and to enslave.
Democracy becomes impossible with Im-
perialism, because the nation which sets up
a military caste to destroy the liberties of
other peoples cannot reasonably suppose
that its OAvn freedom will be safe. Im-
perialism is, in short, utterly opposed to any
sane idea of Australianism. Our part in it
could be only that of a subject, or semi-
subject people, called upon to participate in
the wickedness and the loss, without any
real share in the gain.
* * • •
Mr. Barton, if he permits himself to
think on the subject, probably recognises all
this, and fidelity to his position as Australian
Premier should have made him speak in
some such fashion to point out the folly of
Australia indulging in any “Imperial”
enthusiasm. He might also have shown how
unnatural and absurd it is to expect that
Australia should aspire to give up a share of
its own government to far-away England ;
how specially dangerous modern Im-
perialism is, with its practical control by
predatory capitalists, men promoting wars
as they would promote companies for the
sake of money-making ; how English Im-
perialism is frankly and openly an aspiration
for absolute English ascendancy of the
Empire, the English King being King only.
in England, “Emperor” elsewhere; how
despicable and cruel are the means which
must inevitably be adopted by an aggressive
Empire. But Mr. Barton did none of these
things He expressed to the Empire Day
deputation his sympathy with Imperialism,
and promised that some day he would grant
them their holiday —their holiday to cele-
brate in a savage fashion a savage idea. How
thoroughly Mr. Barton is effacing the
record of his past services to Australia 1
The Great Absentee Mendicant.
The position of the Salvation Army —the great
absentee holy mendicant of Australasia -has just
been brought prominently before the public by a
speech from its Australian head, “Commissioner
M'Kie. This gentleman, at “a great Congress
meeting” held in a tent erected in Hyde Bark,
Sydney (the See Government, following its usual
cowardly plan of cringing to every and any
sectarian interest, allowed this trespass, on a
public park), made a suggestion of astounding im-
pudence :
I have lately gone over the drought-stricken portions
of New South Wales and Queensland, as far north as
Charters Towers, seeing nothing but barren country, with
dead or dying stock. It has grieved me more than I can
tell. There are more unemployed than is usual at this
time of year, of both men and women, and I fear there
will be 'a good deal of suffering in consequence in the
approaching winter. Of course, we shall continue as we
have done —seek to relieve it up to the limit of our capa-
city. If we are to do more, we must get more supplies
from somewhere. We already maintain nearly 60 institu-
tions, with a nightly accommodation for about 3000
persons. The secret of our ability to maintain them is
the practical knowledge, good sense, and ready devotion
of the officers and people who are in charge of these
works of mercy. They disregard self in their endeavors
to benefit others. If there were any large measure of
relief to be administered, the Army could undertake it
at a less cost, and with more satisfaction, probably, than
any other agency', as they have the necessary machinery
in operation.
» * * *
This hint that the Drought Relief Fund should
be entrusted for distribution to the Salvation
Army sweating organisation comes opportunely
with the publication of details as to the treatment
by the French law courts and Government of the
“Convent of the Good Shepherd, Nancy.”
The judgment of the Court of Appeal at Nancy in the
case of Mile. Lecoanet against the Bon Pasteur nuns was
delivered yesterday. The defendants are condemned to
10,000 francs damage and costs. The clauses read in
court censure the community- for the grave material and
moral prejudice caused by their malpractices under the
fraudulent guise of Christian charity. The judgment
will be a salutary' lesson to other so-called charitable
institutions amassing enormous capital by sweating
and half-starving the objects of their sham benevolence,
and Mgr. Turin az, the Bishop of Nancy, deserves the
highest praise for his courageous action, notwithstanding
the high influences at the command of the offending
sisters.
Subsequently the French Government sup-
pressed this sweating convent (the offence, it will
be recollected, was injuring the health of the
inmate, Lecoanet, by sweating). Not only was
it punished for its past offences, but the best
possible care was taken that those offences should
not be repeated. That indicates the method
which should be adopted in dealing with the
Salvation Army. As a sweating institution
it should be absolutely suppressed, allowing it to
remain, if it chooses, as a religious organisation,
but taking away its license as a public mendicant
and enforcing to the full all factories, shops and
lodging-houses legislation in regard to its institu-
tions.
• * • •
The Salvation Army is not the only religious
organisation which indulges in sweating in Aus-
tralia. But it is far and away the greatest
offender. Other bodies do a little sweating in the
holy name of charity as a side line; with the
Salvation Army it is the whole religion—the
“saving” of the hard-up derelict so that he
or she may be employed, for nothing a week
and poor food and a vermin-haunted bed, to
slave for Booth, the great absentee sweater and
mendicant. The “Army,” therefore, deserves
first consideration; and it would probably be
found that legislation for the suppression of
its sweating practices would also crush out any
weak little imitations of the Booths’ tactics
which Anglicans, Roman Catholics, Presbyterians
or other religious sects have ventured upon.
There is no form of religious sweating which the
Booth organisation doe 3 not undertake. It cap-
tures the weak, harmless, rabbit-brained enthusi-
asts of the community, gives them a gory uniform,
a yell and a big drum, and employs them working
16 hours a day at cadging, for a pittance which
means bare living expenses. It lassoos the
“fallen woman” and shuts her up for as long
as it can keep her, to /work long hours at the
wash-tub for nothing a week and a diet of scrap
food and epileptic prayer-meetings. It hunts up
the released prisoner, and gets a stretch of work
out of him for practically nothing but his food.
Most pathetic of all, it ensnares the poor derelict
“unemployed” —the weak-willed man who has
gone down through drink, or through want of any
hustling power —and grinds his bones for the
glory and the profit of Booth. All the while it
collects—collects from the State Treasury on the
ground that it is rescuing people from crime;
collects at the street corners on the ground that
it provides an entertainment of corybantic
religion; collects from misguided charitable
people on the plea of its relief work; collects
from people who despise and loathe its ways,
but give it broken victuals and old clothes and
stray coppers because it is such a sturdy, persistent
mendicant, and because its misguided rabbit-
brained, retreating-chin collectors are so patheti-
cally in earnest. All the while, too, it works in
competition with outside labor. From its fac-
tories, wood-yards, laundries, farms, comes a
stream of cheap goods to compete in the market
with the product of labor not enslaved to the
Army, and this stream, as it runs, wrecks many
an honest little private enterprise and forces the
workers therein among the unemployed—to pro-
vide fresh victims for the “rescue” work of the
Armv.
• * * •
To add to the economic damage, the Salvation
Army is, as regards the destination of its profits,
an absentee institution. The money ground
out of the unfortunate unemployed of Australia
goes to General Booth in London. The Army is
thus even worse in an economic sense than a local
sweater. The man or company that grinds doivn
the poor and scatters their dust locally may be on
the same low moral plane as this Succubus-like
institution, but as a matter of mere community
profit and loss he is preferable, for he at least
spends his ill-gotten gains in the country. But
the unhappy victims of Salvation Army sweating
—the pathetic officers who cadge so pertinaciously
in the bad cause, the “fallen” who get down to
the depth of a Salvation Army laundry, the un-
employed who are the victims of its methods, the
derelicts w-ho patronise its doss-houses and soup-
kitchens—cannot even hope to get back, some
day iu the round of distribution, au atom of the
profits they have made for the Army. The pro-
fits are mopped up by the absentee Booth ; from
him, as from Sheol, there is no redemption.’
* • • .
To suppress the Salvation Army as a religious
institution would not be exactly advisable. It j g
true that its methods are repulsively savage and
indecorous to the average mind ; but an essential
part of freedom of religious belief is freedom of
religious method. It is impossible to grant one
without the other, without permitting to the Roman
Catholic _his ornate and sensuous ritual which to
the Calvinist is idolatrous, to the Calvinist his ma-
terial-hell and predestination fulminations which
to most people are revoltingly savage and cruel
to the Salvation Army man Ins big drum and his
horn out of tune and his curious saltatory exercises
But whilst allowing to the Salvation Army fuli
freedom for its religious beliefs and observances
in the public interest its street processions and
street mendicancy should be stopped ; its factories
laundries, workshops, doss-houses and restaurants
should be rigorously inspected and rigidly
refused the right to work over long hours to
under-pay, to keep vermin-haunted premises or
to sell unfit food. Also, the export of money to
Booth should be stopped just as was the export
of money to the inventor of the “Spanish
swindle.” Nothing less can be demanded in
the interests of the public, nothing more is needed
to put an end to the scandals associated with
Salvation Armyism. As an employer, the Army
is a soulless sweater ; as a distributer of charity it
is a brutal and unfeeling martinet; as an economic
institution it is a cause rather than a cure of un-
employment ; as a capitalist it is an absentee.
Those facts sum up the case against the Booth
institution and provide the amplest reason for
putting an end to it in its business and mendicant
capacity.

The Bitter Cry of the Unborn.
The A.N.A. is a great Association, but seems to
be badly off for Prophets. Bruce Smith was in-
vited to deliver an address on Municipal Govern-
ment before the A.N.A. delegates in session
assembled. Here follows an extract from his
address:
The two chief principles that should be kept in mind
in t he art of government were the protection of the in-
dividual and the property he had earned or won. These
principles were often transgressed, especially the latter.
How would they operate in a municipality? Take the
case of a creche. Such an institution, established at the
expense of the whole of the taxpayers to take the care of
the babies of the improvident classes, was a violation of
the elementary principles of government. The provident
man was limited in his aspirations for a family, while the
improvident classes bred apace.
Prophets in these days are scarce—
Hear the Prophet of the Purse,
Gaze on him, O universe !
Buttoned, brushed, with shining shirt
Prim, pragmatical, and pert,
Bruce Smith —woodenly alert.
Full of shreds of phrases brought
From the Lumber Room of Thought-
Full of wisdom —full of Naught.
Mister Smith no problem awes ;
He would deal with the First Cause
By municipal by-laws.
And he says, without a sigh,
When he hears the children cry—
“ Let the little paupers lie !
“They have neither land nor lith.
They have feckless kin and kith,
Let them go,” says Mister Smith.
“We who are the Provident,
We who on the Earth were sent
To build homes and gather rent—
“ Why should we be taxed to keep
Kids whose genesis is cheap—
Worms upon a midden-heap?
“ We who are too Provident,
We who are with less content —
Gallically continent —
“ Shall we still be troubled with
Paupers without pelf or pith ?
See us smile,” said Mister Smith.
“ I would have the workers breed
Workers still to serve our need—
Just enough ; that is my creed.”
There is neither night nor morn
In the Land of the Unborn,
Where Bruce Smith is held in scorn.
There is neither sun nor moon,
Starry night, gold afternoon,
In that vast confused cocoon.
And the shapes unborn go by,
Crying with a bitter cry ;
“ Let us live, ye who must die ! ”
“ In the world wherein you dwell,
Somehow, someway, we can tell
Life with you goes very well.
“ Here we linger, here we halt;
You have all the wine and malt,
All the savor and the salt.
“ Many a golden Guinevere,
Many a yet unborn Shakspeare,
And a million Smiths are here.
“ All we want beneath the sky
Is to simply occupy
Bruce Smith’s houses—and then die.
Spectacle of tragic mirth,
Smith—of all the Smiths on earth—
Warder at the Gate of Birth !

Truly the borrowing disease has brought us to
such a servile pass that Australia has to go about
in its stockinged feet lest the noise of its boots
should create alarm in English financial circles.

A Humorom Harpy.
There is a certain humor in the exalted impu-
dence of that Somers Vine syndicate which
tried to work a “grab ” in New Guinea by back-
stair methods, was discovered in the nick of time
and shooed off the premises, and which has since
clamored at the front door and whined at the back
door of Australia for “compensation.” The
simple facts of the matter are that a little band of
concession-seekers wanted to snatch a large area
in New Guinea for practically nothing. New
Guinea at the time was governed in a curious sort
of way, partly as a Crown Colony, partly as
a dependency of the States of Queensland,
N.S.W. and Victoria, which paid the cost of its
administration. The agreement was that in
all minor matters of government, the New
Guinea administrator should either act for
himself or rely for advice upon the Govern-
ment of Queensland: important matters were
to be considered by the Governments of the
three States. The concession-seekers probably
recognised that they had not a shadow of a
chance of getting the three States to approve
of their proposed grab, and so carried on negotia-
tions with the New Guinea administrator; and
while some members of the Queensland Ministry
seem to have heard of the matter others had no
idea of what was happening on the back stairs.
As for the Governments of N.S.W. and Victoria
the matter wasn’t even breathed to them. The
Colonial Office in London, which was evidently in
full sympathy with the grab, approved of the con-
cession—which was just as illegal as it would be for
3omers Vine’s junior clerk to profess to grant
all Somers Vine’s property to Dan O’Connor
without Somers Vine’s knowledge—and cheerfully
ignored the fact that N.S.W. and Victoria hadn’t
been consulted. But when the people really re-
sponsible for the government of New Guinea heard
the facts, which they only did through a casual item
of news leaking through from New Guinea, such a
storm of protest was raised that the illegally-
obtained concession had to be cancelled.. Since
then the syndicate has claimed compensation, and
as recently as March of this year one of the syn-
dicators wrote :
If I could have my way in the matter—l admit I can-
not—l would call the syndicate together and wipe the
whole business out of existence, with a strong resolution
directing public attention to the perfidy of the Govern-
ments concerned. I doubt if the annals of British colonial
government disclose a more disgraceful repudiation of a
solemn agreement or a more shocking breach of good
faith by the then rulers of the responsible colonies.
Which fine, large, generous indignation recalls,
somehow, to The Bulletin that, when the claim
for compensation in this matter was first made, it
suggested to it the claim of the indignant burglar
for loss of time and depreciation of burgling plant
whilst vainly endeavoring to break into a safe.
And with the lapse of all these months it still
suggests that, and nothing more.
“A Well-Known Banker.”
Melbourne Age, in its rage at Victoria’s new
income-tax arrangements, lately sentout emissaries
to interview the fat and sluggish non-producers of
the land, and collect their depressed opinions on
the matter. And their opinions, it is needless to
say, were that any legislation that imposes any
burdens whatever on the stout gentleman who sits
non-productively in an office is utterly reprehen-
sible. There were two columns of opinions, but
the following is as good a sample as any :
“ People are thoroughly discouraged,” remarked a
well-known banker. “ There is no enterprise, no go, no
push ; they have no heart to hit out in any new under-
taking. This condition is partly produced by the bad
times, and the drought has had something to do with it,
but bad legislation and over-taxation are largely.respon-
sible for it. There is no doubt that money is being
withdrawn from Victoria in consequence of the trend of
legislation. Australia can absorb money like a sponge
that is to say, we have a lot of land awaiting develop-
ment, and any amount of money could be spent on im-
provements on it. If we drive money away by over-
taxation, we cannot develop our property and we cannot
employ labor. I think this country has too much legis-
lation. People are pouring into America, whereas here
the foreigner is discouraged. How can you expect a
country to go ahead which is losing its population just
because some people have taken up a fad that Australia
is to be for Australians, that no one else is to share in our
prosperity, although, unfortunately, there is not much
to share at the present time ? They welcome newcomers
in America, recognising that every man who is a worker
is a source of wealth, and that the more that come the
more the prosperity of the country is increased.”
It is unfortunately true that Australia (and ev£ry
other country) can absorb borrowed money like
a sponge.” Every country that is foolish enough
to get into debt without any regard for the future,
and which can borrow the money, can absorb it
when it comes, and the non-productive person in
the office makes commissions and percentages out
of it in transit. There is no doubt that \ ictona
alone could absorb £500,000,000 easily enough, and
have nothing but a burster! boom to show for it,
and be more spongy than ever when the business
was over. Any well-known banker could know
that much without an effort, seeing that eve U
Victorian bank save one broke down in 1893
through absorbing money like a sponge, and being
unable to repay it when it was wanted. And by
that smash the banking class dragged Australia s
credit in the gutter, and gave it a shock from
which it has never since recovered. Apart from
this, however, the Age’s well-known banker is a
fair sample of his narrow-minded, unscrupulous,
morally-diseased tribe. His theory that the
United States welcomes a wholesale influx of
population is a common enough lie among the
Tory section of the community, though as a matter
of fact, the U.S. Immigration Restriction laws
are far more severe than those of Australia.
If the Age’s banker doesn’t know that, then
the shareholders should seriously consider whether
it is safe to leave such a horribly ignorant person
in charge of their affairs; and if he does know
it and is simply a liar, then they should further
consider srbethe r^,, eh an individual can be trusted
with the cash. The really pathetic feature about
the Age’s banker, however, and about all the
other opinions it publishes from non-producers
who sit in offices, is the assumption that Australia
is quite helpless and done and dead-broke if the
influx of debt leaves off; that it can’t hope to have
any money or any vigor or any enterprise of its
own; that it not only can never hope to repay
what it owes, but that it must go down with a
pitiful slump if for a year or so it doesn’t accumu-
late new debts with quite the old-time velocity ;
and that the Australian can’t possibly contrive to
pay enough taxes to keep himself square, but must
double up like a wet rag if asked to pay a much
smaller amount per head (Government and local
taxation all counted) than the Englishman gives
with hardly a murmur. The lie about Australia’s
loss of population may be passed by as a small
matter; it did lose by emigration in various past
years—notably the years when a lot of ignorant
and incompetent bankers brought about the great
downfall—but at present it is gaining. Perhaps
if it had the 100 per cent, tariff and the rigid laws
for the State inspection of banks and the sup-
pression of ignorant and fraudulent bankers that
the United States has, it would gain a lot more,
just as the United States does. The immigration
lie doesn’t really matter much. Also the ghastly
cheek involved in the spectacle of the class whose
gross mismanagement brought about the great
downfall from which Australia has never recovered
presuming to offer advice to anybody, is only a
passing circumstance. But what the non-pro-
ducer in the city office should be hanged
for is his constant preaching of the doctrine
of debt, helplessness, incapacity and cowardice
—his wail about our alleged inability to
pay the taxes required to keep the public accounts
square ; our alleged inability to do anything with-
out borrowing the money from abroad ; our alleged
inability to exist without getting deeper in debt
every year to the person who lends us the “influx
of foreign capital.” As a public enemy the plague
doesn’t begin to compare with “well-known
bankers ” of the Age type.

Tasmania’s Unbuilt G.W. Railway.
Years and years ago Tasmania gave a concession
to Private Enterprise to build a Great Western
Railway on the land-grant principle. It was
never built, because the promoters didn’t try to
build it ; the only thing they did try, so far as
the public could see, was to sell their concession
for about £250,000 profit in London. The time
allowed for the commencement of the work
expired, and the concession was renewed more
than once. At one time, when it had run out and
the syndicate had no concession or legal rights or
claims whatsoever to sell, it nearly sold the thing
it hadn’t got. Premier Braddon cabled that the
thing had expired, but promised a renewal as soon
as Parliament met, but of course the sale fell
through, and Braddon was abused like a
pickpocket for honestly explaining the posi-
tion. Then there was some more renewal,
and still Private Enterprise did nothing.
The concession was varied, altered, patched
and amended ; and the syndicate demanded
that the Government should either lend it the
money or guarantee the interest on its borrowings ;
but the railway itself continued unbuilt. Now,
at long last, Tasmania feels tired. Also the con-
cession has expired again. -And the syndicate has
the ineffable cheek to cable out at this juncture
that it expects shortly to raise the necessary
funds in New York, blandly ignoring the fact
that all its legal rights are a thing of the past.
Also it talks of defending its position at law.
And if Tasmania, after granting all manner of
concessions and renewals, decides to stand by
the letter of the last agreement and be fooled no
longer, the Propsting Government will doubtless
be denounced in London as a thief, fraud, swind-
ler, bankrupt, perjurer, and repudiator—in fact,
all the tactics of the New Guinea land syndicate
sharks and the Maoriland Midland Railway
boodlers will start all over again. And if Tas-
mania meekly renews the concession it will,
probably, still get no railway, and still be
besieged by the wretched syndicate of lies,
failures, disappointments and broken promises,
demanding more concessions.

The Cheek of Bull.
Premier James talks back at the English co.’s
which sent a long petition to the VVestralian
Government showing how the State might be run
in the interests of the Boulder belt: —
In Westralia there exists a firm conviction that
nothing has caused such harm to this State as the method
adopted by its mining companies ; the exorbitant de-
mands of their promoters ; the excessive over-capitalisa-
tion ; the sacrifice of the mines to the exigencies of the
market; and the unlimited use of those companies as
mere pawns in the game of market-rigging.
But he need not have stopped at that. He might
have proceeded to say that in the past three
months the (mostly foreign) mining co.’s had torn
out of the soil of Westralia £2,150,752 worth of
gold, and had returned only £33,802 in duty-tax
to the State, and he might have put it to them
that that was an utterly foolish and inadequate
return to make. He might have added that up to
the end of March the Westralian public estate
was the poorer by £39,829,678 worth of gold
rooted out of it; yet Westralia, instead of having
any part of this put away for a cold and rainy
season, had, on the contrary, contracted public
debts amounting to £15,000,000, mostly for works
undertaken for the benefit of these foreign com-
pany-mongers. Also he might have put it to
Geo. P. Doolette, the chief representative of J.
Bull in this matter, as a man and an (alleged)
brother, whether he thought it a fair thing that a
State whose stores of wealth have been drawn upon
at the rate of £205 per head of its people, should on
top of that be saddled, mostly for the benefit of Bull
companies, with a debt which had grown from
£3o‘per head in 1891 to .£72 per head in 1902.
And the same G. P. Doolette might be asked
whether, in his private capacity, he would be
willing to give away five sovereigns in order
to get an unpaid bill for £l. Then he might
be led gently into figures and asked to reckon
it up and see if it wasn’t a disreputable fact
that, in the past three months, the companies’
tax came to under fourpence for every pound’s
worth of gold dug out of the State and carted
from it ; and finally he might be told with
emphasis that he ought to be ashamed of him-
self, and that things were going to be different in
the future.

The Maoriland Land Assessment
Principle.
One of a whole magazine of questions with which
Tasmanian candidates were bombarded at the
recent elections was—“ Are you in favor of the
Maoriland of self-assessment ? ” And they
all said they were—all, anyway, but one or two.
Probably a good many of them didn’t know what
the Maoriland system of self-assessment was, and
therefore said “ Yes ” on general principles. The
men who did not know usually proceeded to
point out that their belief in the idea proved
what thorough-paced Liberals they were. But
now, the excitement of the contest over, even
the Toriest of them needn’t be alarmed con-
cerning their avowal ; for the thing of which,
in their heat or their ignorance, they approved is
not half so terrible as it looks. Certainly it does
provide that the State may take over a property
at a slight advance upon the price at which the
owner has himself valued it for land-tax purposes ;
and in theory it is therefore supposed that no
owner will dare to undervalue his property.. But
in practice, the position is just the same as it used
to be under an easy-going State Minister of Cus-
toms ; if the under-valuation or other form of
tax-dodgery got through, well and good ; if it
didn’t—well, the gun wasn’t loaded, and
nobody was hurt. All that was necessary was
to call on the Minister, furnish an explanation
(which nobody believed), and pay the shortage. A
landowner may shamelessly undervalue his acres,
and not lose a minute’s sleep in consequence. The
gun can only be loaded by placing at the command
of the Treasurer a fund with which he can buy up
the under-valued property ; his right of purchase,
if he hasn’t a copper to purchase with, is a very
empty gun indeed. And as the average Treasurer
never has any money, and the landowner knows
it, the said landowner can smile pleasantly at the
Act and under-value just as usual. Once load the
gun, however, by building up a fund out of which
estates may be purchased, and then hold that fund
sacred to that purpose, and this self-assessment
will become a really serious thing. It will not be
fooled with any more, except at the very great
risk of a bad accident. Certainly this involves
the unpopular idea of saving up money, of earning
and collecting a little more than is spent; but if
a start were honestly made with the fund, it
might, on its own account, easily bring in enough
extra land-tax to keep itself going, merely by
putting the fear of death in the fraudulent land-
owner. And, anyhow, self-assessment or not, all
the States might do and are doing a lot more
foolish things than to set a fund like this going,
because sooner or later, and for one purpose or
another, all the States will pretty. certainly have
to buy back land which, in their madder days,
they so recklessly got rid of.
