Twenty-four Years of Borrowing’.
The two voices and the dead silence of the
N.S.W. Government grow perplexing. Treasurer
Waddell has stated at Cowra that hard retrench-
ment is essential. Premier See has refused to
state anything. And Works Minister Owe’
Sullivan has added to the confusion by stating
this, which looks like a flat denial of any need for
economy:
I have been charged with expending too much loan
money, but do those who make this charge know
what my predecessors have done in this direction ?
Are they aware that in 1879 no less a sum than £7,352,768
was voted by Parliament at the request of the Parkes-
Robertson Administration ? Are they aware that in 1881
the same Government prevailed on Parliament to pass a
loan bill for public works amounting to no less than
£8,102,000? Will they believe that in 1884 the Stuart
Ministry passed a loan bill for public works amounting to
the enormous sum of £14,388,303? Yet such is a fact, as
the records of my department show. In 1881 the Parkes
Administration passed a loan bill for no less than
£3,065,017, and in 1890 the loan bill for public works for
the same party was no less than £2,620,796.
No doubt, in those days there were men who believed
that the country was going to the dogs. But the country
did not go down. On the contrary, it flourished all the
more for the expenditure. Many new districts were
opened up, and many new sources of wealth were tapped
by means of the railways then constructed. So it will be
with my expenditure on public works. A few years hence
people will laugh at the timorous fears expressed about
it.
The Owe’Sullivan’s figures as regards the loan
expenditure of his predecessors are shamefully in-
correct, but, even if they were wholly true, it is a
disgrace to the allegedly intelligent electors of
N.S.W. that a man whose puerile intellect thinks
such an argument worth advancing should be in
Ministerial office. If the N.S.W. Government
borrowed a large sum in 1879 when the public
debt was £l9 per inhabitant, and nothing broke
as the result of its doing so, it doesn’t follow that
it can borrow a larger sum with equal safety now
when its debt is £53 per inhabitant, any more than
it follows that because a sober man can take a
tumbler of whisky therefore a man who has already
taken tumblers of whisky can take a tumbler of
whisky. And even if N.S.W. was somewhat
riotous in the old days when its unproductive
debt (the debt for which there were no revenue-
yielding assets to show) was about £1 for
every inhabitant, it doesn’t follow that it can riot
equally now when its unproductive debt is £ll
per inhabitant. Away back in the late Seventies
N.S.W. used to have a surplus pretty regularly
without collecting either land-tax or income-tax,
with practically no excise duties, with no stamp
duties, and no probate duties, and a very mild
revenue tariff. Twenty-four years of hard borrow-
ing have left N.S.W. in the condition that it has a
big deficiency regularly, after collecting land-tax,
income-tax, considerable excise duties and stamp
duties, also probate duties that swallow as much
as 10 per cent, of the whole estate if it is a large
one, and with a much larger revenue tariff than it
had in 1879. Therefore the things that N.S.W.
did in 1879 are no criterion of what it can
do in 1903. Though, for that matter, it
didn’t do the wild things in ’79 that Owe-
Sullivan suggests. In that year a very
affluent N.S.W., with little debt on hand, spent
loan money representing about £2 for every inha-
bitant. In the last financial year a very poor and
heavily-indebted N.S.W. spent £3 11s. per inha-
bitant. If N.S.W. in 1879 had put on the taxes
it levies now, it could have built all the public
works it hRS constructed since then without
borrowing anything. It borrowed instead, so it.
has had to put on the same taxes to pay the
interest. The whole record of the great borrowing
era since 1879 has been a record of putting on one
tax after another —stamp duties, probate duties*
beer duties, tobacco duties, income-tax, land-tax,
revenue customs duties —to cover the interest bill;
and with it all there has been increasing poverty,
increasing deficits, increasing failure. And the
only thing Owe’Sullivan can recommend is to
keep on borrowing.
♦
“ Ne’er do-well ” writes on the Queensland Local
Government Act:
I have had 15 years’ experience of Local Government in
Queensland in an official capacity, and it seems to me
that the Act is a good Act deliberately rendered of no
avail by the personnel of the Boards and Councils elected
under its provisions. North and South Brisbane, to start
with, show how utterly bad Local Government can be.
The sole ideas of the majority of men composing these
Councils, appear to be, to find comfortable billets for
their own relatives and hangers-on, and to keep down the
rates. Of the undertaking of important municipal works,
or the proper functions of municipal bodies, they have no
conception whatever. Electric trams and lighting are in
the hands of private enterprise, the very water supply of
the city is in the hands of a separate body.
That curse of Local Government —the election of
members for the sole purpose of keeping down the rates
—flourishes in the bush. I have heard a very slightly
educated, bumptious little Board member, state at a
Board meeting that he was on the Board for the sole
purpose of keeping the rates down, that he was sick of
paying rates, and that he would never consent to the im-
position of any but the minimum.
The sanitary conditions of the townships in divisions
presided over by such Boards is unspeakably horrible—
drinking water obtained from shallow wells in close
proximity to reeking and open cesspits ; backyards
sodden with drainage, littered with decayed vegetables,
fish, meat, fruit, &c., and streets swarming with diseased
aboriginals and more diseased dogs.
Few, if any Boards or Councils assist in any way the
local hospitals, School of Arts, technical colleges, or an} -
institutions for the physical or mental benefit of those
they represent—such matters are quite outside their pro-
vince. They exist, as I have said, solely for the purposes
of keeping the rates down and of providing billets for
needy favorites. No 1 Local Government is beautiful in
theory, but a dismal failure in practice—in Queensland,
anyway.
[Which goes to show that you can give people the
chance of being good by legislation without
making them good—a familiar enough fact. But
it is as well anyhow to give them the chance,
whether they take it or not. —Ed. B.]
“Kiwi,” from Maoriland :
The decision of the Privy Council in the Porirua Trust
case is interesting from many points of view ; but the
moral of it is that Australians have done the right thing
in virtually abolishing the appeal to “the highest
tribunal in the Empire,” and substituting therefor appeal
to their Federal High Court. [But they haven’t. They tried
to, but the British Parliament said “ No.”—Ed. B.J The
facts are briefly as follow : Some 50 years ago the Ngatitoa
tribe of Maoris at Porirua, through the Crown, ceded
certain of their lands to the Anglican church for the pur-
pose of founding a school for Maori children in that
district. To this day no school has been erected
in terms of the trust, and the Solicitor-General,
on behalf of the Crown, sued to have the land res-
tored. The church authorities contended that, as
it had become impossible, on account of the decrease of
the Maori population, to carry out the original trust, the
endowment should be diverted to the maintenance of a
Maori school at Wairarapa. The Court of Appeal unanim-
ously decided in favor of the Crown’s view—that the
trust had failed, and that the church proposal would be
at variance with the intentions of the native donors (for
the Ngatitoas and the VVairarapas have always been
enemies), and would be a fraud on the Maoris. The case
then went to the Privy Council, and that august body
has now decided in favor of the church authori-
ties. Of course, it is not unusual for the decisions
of our Court of Appeal to be reversed by the
Privy Council; but the unusual feature in the'judg-
ment in this case is that it is interspersed with scurrilous
attacks on the Government for “despoiling a charity,”
and on the Solicitor-General and Court of Appeal as being
the willing tools of the Government, Amongst a small
minority of antiquated fossils, whose hatred of Seddon
will carry them to any length, there is rejoicing at the
onslaught; but the effect on the great majoritv of Maori-
landers is to enkindle a feeling of deep and just resent-
ment that an outside judicial bodv should descend
to such an uncalled-for and untruthful attack on
the Executive and courts of this countrv. I cannot
help recalling, however, that, as far as SErnox is con-
cerned, there is not room for unmixed regret ) hen the
Australian delegates were wrestling with Chamberlain,
a couple of years ago, on the question whether the High
Court of Australia should replace the Privy Council,
“ our Dick” went out of his way to declare that the Privy
Council was one of the bulwarks of Imperial unitv, that
no other court inspired such confidence, &c., all of" which
was duly cabled ’ome per Reuter. Now, however, we
have his official press organs vehemently attacking this
precious bulwark of Imperial unity. Personally, lam not
sorry at the insults which the Privy Council has seen fit
to hurl at this country. The people’s virility has been so
completely sapped by crawlsome Jingoism that a few
insults are about the only thing to rekindle the slumber-
ing embers of national self-respect.
The State Premiers, assembled in conference,
have devised a new means of being cantankerous
towards the Australian Government. The situa-
tion is like this :
(1) The Australian Government is to pav the State
Governments for property taken over—customs houses,
post-offices, etc. It hasn’t done this yet, because the
work of valuing all those scattered buildings hasn’t been
completed.
(2) Taking N.S.W. as an example Premier See reckons
that the Australian Government owes that State
£4,000,000. In other words, that it should, about 18
months ago, have assumed £4,000,000 of N.S.W.’s debt in
payment for buildings taken over.
(3) In that case Premier See reckons that the Australian
Government owes N.S.W. the interest on that £4,000,000
for 18 months or so, and he wants it “ recouped.”
(4) And if he gets it “ recouped ” he won’t be a penny
better off. The Australian Government, instead of pay-
ing this £140,000 a year interest, and consequently
handing back to N.S.W. £140,000 a year less surplus, has
handed back to N.S.W. £140,000 a year more surplus and
left N.S. W. to pay the interest.
(6) So if the State Premiers are successful in their
claim for arrears of interest the Australian Government
must first stop enough out of the surplus returned to the
States to pay the arrears of interest, and then hand them
exactly this same sum as arrears of interest—in other
words, instead of handing the State Governments £1 aa
the ordinary surplus customs revenue it must hand them
the same £1 as interest on the value of properties taken
over. And the only satisfaction is that the State Premiers
have the joy of being small, mean and nasty.

A Correspondent from Walgett
Management of our artesian bores by the Works Dept,
is disgraceful. From time to time groups of settlers
have induced the Govt, to construct bores for them—a
very laudable work, and good flows have been obtained,
but not the slightest effort has been made to collect the
interest on the cost of construction. The Works Dept,
seems to be too tired or indifferent to draft the regula-
tions setting out the quantity of water each man is to
receive, &c. Yet the Artesian Wells Act was passed in
1897. What an enormous amount of revenue by way of
interest has already been lost, for I am informed that the
Crown has power only to collect from the time the
settlers were assessed ! With the exception of some
revenue from about a dozen bores which have been leased
to single individuals not a penny is being received.
♦
The “first sod’’has been turned in connection
with a second water-supply for Broken Hill and
its mines, for which, as usual, the N.S.W. Govern-
ment is expected to pay. There was a time when
the local mining co.’s were making two millions
profit a year; but even then anyone who sug-
gested that these co.’s ought to return in taxes
enough to provide themselves with a decent water-
supply was frowned upon as a disreputable and
unpatriotic person, who obviously wanted to
hound capital out of the country. And now,
when the mining co.’s don’t make two millions a
year—when, indeed, a good many of them can’t
make anything but calls—the necessary water-
supply has to be provided by cutting off half-
crowns from the wages of the 45-shillings-a-week
public servant. N.S.W. is a great country for
missing its opportunities.
The instinct of esprit de corps, which is as much a
part of human nature as patriotism or love of
freedom, is only what is saving Victoria’s Bext-
managed railways from utter collapse. Although
the men often profess to be indifferent as to how
things go, that spirit which is usually attributed
to the military is deeply rooted in their natures.
Individually, the drivers may feel that they are
quite justified in not caring a hang, but collectively
they don’t want it said that the loco, branch is
the cause of any trouble that may arise.
The traffic officials—guards, station-masters, &c.—
in turn, do their little best on a pinch to save their
branch from the responsibility of failure. The
permanent-way men keep their end up to the best
of their ability (in spite of five days a week), so
that the road may be as safe as possible with the
limited maintenance material and time allowed.
And the same right through the service. So, bad
as things are, the railway system still holds
together somehow. The old, rusted, obsolete loco-
motive, which is only fit for the scrap-heap, is tied
up with string, and shoved somehow or other over
the rotted permanent way and the decayed bridge

“ Dick Whittington ” and his attached Cat
are enjoying a fair measure of prosperity at
Sydney Royal, but this paper’s belief that it is a
rather worn sort of show, and that the old panto-
mime subjects are about played out, remains as
expressed last week. The fact that pantomime
has stayed so long is itself not a little remarkable.
There are a dozen or so well-trodden panto, sub-
jects still lingering above ground, “Cinderella”
and “ Dick Whittington ” being two of the most
enduring.- Year after year these venerable names
rise again 'in some new aspect, that is so painfully
like the old aspect, and with new gags hung on to
their hoary frames, and an entrenchment of
moderately new jokes built around them; and
they try to look as if the Whittington’s Cat
of this year wasn’t just one more of a centuries-long
drove of catsthathad drifted down the avenueof the
generations, and the sameness of the same old
dashed Cat defies all disguise. The struggle to
put new liquor in the old bottle ought to be aban-
doned. There must be a new bottle somewhere ;
and somewhere in the wide world there must be
some pantomime subjects besides “ Cinderella,”
“ Whittington,” and the other familiar literary
daddocks. For a little while, in the time of
“Matsa”and “Djin-Djin,” there was a praise-
worthy tendency to make a new departure, but it
didn’t last long. Now the cover is taken off the
dish at the dramatic banquet to reveal more Cat.
X X x X t
A second visit to “Are You a Mason 1 ”,
allowing of a closer study of the subordinate parts,
accentuates a first impression as to the general
merit of the oompany. Very good work in minor
parts is done by Lawrence Hanray, as a humorous
old buffer of a farmer, and Herbert Jarman, as a
Poverty-Pointer. A travesty of a down-at-the-
heels tragedian is one of the easiest things to do,
but Mr. Jarman has some specially good touches.
Also, Misses Kingston and Pearson call for some
kindly notice. The play has been received by
crowded houses during the week, and has ap-
parently settled down for a lengthy residence
beside the beautiful harbor which nearly drowned
Treasurer Turner the other day.
X X X X
“ Charley’s Aunt ’’ is bound for a long run
at Sydney Criterion—so at least it is affirmed by
almost everybody. Once get a liking for the old
lady in the impossible cap, and the taste is never
lost. Curiously enough, this comedy of the dis-
guised nobleman in skirts is a mild mania with
geople who see nothing much in “The Private
ecretary ” or other comedies almost on the
same level as that of the Brazilian lady. And
the same people, who sit tiredly while the Rev.
Robert Spalding pursues his goloshes and his bath
bun, are roused to frantic enthusiasm when Frank
Thornton merely mentions what a naughty girl
he was this morning, or smiles ingratiatingly at
his two admirers.
X X X X
The Sydney Lyceum drama still pleases,
in spite of the fact that the ocean waves, which con-
tinually heave beneath the distressed heroine, are
not nearly long enough, and their wooden ends can
be seen an inch or two from the wings. The real
hens in the farmyard, and the live cow which
Ethel Buckley pretends to milk in a gingerly and
half-hearted fashion, are to the fore as usual.
Ada Guildford makes her embarrassing bargain
with the earl in a serene and graceful fashion, so
that one forgets when she rushes that middle-aged
peer into matrimony that she is really the worst
sort of blackmailer. Later, when she flirts with the
Major, the melancholy nobleman informs his small
son in a vague way that the escutcheon of their
family has got its first blot. The drama begins
with a reoital of various sins that the earl
himself committed, but he does not seem to regard
them as blots. The African War scenes are
humorous, and can be recommended to anyone
wanting to know the inner history of the war.
This writer comes away with a vague impression
that no one was to blame for the trouble exoept a
Frenchman called La Renay (unless he is called
De Vere). Everybody sits on this Frenchman,
and it is understood that he is responsible for the
White Flag, and the surrender of Nicholson’s
Nek, and Kruger’s whiskers, and all the other
happenings.

Fitzgeralds’ circus maintains its record by

tinues to encounter the other King of Beasts
ev@ry evening, and to toss the deposed monarch
about his domain as usual. Mdlle. Oura does not
come up to Caicedo, the old-time wire walker,
whose claim to be a wire-walker was denied by
Borne, because he was hardly ever on the wire,
but leaping in the air some 4ft. above it; but she
j 8 a pleasant-enough feature of the performance,
anyway. The tired ton-weight horse that Pagel
carries np the ladder and all the various other
items, animal and human, continue their former
business.
X X X X
“ E.F." : The trouble with Pagel’s gawky
lion seems to be a lack of discipline in friendliness
with his keeper such as an ordinary performing
lion has been subjected to. When Mrs. Pagel
was rearing the infant animal “on the bottle”
her husband accustomed it to rule of force, but
took little or no pains to get it under moral con-
trol, and so the dazed child grew into a discon-
tented youth, dimly conscious of the fact that
Pagel could no longer toss it in the air with
such a masterful grip as of yore. Increasing
strength made the beast more insurrectionary as
time went on, whereas a properly trained per-
former would have become more philosophical and
docile. Pagel’s hobbledehoy is neither tame nOr
intelligent. Zapf, who trained the well-bred lion
“ Prince ” to ride on the elephant, is“of opinion,
or was, that Pagel’s lion is a fool. In Melbourne
last year Pagel said “ Pooh ! der lion haf no shance
mit me.” His views as to the relative chances of
a man and a lion in a catch-as-catch-can contest
have undergone a violent change. Wonderful
how these strong men underrate the strength and
ignore the superior agility of a lion when talking
fight. The most risky attempt at “ taming ” that
ever I saw or heard of was when Jack Graham
entered a cage containing the three strapping
young lions —“John Burns ” and his two sisters—
which Fitzgerald Bros, bought as cubs from the
Sydney Zoo. Graham’s first interview with those
sprightly growlers, took place at the Alexandra
Theatre, Melb., one morning. It was a private
test show. Nobody could say how the experi-
ment was likely to result. No man had gone into
the cage for eight months, and the last interview
between John Burns and his “ tamer ” —a Captain
Somebody—had ended in a scrimmage, the Captain
getting all the worst of it. Since then Burns and
his sisters had grown to nearly their full size.
Graham slipped into the cage, whilst the Fitz-
geralds and others stood around, armed with red-
hot irons, ready to singe the beasts off the man if
they attacked him. They didn’t attack him.
They merely jumped frantically. Graham stood
in his corner grasping a bludgeon. His instruc-
tions were to preserve a masterly inactivity, and
he preserved it. Soon he got the word to slip out
of the cage leaving Burns and Co. to sober down.
We greeted his exit with cheers, and then I
learned that Jack Graham had been comforting
himself in the cage with the belief that if he were
put to it he could successfully wrestle the lions
singly or all three at once.

Unless something disastrous and unex-
pected happened just as this issue was going to
press, Edward Lloyd gave his opening concert at
Sydney Town Hall on Tuesday, just too late for
the event to be noticed. The silvery tenor is
therefore a postponed item so far as Sydney is
concerned. All silvery tenors are hereby urged
to open on Saturday night, or Monday at the
latest, in order that they may be cordially ap-
proved of in this column on the ensuing Thursday.
The great desire of The Bulletin’s life is to give
all possible countenance to every good tenor who
comes along, and it hates to appear neglectful.

“ C.C.” reports a little Sydney side-show :
Some sections of various women’s political leagues
crowded into the School of Arts the other evening, and
sat about on the platform steps (when seats gave out) to
hear Dr. Mackellar give his views on the treatment of
State children generally, and to dilate upon his own
Infants’ Life Protection Act in particular. The Doctor
is not very keen about the boarding-out sys-
tem ; he thinks children should enjoy the benefits
of home life and family affection, and evidently believes
that by paying ss. a week they can get it. He also con-
siders that the sight of the State Reformatory boys
in their nice uniforms tends to annoy the respectable
mothers so that they want their children to steal some-
thing in order to be taken up and be as well treated.
But, according to his own argument, the Infant Life
Protection Act will annoy the respectable mother still
more, as by its rules the unmarried mother will have
nothing to do but look after baby for six months (the
Women’s Leagues want to make it nine), and the ordinary
married mother has to do this, and also accomplish a lot
of housework and dress four or five other children, and
do the cooking and washing as well. Still the
respectable woman’s habit of getting annoyed must not
stand in the way of progress. At the end of the
lecture a lady remarked that in iI.L. and S. Aus-
tralia the illegal Infants were less numerous than
elsewhere, and said it might be due to the Franchise.
Another person arose and said that President Roosevelt’s
remarks on the subject were very pertinent; but
Roosevelt makes almost as many remarks as Kaiser Bill,
and it is difficult to tell one remark from another at
times. The Infant Life, etc., is to decrease the number
of illegal births, so its supporters affirm, and with the
illegal births dropping off and the legal ones not in-
creasing, this county will be in a worse state than before.

At Melbourne Royal the steeplechase
drama is drawing general attention to the re-
appearance of Bland Holt and Co. “The Pro-
digal Daughter,” as previously remarked, is a
well-bred thing by Pettit and Harris, out of
Drury Lane —sound in wind and limb, and an
attractive goer when fully extended. It has brisk
action and a coherent tale, is mounted in a win-
ning style, and seems to be greatly fancied by the
public. Bland Holt, whose opinion is law in
these matters, considers “Prodigal Daughter”
to be about the finest horse-drama he has ever
trained, if not the absolutely fastest that ever
looked through a bridle.
• • • •
The company is a year older than it was
last Easter and 12 months better in some
instances. Bland and Mrs. Holt have not lost
any popularity. Walter Baker needs a little
fining down and bracing up as regards his figure :
otherwise, he is the same hearty, full-blooded
hero as of yore. Miss Harrie Ireland, who plays
the Prodigal, has improved her acting and put
some new frilling on her handsome appearance,
but she still drops her voice at the end
THE LATE MR. CHARLES BRIGHT .
Photo, by Kerry & Co., Sydney.
of a sentence, and makes some of her
remarks as though it didn’t matter whether the
audience heard them or not. Arthur Styan, as
the sporting gent, who administers beans to the
wrong horse, is a strong artist, apart from his
weak knees. Albert Norman is now slender and
satisfactory instead of plump and passable.
Brown, of the raucous voice, continues to rauc
cheerfully, whilst Kemp, Watson, Maxwell, and
others are slowly strengthening their claims to
forgiveness. In fact, they may expect a full
pardon presently for their past sins.. The case of
Miss Ross, the languishing heroine, will be
further considered when she gets another part
wherein she will not feel tempted to languish.

“ Ma Mie Rosette,” at Her Majesty’s,
seems to suffer from the comparisons which are
equally odious and inevitable, so the brief revival
of that warm, romantic opera will terminate on
Friday. Melbourne can excuse its Florence
Young for not being a Nellie Stewart, but the
city declines to overlook the shortcomings of
Harold Thorley, whose feet and legs were never
intended for Brownlow’s royal boots. Thorley
has no swagger, no attitudes, no decorative man-
nerisms such as made Brownlow a notable King of
Navarre. On Saturday (25th) “Paul Jones” will
appear in place of “Rosette,” and after Paul has
pirated for a week “ My Lady Molly ” will be
introduced to Australia by Manager Williamson.
* * * *
“Mistakes will Happen,” which should
have been christened “The Giddy Angel,” is
satisfying the demands of a frivolous generation
at Melbourne Bijou. Charles Arnold was not out
in his judgment when he struck this Yankee farce
for the Willoughby Co. It is somewhat original,
and sufficiently slick and humorous —as a rule.
The trouble doesn’t proceed from an illicit oyster
supper, or an overwhelming mother-in-law, or an
aunt who has come to live with the newly-married
couple until further orders. Two authors conspired
together in writing “What will Happen, ” and hav-
ing found one new joke between them they didn’t
work it for more than its full merit. The coach-
house and hay-loft scene, in which the joke comes
to a head, would bear a little more elaboration
than it gets at present. “Screaming ” situations
are ordinarily cut too long, but this would not
drag if it were cut a little longer. Geo. Willough-
by, Tom Cannam and Miss Watts-Phillips
provide the steam power to keep the comedy
moving. Miss Roxy Barton, who scored her
best effort as the serious lady ’tec in “Wrong
Mr. Wright” is sweet where she ought to be
stern, and hard where she ought to be
tender. Miss Bryer gives a genial character
sketch of a landlady, and Geo. Leopold, T.
Leonard, Edwin Lester and Miss Mai thy imlce
themselves useful. Miss Maltby, as the D. ch
servant-giil, has a good many lines to say, and
one of her lines is repeated too often.
• • * •
In a sentimental curtain-raiser, Miss Ethel
Appleton plays a sorrowful young woman with a
sadly-chalked neck, and does it rather sympathe-
tically.

There was only a small audience, in the
large Melbourne Town Hall on Easter Monday
night, when Superfluous-Governor Clarke and his
family belongings (including Melba) brought their
belated patronage to the second of the Edward
Lloyd concerts. The vice-regal scorn with which
they glanced at the prevailing emptiness was
emphasised by the tone of their comments as they
dropped into comfortable arm chairs placed im-
mediately in front of the platform. His Ex. mur-
mured “Disheartening!” His Ex.’s wife said
“ Too bad ” whilst the aideykongs backed up her
observation with snorts of approval. Melba said
nothing : but Miss Clarke gave loud tongue to her
feelings. “Disgraceful ! Such a house for such a
man as Lloyd! ” chirped his Ex.’s musical
daughter. The effect of all this upon the loyal
subjects seated (on hard cane chairs) near the
distinguished visitors was to make them feel
ashamed of their inability to expand into a great
multitude, suitable to the occasion. It is discom-
forting for people who do come to a concert to be
twitted, as it were, with the “disgracefulness” of
the absentees. And, after all, the explanation
of that poor attendance was quite simple.

“ A Midsummer Night’s Dream,” written
by W. Shakespeare, and embellished with scenerv
and effects of which he never dreamed at all is
likely to prove a good business spec, for Geow e
Musgrove. A black frost was freely predicted
for “The Dream” at Melbourne Princess’s but
on the second Saturday night of that risky’ven
ture the house was overcrowded. People don’t
come solely for the purpose of encouraging a
revival of the Bard, although popular reverence
for William is quite as sincere as Earl Tolloller’s
respect for Brains. They have heard “The
Dream ” spoken of as an interesting variety show
and are glad to find it not too full 0 f verse
Beginning with the arrival of a solemn
Athenian duke who resembles Wilson Barrett
the story proceeds to vary its character every 20
minutes or so, and the scene-painter, the pro-
perty-master and the limelight-man are working
at it all the while. In the foreground of a superb
woodland scene, Peter Quince, the convincing
old Athenian carpenter, kicks Snout, the tinker
and finally sits on his carcase to keep him quiet’
which is a brilliantly successful diversion. Poetry
merges into pantomime, and joy reigns around.
Bottom, the weaver, with or without his
ass’s head, has established hirffself a great
favorite. He is rather too extravagant except
in the second act, but the multitude loves him all
the more for that, and if he were to roll down the
steps of the Duke’s throne he would probably get
a further roar of applause. All the ladies actively
engaged in the poetical parts of the play have
confirmed the first night’s impression. Miss
Maud Milton as the Helena who is scorned by
her one boy at the outset, and chased by both
boys later on ; Miss Cynthia Brooke, as the other
spinster, and the fairy Queen and King, all of
them are well placed. They are a quartette of
fine artists in a series of beautiful stage pictures.
** X X
Rudinoff, the new high-toned turn at the
Opera House, is a versatile Polish gentleman in
knee-breeches. He makes pictures while you
wait, and one rugged view of New York Harbor
is performed on a plate which he smokes— i.e.,
soots —before the eyes of his audience, to
show there is no deception. Rudinoff doesn’t
strike everybody a 9 being a phenomenal
trick artist, but his pictures and incidental
patter are bright enough. Then he whistles
in bird fashion. He is a whistling humorist, and
he pipes a flirtation between two nightingales.
After which he sings in a respectable baritone
voice, and shadowgraphs considerably. Judging
from the length of his turn on Saturday (it lasted
40 minutes), Rudinoff has been in the habit of
entertaining a world that demands less ginger
and velocity for its money than is expected of a
“ star turn ” at Rickards’s.

Mr. Mephan Ferguson, of Melbourne, reports
that there has been a great improvement in the general
outlook of the engineering business since the new year
started, and that his pipe making works in Melbourne
and Perth are working full-handed. Amongst the orders
now being executed at Footscray works is one for 7 miles
10 chains of 18in. diameter light steel spiral-riveted pipes!
for the Victorian water supply; and at Perth works a
contract is in progress for over 6 miles of lOjin. diameter
spiral-riveted pipes, flanged joints, for the Greenbushes
Tin-Mining Syndicate. About two miles of these pipes
are to stand a test pressure of 8001 b. to the square inch.*

Assayers throughout Australia will be interested
to hear that Ernest de Beer of Salisbury Building’s
Melbourne, has taken unto himself a partner—Mr. H. B.
Silberberg. The arrival of the new partner, who brings
7 years Kalgoorlie experience into the business, means
that the firm of De Beer, Silberberg and Co., will be in a
position to stock all sorts of laboratory requisites and
assayers’ sundries. At the present time they can supply
from stock plumbago and other crucibles, plumbago
finely ground, microscopes and scientific instruments,
chemical apparata, including full lines of jena and other
glassware.. Royal Berlin porcelains, and so forth. In a
few months there will be nothing that De Beer, Silber-
berg and Co. cannot supply from stock. Test orders are
confidently invited. Special attention is called to
Morgan’s Battersea “ F ” shape plumbago, and salamander
crucibles, also extra special consideration is claimed for
the gasoline furnaces, which are rapidly growing in
popularity. Messrs De Beer, Silberberg and Co., are
direct agents for Schott and |Genossen’s compound, and
the famous “ Durax ” gauge glasses, which, by the way,
have lately been adopted by the Victorian Railways and
the Melbourne Tramway Co.*

The balance-sheet of the Great Southern Con-
sols G.M. Co. (Vic.) for the last half-year shows that the
underground electric haulage plant, which was supplied
and erected about seven months ago by the Austral Otis
Engineering Coy., South Melbourne, sole representatives
of Ganz and Co. Ltd., Budapest, Hungary, has effected a
saving of 60 per cent, in manual labor required to operate
the mine. This excellent result, and the great satisfac-
tion the plant has given, has influenced the directors of
the Berry Consols Extended Company in placing an order
for a §in»lar plant with the same firm. The plant will
consiscof a generating station, three underground loco-
motives, and a complete electric lighting installation.
This order furnishes further evidence of the high esteem
in which Ganz’s apparatus is held in Australia.*

The Wild Cat Column.
There is a pretty large howl in M.L. against
the State taking over the Bank of N.Z. Some of them
have the notion that Seddon will march along one morn-
ing, and pull down the old sign, and nail up a new one,
and will drive out the old manager and staff at the point
of a pickaxe, and compel the Commander-in-chief to sit
on the steps and see that they don’t go back again. The
fact, of course, is that the State can’t take over the
bank if it won’t be taken over. All that even
Richard Seddon can do is to point out in a
fatherly spirit that the bank owes the State £2,000,000,
that the bill falls due in a few months, and that the
State can’t continue to provide any concern with money
and make nothing out of the transaction ; and finally
Richard may ask what the bank is going to do about it.
If the bank can repay the £2,000,000, Richard will give
a receipt for it and go home ; but if the bank can’t do
without the State now any more than it could when first
it held out its begging paw to the Govt, nine or ten years
ago, then the less hifalutin there is about “ the sacred-
ness of Private Enterprise ’’ the better.
• • • •
Sydney Telegraph lately thr-w another in-
effective brick at the State Bank idea by explaining that
there is no private bank in Australia which stands so
much above all the rest in size that it could be taken over
and made a State Bank. Therefore, the Telegraph
reasoned, a State Bank is impossible. This is painfully
inconclusive. When Victoria and S.A. and Maoriland
started their State Land Banks they didn’t take over
anything at all—just opened alittle office and commenced
business, and they are all pretty large concerns now
Certainly it wouldn’t be advisable to start an ordinary
State Bank in that fashion, for there are already too
many banks, and to add one more would be unwise.
But the State doesn’t need to take over a large and
overwhelming institution, and buy out the existing
shareholders at a huge premium, as the nucleus of a State
Bank. Such a proceeding would be foolishness and ex-
travagance. The State should buy up, consolidate, and
bring back to life some inferior concerns—partly because
it can get them cheaply, partly because their absorption
would remove certain institutions which don’t improve
the credit of this country any too much; and partly because
any Treasurer worth the name would find a real pleasure
in dealing with such a problem. The National Bank
of the Commonwealth would make a very fair beginning
if it started by the acquisition and amalgamation of the
Queensland National, A.J.S., Australian Deposit and
Mortgage, and Land Mortgage Banks. With the
national credit behind it, such an amalgamation would
command as much confidence as would a State Bank
whose nucleus was the largest and soundest concern on
this continent; and with the State business to bring in
profits, and with only one management to pay instead of
four, the new bank could be made an excellent concern in
a very short time. There would be some satisfaction to
the country in seeing these monuments of the mis-
management of Private Enterprise removed from the list
of more or less embarrassed institutions—much more satis-
faction than would be experienced if the State took over,
say, the Bank of N.S.W., which is a nice-looking concern
and doesn’t require repair.

• • • *
Maoriland, so progressive in most respects,
issues no monthly or quarterly returns of revenue or
expenditure. Now and again, when Parliament is sitting,
a member asks the weight of the cash-box, and a few
days later the Treasurer answers to the nearest cwt. or
so ; or when Parliament isn’t in session, and the figures
are calculated to astonish the natives, a Minister takes
them on tour and gives a free show. Parliament not
being in session just now, the onty intimation of how the
State finished the year on March 31 comes from Gore,
where Premier Seddon made one of his numerous
speeches. The year’s revenue was put at £6,328,500,
which is £275,439 better than it was the year before.
Expenditure figures are not available. Revenue, pre-
sumably, includes the regular annual steal of the sinking
funds. Maoriland is pledged to provide a sinking fund
on some of its early loans, so the Ministry of the Infidel
and the Jew devised the idea of paying tiie sinking fund
out of revenue as usual, but putting enough loan money
into revenue to make up.

Since last year the Equitable has seen one of the errors of
its ways, or the error of one of its ways, and now calcu-
lates that it takes 4’86618 dollars to make £l, instead of
4'B dollars as it has calculated hitherto. A matter of
‘06618 of a dollar doesn’t seem to be of much consequence
so long as you keep to single dollars ; but when you get,
as the Equitable has got, to a thousand million dollars,
it amounts to as much as would make the O’Sullivan very
happy for another few months. The change doesn’t very
seriously affect the figures given above, though, of course,
it has reduced all the last line of figures as compared
with all preceding lines ; but it has made a difference of
£931,340 in the accumulated funds at the beginning of the
year. Therefore, instead of the £68,480,879, which the co.
wa3 alleged to possess when it issued its former balance-
sheet, it is now reckoned that itonly possessed £67,480,540.
Still, the new method of calculation reduces the
liabilities (when turned into English money) as well as
the assets. In the end it comes to this : that, instead of
the surplus being now £15,630,728 as it would have been
formerly reckoned, it is £15,418,151. Of course in reality
the society isn’t a ha’penny worse off than it was.
But what a concern, though, it is, even with the million
or so thrown out 1 Its funds at the end of 1902 were
£73,324,234, while the capital and reserves of all the
banks of Australasia amounted then to only £26,001,000.
The Equitable expense-rate for 1902, like most
other expense rates for that year, is rather higher than it
has been lately ;

There are, however, a good many higher than this, even
among those that don’t do anything like so large a pro-
portion of new business. In fact, this rate always must
be read in conjunction with the new business written, to
be of very much value. By a remarkable coincidence,
earnings per cent, on average funds were the same in
1902 and 1901, being £4 Is. lid. in each year. That is
without taking into account the item of “ gain
in valuation of assets.” In 1901, this gain amounted
to £441,246; in 1902 to £313,427. On account of this
falling-off, the earnings, counting in this valuation gain
with the interest, have been £4 10s. lOd. per cent,
against £4 15s. 6d. in 1901. But, notwithstanding that
there has been a decrease, the gain of £313,427 in 12
months is very fine : and it looks particularly fine in Aus-
tralia, where bonds and other securities haven’t yet
developed- the regular habit of getting more valuable with
age. If the Equitable would only abolish that very
papery asset, “ Deferred instalments on policies when
paid semi-annually or quarterly, £488,417,” there would
be practically nothing to find fault with in its accounts.

Official figures of the S.A. wheat harvest show
an average of 6 bushels per acre, for the acre reaped, but,
as 683,000 acres were sown and not found worth reaping,
the average comes down to 3'6—which is still a lot better
than N.S.W. and Victoria. Hay was, as last year, just on
a ton to the acre. Prices in both cases being double last
year’s, the farmers in the fairly-moist districts have had
a good time, but those of the Upper North have fre-
quently had no return at all. Total figures for S.A. :
1,746,842 acres under wheat, 6,354,912 bushels reaped.
Hay, 325,789 acres, 308,825 tons. Since 1891 S.A. has had
one wheat crop of nearly 8 bushels ; one just over 6 ; two
between 5 and 6 ; six between 4 and 5 ; one between 3
and 4 ; one of 2J bushels, and in one year (1897) the aver-
age was about It bushels.
• *
For having unwittingly catalogued some wool
that had been offered and withdrawn in another broker’s
catalogue, John Bridge and Co. were last month fined
£5O by the associated buyers and sellers, and were sub-
sequently boycotted by the wool buyers. In the words
of the firm’s manifesto, “no single buyer turned up at our
sale ” —though the fine was paid, and it was explained that
the wool had come to hand in the usual way of business
with nothing to show that it had been offered elsewhere.
Portion of the infringed rule read? : “No lot or portion
thereof shall be offered at auction more than twice. Once
a wool has been shown and catalogued by one broker and
unsold, such unsold wool, or any portion thereof, shall
not go to another broker for public or private sale.”
This agreement may be of considerable benefit to the
brokers, but where do the producers come in ? The latter,
after the wool has been offered and passed a second time,
have no alternative but to ship, no matter how the state
of the local market may change in the meantime. This
is a beautiful example of the roost being ruled by the
middleman.

The record of the Australasian Sheep Dip
Co. shows that the people who start to build up an
industry in Australia want all the help they can get in
an uncommonly hard battle. The figures available are
not complete, but they are conclusive on that point,
anyhow. The co. has half-yearly balances; but the
funds won’t run to printed sheets yet, and only the
statements for the June-Dee. periods are submitted :
t „„„ Added to Visible
Loss. Dividends. ReaerveSi Deficit.
Dec., 1899 £220 Nil. Nil. £484

Dec., 1900 £5B NiL £5B 919
Loss.
Dec., 1901 £ll5 Nil. Nil. 1465
Profit (less
amount
written off)
Dec., 1902 £563 Nil. £563 1453
The dividend column is put in on general principles and
by way of encouragement. The deficit is a very unstable
item up to now, because the co. has decided to gradually
extend its operations to other States and countries ;
and this has involved the purchase of more patent rights,
which has involved more brokerage and formation ex-
penses. (Brokerage and formation expenses are struck
out of the assets in the above statement as being no sort
of a tangible or saleable thing.) A start has, however,
been made by the co. to wipe off this paper item. When
it has disappeared, another will have to be tackled—the
far bigger one of “ Patent rights, £13,500." Patent rights
are a rather unsubstantial item, too, which, in any case,
ought to be written off with as much speed as possible,
because patents don’t live so very many years, and
because inconsiderate persons have the habit of thinking
out better ideas and patenting them. On the co.’s
capital (£15,488), of which £13,000 is apparently repre-
sented by patent rights, a profit of £774 will have to be
shown each half-year to pay a 10 per cent, dividend ; and
to do this, while at the same time providing for bad
debts (which seem to be heavy in this line of business)
and allowing a reasonable sum towards writing off the
paper item of brokerage and formation expenses, and a
further reasonable sum towards writing off the huge
patent rights item, is a pretty large order on present
appearances. Perhaps the preference shareholders would
simplify matters by writing something considerable off
their shares.
* * * •
There is a superstition that, once a Govern-
ment puts a duty on a thing, the price bounds up, and
the people who have to use the thing then have to live
on bread and grease and ride second-class, while the
people who make the thing fare sumptuously and ride
first. And there is a second superstition that, so long as
there isn’t any duty, the people get the thing at about
2d. per ton above the cost of making it in foreign places
by cheap foreign labour, the philanthropic importer
being quite content with the twopence. Yet, before
there was any Australian made sheep-dip in the market—
at least, any in a position to push business—the grazier
had to pay from 15 to 25 per cent, more than he is paying
for the same article to-day. Also, instead of the local
manufacturer waxing fat, he hasn’t had a dividend out
of the concern in five years, and doesn’t look very much
like a person who will get any to speak of for a few years
to come.
• * • •
N.S.W. Arbitration Court has been wrestling
with the tough problem whether extra payment ought to
be made to coal-miners for working with safety lamps.
The Newcastle men claim 3d. per ton, or 9d. per shift,
for using them. They claim this on the ground that the
light isn’t good for their eyes, wherefore they must put
by a trifle against the time when they will probably have
to buy a dog and two yards of string ; also that it isn’t s o
good a light to see with, wherefore they take longer
sorting out the coal.; and that they can’t carry a safety
lamp in their hats, as they do the garden variety of flare
light, wherefore they can’t use both hands freely, and so
can’t break as much coal; and for several other reasons,
among them that in Scotland, where it is alleged by
travellers that the natives don’t bang sixpences without
cause, they pay 6d. a shift as consideration money in gassy
mines. As this issue was going to press the owners were
busy assuring the Court that if they were miners they
would use safety lamps and no other, and they were using
so much eloquence over the matter that, the Court was
seriously thinking of discarding gas and the electric light
and having a safety-lamp installation throughout its
private premises. Eight or ten lawyers were interested
in impressing this view upon the Court; James Curley,
for so long miners’ general secretary at Newcastle, was,
single-handed, holding up the miners’ end, and holding it
up very well.
R.H.T.: B.H. Prop.’s divs. for 1902 were the
lowest in its history. Total, £96,000, or £72,000 less than
in 1901 Quadruples : B.H. Prop, shares, when there
were 16,000 of them in the co., were quoted at £4OB on
Feb. 28, 1888. On March 2,1889, when both new and old
issues were quoted, the former were £3O, the latter £3OO.
* * • *
Broken Hill: The quarterly count of mine-
workmen shows a total of 6625, or an increase of 511 in
the three months. Greatest number of men employed in
recent years was at the end of 1900, when there were
7010 on the pay-sheets. All these figures are exclusive of
about 2400 employed at reduction works on the seaboard.
The British, which has absorbed the greater number of
the increase, is doing very well; the stuff going to the
mill averages about 2300 tons a week, and runs to 18 per
cent, of lead, lloz. silver, and 17 per cent. zinc. The ore
is of a good milling character, and the developments
are satisfactory. There is talk of a more vigorous
policy with regard to South Blocks, to which
manager Mayne, of the South, will probably wholly
transfer his attention. But at present the mine has no
mill, and the co. has hardly any capital left, though it
has a great number of contributing shareholders, who
will presently feel the joy of paying calls. The Austra-
lian (German) Metal Co. has everything ready at its
works to begin the magnetic separation of the metals in
the tailiqgs. Success here means a very great deal
indeed. The plant will work on tailings, which are really
a great deal richer than the crop already taken from the
ore. Generally speaking, Broken Hill concentrates contain
about one-third in value of the metals in the crude ore ;
the tailings carry nearly the whole of the other two-
thirds. The new plant, working a modification of the
process previously in use, will make a zinc product and a
silver-lead product, and will ship both to Europe for final
treatment. The co. has a pile of about 80,000 tons of
purchased stuff to go on with. Meantime, what has
become of the lately much-boomed Delprat zinc process ?
• * • *
St. George, Lake Darlob (W.A.), maintains
phenomenal returns from a rich leader. Two months
ago 12870 z. of gold were won from 24 tons. This has
just been followed by 1087 oz. from 30 tons, making
23740 z. from 54 tons. Mine is located in the vicinity of
the richest alluvial rushes of the early days.
• * • *
Soutn Kalgurli (W.A.) is likely shortly to be
the scene of a new-broom sweeping, but who is to tackle
the job has not yet transpired. At recent meeting,
director Ellis Parker pronounced the treatment plan “ an
unqualified failure.” He has taken a long time to dis-
cover this. Capt. Isles’ recent London trip of four
months’ duration is credited with costing the co. a loss
of £lO,OOO ; £5500 in stores and £4500 in gold. As it is
admitted that the gold was a myth, the quantity won
being overstated by 13000 z., the £4500 loss is also a myth.
As to the loss in stores, that also is illusory. The stores
were used month by month during the three years co.
has been crushing, and their value should have been
added to the cost of treatment of the 70,000 tons treated.
Mine is not looking too well, and monthly returns are
being maintained with difficulty.
e •
Queen Margaret, Bulong (W.A.), it now tran-
spires, never recovered any pay ore below the 400 ft.
level. The long years of patient toil and the thousands
of feet of development work below that level were abso-
lutely fruitless and were paid for out of the proceeds of
gold won in the shallow ground. A few odd patches of
this still remain, and, on its being completely gutted out,
the mine will be abandoned. After the final clean up on
the Vosperton property, before the expiry of the option,
that show may likewise be abandoned, as the vendors are
not a happy family, and don’t look like agreeing to any
further extension of option which the co. demands. It
therefore looks as if co. would be wiped off the slate.
• * • •
Queen Margaret South (W.A.) appears to have
suffered from a combination of bad luck and bad manage-
ment. The tributors continue to tear out gold, and since
coming on the.“dab,” have banked something like
IOOOoz. for about a fortnight’s work. As a good deal of
ground still remains to be taken out, the tributors seem
on the highway to fortune. Co. gets 20 per cent, royalty
on all gold won from rich stone, but that is little consola-
tion for losing the other 80 per cent, it might also have
had if it had looked for it.
* * • •
ada H., Laverton (W.A.), has declared simul-
taneously a belated final div. of 3s. a share for last
year, and an interim div. of 2s. a share for current year.
This is a nice little surprise for the happy shareholders.
The vendor’s shares are still unsaleable on ’Change, but
the “Boulder King" has, it is alleged, for some time
been quietly picking them up at his own price from im-
pecunious or dissatisfied holders. The mine is only a
small affair, but as the issued capital of 54,000 shares is in
keeping with this, it may continue fairly substantial divs.
for some time to come. The gold chute does not appear
to be so long in depth as it gave promise of being on the
outcrop.
• • •
Cue G.M. and Exploration Co. (W.A.) owns
two leases in the Great Fingal neighborhood, and may
possibly be more heard of in future. It is a much recon-
structed concern, being successively known as the
Kinsella, Salisbury-Murchison, and Agamemnon. Under
its new title it was recently revivified with a working
capital of £25,000, the nominal capital being £lOO,OOO in
£1 shares. On the different properties held at various
times by the various aliases of the co., some £76,000
has been spent, or squandered, without tangible
result. The main vertical shaft of the present
concern is now taken down from 100 ft. to 200 ft.
An underlay prospecting shaft followed the reef to 118 ft.,
where water put an end to further sinking. A test
crushing taken out at this level for a width of 12ft.
yielded 4400 z. from 470 tons, by amalgamation. A pump
and 10-head battery are on order, and, as usual, good
results are anticipated. Mine is now in charge of Mr.
Hoskings, late of Lady Loch, and, as he has now a prac-
tically virgin property to operate on, instead of having to
try and straighten up a fearful and wonderful jumble of
holes left by his predecessors, he can demonstrate his
real abilities. Anyhow, after five years of abortive
mining on the Greal Fingal, at a total cost, including
£85,000 worth of gold won and £lB,OOO overdraft, of
£168,000, anything may happen. This Oue-Day Dawn
district is now receiving more attention, which it appears
to merit.
• • • *
Great Boulder (W.A.), by cutting the northern
continuation of the Ivanhoe east lode in a diamond drill
bore from Jansen’s shaft at a vertical depth of 1330 ft.,
has revived interest in this part of the field. The lode,
where intersected, is 10ft. wide and worth 27dwt. The
work of testing this lode has been going on in a desultory
manner for the past two or three years, but, though the
lode itself has been located at shallower depths, the
quality has hitherto been quite unpayable, con-
taining chiefly silver, and has given no encourage-
ment for the spending of money. All ore chutes
on the field have hitherto invariably dipped south,,
and this one apparently dipping north is a novel
experience. The lode has been located well inside
the pegs of the 16 acre block No. 102 E., and
within 300 to 400 ft. of Boulder No. l’s southern boundary.
Latter co. may, therefore, still come out on top with its
original property, although so unlucky in locating a new
venue elsewhere. Great Boulder co. is now sinking the
main shaft to 1800 ft., but, as reserves of ore are a long
way ahead of the milling plant, this work is not likely to
be unduly pushed, and may occupy the best part of a
year to complete.
Ivanhoe, Kalgoorlie (W. A.), has finally arranged
to test all its five lodes from the 1600 ft. level of the
Great Boulder by means of a diamond drill. The proving
of these lodes at such a depth, being practically double
that so far reached, should have a very good effect, both
on the Ivanhoe scrip, and on the market generally.
Mine continues to open out satisfactorily at all levels
reached, and scrip looks a very good investment stock
for a long while ahead.
• * * \ *
Golden Pole, Davyhurst (W.A.), will soon be
among the regular producers. The 10-head mill is now
nearly completed, but there may be some delay in
starting owing to the slowness in getting the poppet-head
gear erected. The mine is well opened up in three levels,
and enough stone is estimated to be in sight to keep mill
fully employed for two or three years. At the lowest
level the reef ranges up to 7ft., and is of high grade. Up
to date mine has produced 25640 z, from 970 tons, and the
estimated value of the tailings will bring up the average
to Zkoz. per ton.

The water pipes are being laid through Kal-
goorlie and Boulder, and the goldfields youngster will
very soon now have the joy of turning a water tap.
Already, too, the inevitable attack upon the price begins :
|he mines, which hare been paying VK per thousand
gallons, have got it down to 65., and that is reckoned to
be the lowest at which the scheme can ever pay. Yet
they want to pay less.

• • • •
So deadly is the dust in the deep workings of
the Bendigo mines that at Eaglehawk, which is occupied
mostly by miners’ homes and graves, investigations show
that 13-times more men than women die of lung disease.
How to reduce the frightful mortality, and the more
pathetic sickness that comes before death, is the com-
monest crib-time topic. Besides ventilation, an amend-
ment of the Mines Act compelling the use of the jet or
spray in conjunction with the machine-drills would
probably get nearest to meeting the case. That, anyway,
might be tried before, as a last resource, the wearing of
respirators is insisted upon.
During the Jan.-March quarter 31, Maoriland
West Coast dredges raked up lOOoz. or more of gold
apiece, and there were a score whose returns were
less; the total was 81690z.,0ran average of 1930 z. for
each machine at work. Best three months' work was
done by the Mosquito, 6410 z. Last year’s quarterly
average was 75000 z. For all Maoriland the past quarter’s
gold output was worth £450,206, which is £73,507 better
than for the first three months of 1902.

Even in M.L. and under Seddon’s strenuous
rule, self-assessment for land-tax purposes proves to be a
very small thing in the way of reform. The Levels
Estate, just purchased for closer settlement, has been
paying on a basis of a small fraction over £2 per acre ;
and yet the State had to give £6 9s. for it.

“H.”: The Melbourne movement for a
statue to Queen Victoria is not only dead — it is
buried, decomposed, forgotten; even the location
of its bones can’t be told with any certainty, and
its tombstone has been prigged and put up for a
hitching post against the public house of parties
unknown. It is fully a year now since the com-
mittee abandoned all serious pretence of trying to
get the necessary funds. And this is the second
of the three stages in the freezing of “loyalty.”
Said three stages are these : (1) The public ceases
to supply the tuppence ; (2) The committee ceases
to ask for the tuppence; (3) Both parties forget
that there was ever any tuppence.

Said that the whole bill (about £600) for
the defence of the six Frenchmen and women
found guilty (though five of them were afterwards
acquitted) of the Smith’s Mill murder (W.A.), is
being paid by Marie Fontain, who was one of the
six. Marie is the lady who is “going to live a
different sort of life for the future.” It will have
to be a pretty lucky sort of new life that will
enable her to find £6OO at a few days’ notice as the
old life did.

Pastor Rentoul at Melb. women suffrage
demonstration (Age report) :
Mr. Irvine at the last election was clothed in promises
which he declared would be fulfilled if he remained in
the House. He now appeared before Parliament and the
people garbed only in a singlet—(loud laughter)—a very
short singlet at that—and nothing else but his socks,
(Laughter.) And when anyone said to him, “ You have
not brought back much,” he said, “ No, but I have still
my honor.” (Laughter and cheers.) .
The rev. parson had evidently been studying a
recent Bulletin picture. But Alf. Vincent didn’t
draw any such thing as a short singlet; he gave
Irvine an eminently respectable shirt and collar,
It takes a parson to insist so vigorously on the
brevity of the solitary garment.

this as a memento.” And she had only had 24
years of life, too.

“C.N.”: The swimming damsels of
Brighton (Vic.) may have been too modest to have
any but a Blind Asylum band at their annual
shivoo, but the quality of modesty was not so
strained as to prevent their being extensively
photographed for reproduction in the weekly

considered quite the thing to have one’s portrait
taken with so little on that it may be a coat of
striped paint for ail the difference the casual spec-
tator can detect.
* • • •
Chief Justice Madden’s remarks in a re-
cent breach of promise case made many a
married man smile loudly. In that parti-
cular instance the lady had apparently told the
truth, and the bridegroom-that-wasn’t had claimed
her as his own in spite of the chilling fact that
she didn’t love him. “He knows the thing he is
bargaining for, and that is the thing he has to
take,” said Madden. Was ever such folly guised
in gravity uttered ? He doesn’t even know
whether its hips are pneumatic pads, or other-
wise ; and he doesn’t know how many pockets it
has ; nor whether its teeth are false, its hair dyed,
its chest padded. It might have a shapeless knee
or odd legs. And in spite of this, a fool-Solon
says gravely “ He knows the thing.”

Bendigo seems to be the first Australian
city to actually erect a statue of her late
gracious Victoria. Perhaps this is intended as
coals of fire on the head of the Heir Apparent,
who hadn’t time to visit that centre when he drove
through this country, though he managed to
inspect Ballarat and Geelong. Anyhow the
marble figure, sculped by James White, of
Sydney, was unveiled last week by the mayor (no
fancy Governor or Waxcellency) and proved to be
as pleasing a circumstance as the subject would
allow. A polished trachyte disc bears the inscrip-
tion, “Victoria, Queen of Earthly Queens, 1837-
1901,” which is distinctly modest and retiring. It
suggests the old Persian style of “Artaxerxes,
King of Kings.” Ever since Disraeli intruded his
Semitic nOse and his greasy forelock into British
a certain extra-Asiatic garishne§s of style
and whoop has been visible in the manners of the
once sober Anglo-Saxon.
The court procedure in the Auburn
(N.S.W.) murder case, resulting in the discharge
of the jury, was practically the same as that fol-
lowed bv Judge Burton, in Sydney, 60 years ago.
T ■ „ & j .’■ , -*? .
Gne James was under trial for the murder of his
wife, and the principal witness, a man named
Primrose, was found to be so drunk that he could
not speak. The Judge lectured the insensible
object severely in the witness-box and adjourned
the case for a couple of hours, directing a
medical man to treat Primrose in the mean-
time. When recalled to eoyrt, Primrose
was still incapable, and as tRe Judge had
another engagement, he discharged the jury,
When James was next put on trial, he was
found guilty chiefly on the evidence of the then
sober Primrose, and was sentenced to death. The
question of Burton’s power to, discharge the first
jury was fiercely debated, and an appeal was made
to the Full Court. The Full Court was unable to
decide, and referred the matter to Lord Campbell,
then Attorney-General of England. He also
avoided the point, but recommended that James,
owing to the time he had spent in gaol (over a
year) should be released, which was done.

• * • •
N.S. W. Liverpool Asylum came last week
before the notice of the public in the case of an un-
fortunate who, while slowly dying of cancer, strayed
away from the institution and died more suddenly
outside —he was defunct from exposure when
found. Official evidence at the inquest showed
that the boundary fence was not inmate-tight,
and that no one was to be blamed. Admission to
the State Tubercle Farm and Cancer Hospital is
free to all. Escape from it is limited to one
method—a deal box in the undertaker’s spring-
cart. So the ricketty fence is to be straightened
up, and the lives of the inmates prolonged until
the one lawful release comes. One thing: They
have not to wait long as a rule.

“J.F.D.”: I stayed once in a health
resort with three clerics of the clean-shaved
variety as fellow-boarders. To while away the
time I took the housemaid on small excursions
down the river. She informed me that the
laundress had never washed either a nightshirt or
pyjamas for either of the three clergymen during
their stay. They had been there five weeks.
The remarks the laundress made
regarding the things she did wash
for them could only be published in
the Watchman or Protestant
Standard.
Another of N.S.W.’s old semi-historical
homes will be brought to the hammer presently—
Vaucluse, the house where William Charles Went-
worth lived in the stormy days about the time
Constitutional Government wasgranted toN.S. W.
The estate was once the residence of Henry Brown
Hayes, an Irish baronet who came out in bond in
1801, having been transported for the abduction
of a wealthy Gork heiress named Pike. There is
a legend that Hayes imported many tons of Irish
bog earth which he entrenched round his cottage
in order to keep away the snakes. Then, when
Hayes went home, pardoned, the estate was sold
to Captain Piper, a naval officer, who in turn sold
to Wentworth.

* * • •
A column in a daily paper by Bishop
Stone-Wigg, of New Guinea, narrating his inter-
view with Edward VII., exposes that pillar of the
church as a person who feels awfully glad whenhe
sees a king. He says : “ There was no formality
no elaborate ceremonial, no stiff court etiquette •
nor any formal presentation or special costume’
nothing but the bare announcement of my name’
as I walked in. A pleasant, elderly gentleman at
once rose, greeted me with outstretched hand and
bade me sit down, putting me at once at my ease.”
Edward VII. should be careful or the kingly mana
will wane. Visitors of a certain sort should
always be allowed to enter on all fours and smite
their heads eight times in the ashes under the
grate. There was a time in the Dimmest of the
Dim Remote when both men and women took off
all their clothes before going in to see a king. The
taking off of the hat is said to be a survival of
this olden ceremonial. In those far dim days,
kings knew how to make the populace sit up, and
then, as now, “ there was no special costume.”

TWO SONGS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
'Tis a song of fair faces, of glancing bright eyes,
Of loves that we loved before we grew wise ;
"Lis the song that we sang in the morning of bliss,
’Tis a song of fair maidens—the song of a kiss.
’Tis the song that we sing at the close of the day,
When our eyes are grown dim and our hair turneth grey,
'Tis an echo from years that are vanished and fled ;
’Tis the song that we sing when we think of the dead.
J.A.C.
IN GEELONG.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Once, when I was free from Care
(Years ago) with time to spare,
I was staying in Geelong—
City famed in local song.
I had seen Corio Bay,
Seen the gaol, a building gay,
Seen all that there was to see,
Ending with the cemet’ry.
All the sights exhausted were,
And I still had time to spare ;
Therefore from a window I
Watched the funerals go by.
Two by two the horses dyed
Walked along in solemn pride ;
And in stately style and slow
Moved the caravans of woe.
And I saw, a sight not sweet—
Hearses, plumes, along the street
Nodding to a drowsy tune
In the garish afternoon.
To my host I turned and said :
“ This procession of the dead
Seems a comment quaint to me
On your Geelongevity.
“ You informed me, standing here
(We were drinking lager beer),
That the folk here, fools or sage,
Lived unto a green old age ?”
Then that Landlord of Geelong
Smiled like one whose case is strong
“ Sir, the Funerals you see
Are of Visitors,” said he.

“ R.A.K.” on the fashionable baby :
Babies are in fashion 1 So they are, but don’t see how
it’s going to make much difference in the population.
The poor little devils, after a few weeks of Mamma’s
particular attention, are longing to be back in Mary Ann’s
rough but safe clutches again. It’s not only the infant
in arms that’s the fad of the moment, although they are
very numerous, and it’s a sight to see baby and Mamma
draw up in a hansom at a swell photographer’s and the
tender little things nearly decapitated against
the top of the cab, as Mamma gets in and out
with her unaccustomed burden. But the particular
victim is the interesting child of from three to five, just
before it gets gawky, dressed up in some fantastic garb,
more picturesque than useful, with dainty shoes that are
too tight or else, if it’s icy weather, with bare legs and
sandals according to the whim of the moment. I have
seen a kid on frosty days with sandals, with little chil-
blained toes tied up with bits of white rag, and clothes
not starting till nearly half way up the thigh. But the
upper half of her was very smart to make up for it—
a few inches of rich velvet and fur showed
well against the blue, goose-fleshed offspring. So
mamma, having attired the kid according to the rage,
hooks him or her on her arm along with her parcel, hand-
bag, library book, and a thousand jingling things, and
drags him along to do the Block and a few bazaars and
garden fetes. That takes some time, with a few minutes’
chat to each acquaintance met, and the kiddie hops about
wearily on his tired toes until mamma goes into
somewhere for afternoon tea. He then has some tea
and some indigestible dainties. He goes on to a few “At
Homes, more tea, more indigestible dainties, comes
home, dresses for dinner and takes his place by mamma’s
side, and has more indigestible things. Then he goes
with his fond parents to the theatre, where he is given a
box of lollies to keep him quiet, and eventually he goes
to bed for a few hours, and the next day starts de novo.
Unless the fashion changes quickly around to poodles or
monkeys again, the kid of to-day will be shattered in
health, and blasA before he’s six.
• • * •
Two able seamen have been given 12
months in Darlinghurst Gaol for deserting from
men-of-war on the Australian station. One de-
serted in Sydney, the other in Fremantle. Both
were discovered in Melbourne, and brought on to
Sydney, where they were tried by court-martial.
Why should Darlinghurst be burthened with both
of these men—or, indeed, with either? Even the
form of their trial is independent of local civil
law.
* * * *
Let the Philp push, the Brisbane Chamber
of Commerce, and the calico-Jimmy brotherhood
generally, for ever hold their peace anent Kings-
ton’s “harsh” Customs administration. Quite
recently in Brisbane a struggling, poverty-stricken,
widow was fined for not sending her child to
school on the requisite number of days during the
quarter—the shortage, save the mark! was only
half a day. The widow appeared in court with a
baby in her arms, and gave as the reason of her
other child’s school-absence that she had to keep
the one at home to mind the other when she was
out charing during the day. Yet that section of
the community which allowed its indignation at
Kingston, in the Reid case, to boil over and run
down its starched shirt-front on to the footpath
didn’t even notice the real harshness of the Law’s
administration in another direction, when it was
only a lone, worried, husbandless female who was
the victim.
An im-
moral com-
munication
from a place
better left
unspecified:
It was at a
meeting ol a
Women’s Poli-
tical Organisa-
tion that the
declining
birth-rate was
being seriously
considered.
One after an-
other the
speakers arose
and suggested
various reme-
dies. Some
thought a
small bonus on
kids would
bring about
some improve-
men t. An-
other lady
suggested that
the Premier
should borrow
half a million
from the
Savings Bank
and pay the
accouchement
expenses of
people with
less than £156
per annum.
Another
woman, who
evidently
knew some-
thing, sug-
gested that a
day of humilia-
ti o n and
prayer should
be proclaimed.
When appa-
rently every-
o n e had
finished and
the chair-lady
was about to summarise, a plump little woman, with rosy
cheeks and twinkling eyes, arose and said, “I beg your
pardon ! ” The chair-lady subsided with graceful dignity
and a pleasant smile, and the lady with the twinkling
eyes commenced. After she had got fairly going each
particular thin, grey hair started to rise on her listeneis’
heads, and some of them stopped their ears. The plump
little lady declared it was all owing to want of oppor-
tunity'. She affirmed her belief that there were thousands
of men and women who were prepared to populate the
country if they were given the facilities and opportunity,
and said she ranked herself among the number. Her
scheme was simplicity itself. She advocated that Govern-
ment should set aside portions of the Crown land in each
district as State baby-farms. In all cities pleasant
parks should be provided, with plenty of accommodation
therein for wet evenings, and made attractive to lovers,
with plenty of cross hedges of the sort one sees in pic-
tures of what are known as Italian gardens. Any young
woman finding herself about to become a natioft«l bene-
factor to be entitled to comfortable living at the State
baby-farm till the benefaction was complete, when she
could be sent back to her occupation or her friends with
a small gratuity, say £5, in her pocket. The children to
be brought up by the State, and the parents allowed, if
they wished, to contribute to their support, and call and
see them as often as they wished between certain hours
of the day. The lady was so carried away with her idea
that she did not realise for some time that her audience
had melted away, and the sole remaining listener was a
small, badly-dressed man with ferrety eyes and sandy
hair, who was writing shorthand rapidly, and who leered
at her in a disgraceful manner when she suddenly closed
her address.

In odd moments Maoriland is wondering
whether it didn’t nearly hang the wrong man a
few years back. It was over what, 15 years ago,
everybody talked of as the Kaiwarra mystery.
A farmer named Hawkins was murdered ; and
the police reckoned that a man named Chemis
was more likely to have done it than anybody else
they could think of, so they arrested Chemis and
started to build the usual tomb of circumstances.
The most conclusive brick they got hold of was a
fragment of a Wellington newspaper of the same
date as part of a newspaper which was found in
Chemis’s house ; and this fragment had been fired
out of the gun which killed Hawkins. Apparently
the jury’s idea was that the Wellington newspaper
had too small a circulation to allow of this
piece of evidence to be dismissed, so they found
Chemis guilty, and he was sentenced to hang.
The Wellington paper which had been used for
the gun-wad felt, however, that the verdict
amounted to a reflection on its circulation, and a
row was made, and Chemis wasn’t hanged. In
fact, after a few years he was released from gaol;
and then he made the mistake of trying to prove
his innocence. Of course he couldn’t—less so than
years before—and when people finally wouldn’t
listen to him any more he went out to a lonely
place and blew off his head. But a man has now
made a statement to the effect that it wasn’t
Chemis who murdered Hawkins at all, but two
respectable and hitherto unsuspected persons,
who 15 years ago used to live in the same neighbor-
hood. And Maoriland isn’t sure, with Chemis
dead and Hawkins dead, and a great many other
people dead besides, whether the whole thing is
worth bothering about.

An up-to-date idyll, as narrated by Ben-
digo (Vic.) Independent :
The crowd at the railway station just before the arrival
of the express train last evening was treated to a chapter
in a kind of life romance. The hero and heroine were
both middle-aged. They astonished the crowd by walk-
ing into the vestibule locked in each other’s arms. When
near the bottom of the steps leading to the foot-bridge
the couple stopped, and the wooing was such as to indi-
cate that lost time was being rapidly made up. After
these caresses the heroine tenderly lifted off the hat of
her companion, and, sorrowfully viewing the ravages of
time on his once shiny locks, tearfully asked a question
which was replied to by the man throwing his head on
her shoulder. This continued for several minutes, when
the couple walked along the platform still locked in each
other’s arms. Opposite the stationmaster’s office they
stopped, and the woman began to cry bitterly, despite
the caresses which she was receiving from her lover. All
at once the man released his hold, and walking over to
some spectators asked “ Could you tell me what time the
next Melbourne train goes? I’m not going myself," he
explained, “ but that woman over there is. (She was now
weeping sorrowfully, leaning her head against a truck.)
On learning that the last train for Melbourne had departed
nearly an hour before, the man was surprised, and said,
“ That’s a bit of hard luck.”
“ You seem a bit friendly there,” remarked a bystander.
“ Yes ; bh, yes,” returned the man, “ she thinks a lot of
me. We are old schoolmates, but we had a bit of a tiff
many years ago. I have not seen her since until to-/
night, and we are squaring matters. I knew I would do
it; in fact I was determined to do so even if it cost me a
few quid 1 ”
The recklessness of the Australian lover—deter-
mined that even a few quid should not stand in
the way of his fierce affection !
Two advertisements from the same issue
of a Sydney daily :
TTNEMPLOYED.—Meeting at People’s Hall, Sussex-
st., 2.30. Influential gentlemen will address
you. 0. Von Hagen, chairman.
TTNEMPLOYED Meet 3 p.m. Queen’s Statue.
Definite Recommendations to be Considered.
Roll Up. J. Dwyer, Convener.
Unhappy unemployed! “Influential gentlemen”
the bait on one hook, “definiterecommendations”
on the other, and neither likely to be of the
slightest avail. If they had any business-sense,
the unemployed would charge “influential gentle-
men ” and others wishing to address them £2 an
hour, with a discount for orators wishing to under-
take a three-hours’ speech. At present, the work-
less have their woes much added to by being made
the practice-target of every ambitious Parlia-
mentary aspirant and every crank in want of an
audience. Were they to charge (say £2 per hour
per 100 audience) for listening, it would often
bring in tucker money.
