The “investiture” of various persons
with K.C.M.G., C.M.G., and other orders, at
Sydney, last week, was hardly up to the dignity
of comic opera; it was broad farce. Messrs.
Barton, Forrest, Lyne and George Reid in “court
dress” (which was one of the features of the show)
constituted, indeed, a matter of such grotesque
humor as could only be appropriately “set” in
the sawdust ring of a circus. These gentlemen,
with their Advance Australia figures and their
bedizened clothes, ambled around with im-
pressive mock dignity, and, to the fanfare of
trumpets, some of them acted as “sponsors”
for John See and other politicians who were
formally branded K.C.M.G. And everybody
looked proud and pleased. There was not,
apparently, in any mind a shadow of the disturb-
ing thought that this K.C.M.G. business, with
which the British Government “honors” Australian
politicians, is never tried on front-rank Britishers.
Gladstone was never a K.C.M.G. ; nor will John
Morley, Arthur Balfour, James Bryce, Herbert
Asquith, or Joseph Chamberlain ever submit
themselves to the cheap affront of knighthood.
Such rubbish is kept for “ colonial ” politicians,
savage chiefs, English suburban mayors, and
other folk whose low degree of intelligence, it is
thought, will lead them to accept it as an “honor.”

“ Honi Soit ” asks :
Can any right-minded young person, or even any
crooked-minded old one, explain what there is so verv
shocking about a lady’s neat and well-filled stocking?
The writer never yet saw one of them that he was afraid
to face, but it is evident that there is something about
them which calls for what the holy Herald would term
“ grave censure.” For example, a slander case cropped
up in a Sydney court recently, one lac}y having been
accused of stealing the fair and seemly foot coverings of
another. That wasn’t much to worry over; but I couldn’t
help noticing the cautious manner in which the “ respect-
able portion of the Press ” reported the edge. A modest
morning rag coyly alluded to the stockings as “a portion
of a lady’s clothing.” Another morning “ disseminator of
refined educational matter ” ( vide report on carriage per
rail of daily newspapers) spoke superciliously of the
stocking as “an article of female attire.” A shocked
evening paper vaguely referred to “ somethingshe wore.”
Evidently the Ladies Evangelical Union has not lived in
vain, even though it may have lived in vanity.

“ L.M.D/’ : Travelled on the Natimuk to
Goroke line (coekspur from Horsham, Victoria),
the other day. A duststorm was blowing at the
time. Myself and a Chinaman were sole passen-
gers booked right through. Train, consisting of
engine, passenger car and van, bumped along till
a woman waving a red flag called a halt. She got
her parcel (121 b. sugar) out of the van, and we
bumped away some more. Then a Chow, with
dust and humility in his eye, loomed m sight,
waving a red flag. He put on two cases of apples
for Goroke. We stopped some more at East
Natimuk, then at Natimuk, Mitie Lake, St.
Mary’s, and then we ran into the scrub near
Spring Hill. In the middle of the scrub she
stopped. The guard got out and went up to the
engine. I inquired the reason of stoppage as he
passed back. “Broke down,” he said, laconi-
cally, and walked away down the line. I went to
the driver, who told me the telegraph station was
four miles away, and the guard had gone to get
another engine. He was letting down the fires.
So the Chow and I fraternised, and chased rabbits
till sundown. Then we went back to the van, and
camped till 10 o’clock, waiting for an engine.
Twelve hours behind time we got to Goroke.
People are startled hereabouts when Washington
Bent’s train turns up to time.

One Sydney suburban “progress associa-
tion ” seriously threatens to live up to its name.
It proposes that Parliament shall declare that a
howling dog is a nuisance, and on the written in-
formation of three householders the animal shall
be slain. It is a good idea, but it doesn’t run to
the terminus. The writer hates cats worse than
dogs, and if this anti-dog society has any luck he
is going to form a progress association of his own,
and agitate for an extension of the law. In fact,
there is a vast field for the idea of authorising any
three householders to kill things that don’t agree
with them ; and if Parliament carries the
matter to that “logical conclusion” which it is
always talking about, the writer and two other
highly intelligent and discriminating householders
will fill the harbor with things that ought to be
got rid of, and people who would be better dead.
Dogs, cats, church-bells, tramcars, wild ra .bit
men, the push generally, street preachers, brass
bands, organ-grinders, and girls who pound on

IDEAL AND REAL.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
'Tis sweet to roam with you where lurks no sli-me,
- In glades ideal culling dreams for food :
(But sweeter far to hear you call my name
With just a wistful something in your mood).
’Tis glorious to frame .you, goddess, far, st elate,
To whom my flames of song delight to curl :
(But ’tis divine, when, coyly nigh, you ’wait
The hunter who pursues you as a girl).
Sublime that flawless blue, where cold stars shine,
When you are far, and I Devotion’s son :
(But ah ! the warm and rosy flushing wine
When you are here, and willing to be won !)
MELB.
ALCHERINGA.
MY LADY'S GOWN.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
My Lady’s gown doth glint and gleam
(’Tis some soft, shimm’ring shade of cream)
As with her fair companions gay
A down the room she takes her way,
Where the soft, silvery lamp-rays beam !
In every fold of it, I deem,
A grace doth hide. So neat each seam,
Full well it doth her form display,
My Lady’s gown !
I sit and watch, and then I dream—
As o’er her matchless form doth stream
The pale, soft light—of some fair day,
When none but Love is there to say
What rare and hidden beauties teem
’Neath that fair gown !
ULOOLA.
He who repeats a tale oft-told
' Is not so great a bore
As he who yawns—“ Ah, that is old ;
I’ve heard the yarn before !”

RAINCOATS.
A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, April 21, 1903.
My Drab Ahini,—
Through the mania of my maternal progenitor
for punctuality I (and the m.p.) were at Govern-
ment House for the Gov.-Gen. and Lady Tenny-
son’s first ball to the Sydney-siders somewhere
about the time the fowls went to bed.. The
function was timed for 9 o’clock, so I was in time
to see the ball-room and its drawing-room continu-
ation empty. I must say I liked it best so, on the
same principle that the interior of a fine church
before the worshippers arrive delights me most.
Lights and roses (roses, roses all the way from the
front door), peacock blue walls —Whistler walls —
and the pure, sleepy red of the carpet —same red as
the blare of a trumpet. Those walls and that car-
pet were caused by the Yorkal visit last year. If
Lady Hopetoun—l believe she chose it —had only
got an Australian wall-paper the room would
have been a light and a guide for our Australian
plutocrats—a dado of waratahs for example. A sad
thing it is that Australian riches go so little to
the encouragement of Australian art. Any local
effort at the applied arts rich Australians simply
ignore. Australian-used chairs are never carved
in Australia, nor are tapestries woven, nor wall-
papers designed, nor silver-ware cut in distinctly
Australian patterns.
A profile portrait in oils of Jate Laureate
Tennyson—evidently his favorite portrait, as its
engraving prefaces his works—stands on the grand
piano in Sydney Govt. House drawing-room. It
shows him with a floppy wide collar and half-
masted cloak, his long, wavy hair mingling with
his beard ; the deep, perpendicular wrinkle in
his cheek ; and in his eye the expression which
ought to have been there when he wrote “ Thou
inadest Life, Thou madest Death, and lo ! Thy
foot is on the skull which Thou hast made.” A
cunning fold of curtain is at the back of the por-
trait and the foliage of a ten-foot palm droops
above it. But the “skulls,” some of them
tiaraed, began to arrive, and thenceforth the
interest was wholly human.
It was a brilliant ball; but one brilliant Govt.
House ball is as like another as one league-long
roller is like its fellow. All the women-some-
bodies who were at the races during the last ten
days of weary A.J.C.-ism, with their heads and
shoulders clothed, came now with those members
uncovered. Lady Tennyson’s innocent face
smiled above a pale heliotrope gown of crepe de
chine , with a posy on the train, and her light
brown hair was brushed plainly below her dia-
mond half-crown. Lady Rawson was a Primrose
dame. Every tenth woman wore a great mass of
flowers or foliage on one shoulder; chrysanthe-
mums are in, and chrysanthemums were there.
_ Mrs. James Mills, of Maoriland, wore a thril-
ling gown. Alternate panels of white satin and
white sequined cobweb. Do you remember in the
Hans Anderson fairy tale the princess who had to
weave a gown of white cat’s whiskers and moon-
beams? Mrs. Mills broke out on one shoulder
into that blinding green—the green of certain
beetles’ wings, which is said to be fashionable. It
makes the individual wither.
Mrs. Henniker Heaton, junr., who, with her
husband, is visiting Australia, and has rented
Mr. Hamilton Dobbie’s “camp” at Rose Bay,
was the most inquired-after identity in the
room, because of her extraordinary display of
jewels. Said that her pearls are 800 years old
(and yet pearls are amongst the least durable
of gems—they catch diseases more easily than
their wearers). The fashion of her tiara, or
whatever the lady would call it, was so uncom-
mon as to cause a lot of stiff necks. Mrs. H. H.
is a tall, fair young woman, said to be the best
amateur miniature painter in or out of the Great
Smoke.
Baby roses are having a great vogue. They
wreathed old heads and young shoulders, but one
of the prettiest frocks in the room was Mrs.
Ernest Watt’s blue chiffon, with its bodice and
hanging sleeves closely sewn with forget-me-nots.
Miss Irene Marks, back from M.L., and still suf-
fering from the effects of her recent accident, held
a numerously attended reception, mostly of
solicitous-looking young men, all on her own, from
the coign of an arm-chair.
Several debutantes, amongst them Lily, a
daughter of State Premier See, the eldest ewe
lamb of Senator O’Connor, and Zelma, a
daughter of Mr. Fred Aaronson. Wonder if a
debutante’s first ball ever comes.up to her expec-
tations ? I know of one who, for years before her
first appearance, used to say that she was sure it
would be just like a ride through space on a
comet’s tail. Well, an hour after she debutted,
she started her first toothache, and her disgusted
family had to take her away and rouse up a
dentist. The incident altered her character ; she
joined some noisy sect, and preached and pranced
against balls.
One of the most curious effects in hairdressing
at the Gov.-General’s ball was wilfully achieved
by three girls (who looked like sisters), by brush-
ing the hair over circular frames covered with blue
chiffon. The color showed through in patches, as
if the hair was too scanty to cover the pad. Late
in the evening Misses Mollison and Munroe, of
the “Are You a Mason” co., arrived at the ball.
Former wore an orange gown with black spots, in-
tended to typify the orange tiger-lilies that grow
so liberally in Ethel’s Canadian country.
Among other-Staters at the ball was the Queens-
land short-storyist Ethel Mills, a fair, little dolly
person.
Button-holes for men must have gone out— -a loss
to the florists. Not a man at Government House,
but Prof. Anderson Stewart, wore onfe. I am
told that “they” (this “they” is what a writer
has called “ something as great and impersonal as
the Fates ”) do not put a flower-hole in the lapels
of evening coats now.
One of the conventional shams of our civilisation
was exhibited last Saturday night at Government
House when the Gov.-Gen. invested certain
Australians with K.C.M.G.’s, C.B.’s, D.S.O.’s
and: so forth. Each candidate had two “sup-
porters,” apparently on the understanding that he
was so overcome with gratitude and emotion that
his legs were liable to give way at any moment;
and the proper procedure was for the trinity, after
the centre-flower had got his bauble, to retreat
backwards. That is to say the supporters should
have retired backwards, carrying between them
an insensible candidate who had fainted through
ecstasy. The Westralian Forrest, and a few
others who had practised in London, maintained
the heel-first crawl, but those who hadn’t butted
round, and gave the group the look of a disorderly
circus, or a Mr. Facing-Both-Ways. You might
have heard a hairpin drop on a carpet when the
‘ ‘ brilliant assemblage ” got a clear back view of
genial John Forrest’s great, well-nourished calves
in white silk stockings, foiled against the black,
lead-pencil-like supports of Speaker Holder.
“When they rose knighted from the kneeling,
Some were pale, as at the passing of a ghost;
Some flushed and others dazed, as one who wakes
Half-olinded by the coming of a light.”
The task of yarding these political pure merinos
and starting them off troika fashion was entrusted
to Mr. George Steward, the Gov.-Geii’s Federal
Secretary (a gentleman suspected of pro-Teutonie
tendencies because he wears a fierce Kaiser
Wilhelm moustache). The Windsor Uniform—
with Reid, Lyne, Barton, and Forrest inside
it !! An admirer looked at Reid, sighed,
and remarked: “Wouldn’t I love to plant
pansies over him ! ” I can’t find out who
designed the Windsor Uniform, but I sus-
pect it was given with the Thunders on
Sinai; only Moses was ashamed of it and said
nuffin’. The Victorian Peacock laughed whilst
knighthood was in flower—a laugh like the sound
of a load of road metal tipped from a cart. Later,
whilst the reception was on, he sneezed, and that
—that was beyond a joke. The people didn’t
laugh, they simply shrank right up, and hoped
the Lord would keep his thumb on the plummer-
block.
At Investiture ceremony, Dame Forrest wore her
Coronation gown of Irish point with some etceteras
of mandarin velvet. Whenever I hear of Irish
point lace I think of that aristocratic but very swift
lady in one of Ouida’s novels who always put on
her Irish point over black velvet when her uncle,
the Archbishop, and his grave wife were coming
to dinner, and so dressed felt quite attuned to
higher things. The Forrest lady’s overdress of
I.p. holds birds and flowers and stars in its
pattern.
First charity ball which the Governor-General
and State Governor will attend this winter will
be that of Fresh Air League on the 19th of May.
St. Vincent’s Hospital people are also getting
ready for their annual dance.
Lord Mayor and Mrs. Hughes’ reception to
meet the Tennysons was not in brightness many
rays behind that of a solar system. They may
both step high over the success of last Monday
night. There was one sad item, and that was the
presence of 13 beautiful slain trees. These had
been cut olose to the ground, and were tied on to
the roof pillars. I sat under one and heard its spirit
wailing at being cut off in the flower of its youth
to decorate a holiday hour for a passing ruler.
Japanese mythology says that the man who need-
lessly cuts down a shade tree is imprisoned for
eternity in the bole of some forest giant and is
terribly racked as it grows. A useful religious
belief that! A long glittering chain, composed
of Tennysons and Rawsons and Hughes and vice-
regal relatives, friends and aides and private secs,
trailed up to a dais, and anyone who wished might
speak with any or every link of the chain. Nigh
a thousand persons were present. Tenor Lloyd
came along and exchanged Great Smoke reminis-
cences with the Governor.
Amongst the brides at recent A.J.C. meet-
ings was Mrs. Fisken, who was Mrs. David
M'Caughey—a well-dowered lady with several
olive branches.
Some folks are acting “Do,” instead of taking
the “Don’t” advice. Reports of 19 marriages
were sent to one Sydney morning daily during the
past five days.
A returned Durbarite—young, unsophisticated,
and a girl—tells this incident, that happened at
one of the ports en route. She strolled on shore
alone, and wandered into a print shop. A half-
breed shop-man received her gladly, and asked
her if she would like to look at some photographs.
She joyfully assented, and he put a pile before her.
One glance at the topmost and she turned and
fled, blushing furiously, back to the ship. Some
hours later she walked past the same shop with
two male fellow-voyagers, when the half-breed
ran out after the party, and, addressing the girl,
entreated her to “comeback and finish looking
at the photographs she saw in the morning.” His
revenge, perhaps, for her non-purchase of pictures.
Then she started stammering to the men about
not having looked at any but the top one, but
they began to talk hurriedly of other matters.
The promised Australian autobiography of Ada
Cambridge (Mrs. Cross) ought to be interesting
reading. Title is “Thirty Years in Australia.”
Mrs. Cross came from Norfolk (Eng.) as a bride,
and her husband’s first charge (he is a minister)
was in the Ned Kelly country. They are now in
Williainstown, Victoria. Like mo3t husbands
with writing wives, Mr. Cross never reads Mrs.
Cross. He misses much, for she is an interesting
writer. The item that the first copy of her books
is always written on slates, scores of slates, may
be interesting to some.
An English weekly has been holding a voting
competition on “Who is the Prettiest Panto-
mime Girl ? ” The poll had not quite closed when
I saw the list, but Ada Reeve came 17th, and
Grace Palotta 26th.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“ Melba made her final Melbourne appearance
on the hurricane deck of the Orontes, the other
day. She stood there eye-swollen, storm-shaken
-yone minute locked in the arms of a brother,
sister, or chum, the next, grieving aloud, her face
covered lay her hands. The crowd below—that
of the pier—gasped and stared. And at close
quarters a few fashionables gasped too, and were
silent. No one knew how to offer an appro-
priate word. Before the boat put off, Miss
Mignonette Clarke, tearful behind her conoealing
gossamer, crept to Melba’s side, linked her arm,
and whispered something which set them both
smiling. Gov’nor Clarke broke up the tableau,
and bore Melba off for a final calendar of in-
structions. His pet was not entrusted to the
singer’ hands with a haphazard ‘look after the
child.’ Both ma and pa Clarke held many con-
fabs over the matter, and impressed the custo-
dienne with the importance and weight of her
mission.
“With Melba were the Sassolis (harpist and
mamma). The little Signorina evidently failed
to comprehend why ‘Madame ’ was so miserable,
with colossal concert cheques in her treasury and
a great welcome awaiting her at the journey’s end.
But Madame had confessed to her people that she
was broken-hearted, for the time being, by unkind
paragraphs.
“The diva’s going-away garb took the quiet
but expensive shape of dark-blue voile gown,
sable cape, toque with pale-blue pansies, black
veil lined with white chiffon, and white gloves.
“ Quite a dress-parade, the departure of the
Orontes. Miss Vera Clarke (Cliveden) aired blue
Russian coat, turned back with white, and toque
of nutmeg Color, touched with cherry. Miss Eva
Robertson had revers of Oriental embroidery in
brown, gold, and mandarin on her brown frieze.
Mesdames Buckley, junior, sported the new short
skirts, which, to unaccustomed eyes, seem pert
and ‘pronounced.’ Little Miss Clarke, the
Governor’s departing daughter, had mauve front,
livening up her black frock, and looked very pale
under her white veil.
“People on the wharf were comparing notes in
confidential tones. They wondered if it was true
that Melba intended to sing Tosti’s ‘ Good-bye ’
to the accompaniment of the band as the boat
sheered off. Also they declared that, ‘if it was
them, they would leave off the rouge on a day they
were booked to do some blubbing.’ One or two of
the smartest farewellers were openly mopping
away their roses with their tears.
“Justice Hartley Williams’ dame and daughter
held rival farewells on the first deck that day.
No tears; the usual trite things said ; the usual
flowers offered; everything strictly smart. Yet
it is a final good-bye.
“ There is a heavy crop of stories anentthe Star
now that she revolves no more in this orbit. A
male warbler was practising his tootle for a grand
concert. Melba appeared, hearkened a while,
then rushed at him, snatched his music sheet, tore
it to pieces and flung it aside. And when, the
astonishment over, his face fell, and his eyes grew
moist, Melba advised : ‘For heaven’s sake, don’t
look at me like that. You want your ears boxed.
O you booby ! Now start afresh. Oh bother the
music ! And sing, man, sing—don’t gargle!’
Another. Somebody mentions Tasmania. ‘ Here,
drop it. Don’t rasp on about the brutal place. I
lost half my furnishings there with the tossing of
the tub. Ugh ! ’ Tasmania was dropped.
“ There are regular ‘Stonington’ visitors who
aver that the daughter of the house was able to
finance herself on the present trip to Paris. Miss
Clarke plies a pen prettily, and to some purpose,
and is believed to glean a fair amount of lucre
thereby. A local family paper of great bulk has
published some of her innocent stories, or is
believed to have done so. But the greater number
are said to go to the magazines of the Big Smoke.
“Bishop Clarke radiated his smiles on the C. of
E. Grammar School party on Saturday, and
shone as only the elastic-tongued shine at such
fluttersome occasion:?. Study of the large, healthy,
handsome face bears home the happy conviction
that here gaiters are fitted to a mind of mirth.
When the gathering moved indoors, the Anglican
Chief was moved to platform talk. He said some
wise things, some good things, and showed signs
of a prompt wit.
“A curio in the agony column of Saturday’s
Age :
A LTHOUGH distance separates, Love don’t. Hope
on. Love from all. Guarantee payments alright.
It’s their day now, our’s will follow. “ Raleigh.”
“There has been a rush to the altars. Last
week, in brisk suburbs, popular parsons polished
off two or three knots a day. More are
promised for the coming season of chrysanthe-
mums. The quietest effected out Toorak way
was that of Mrs. David M'Caughey, of illimitable
coffers and rich acres, to Melbourne’s verdant
dandy bachelor, Mr. John Fisken, who is quite
famous for his deals in big cattle runs. The cere-
mony was conducted pretty well in camera at the
bride’s own swagger abode in Orrong-road.
“This week, at “Cliveden,” Miss Mary Clarke is
to marry the Hon. Robert Lindsay. The ceremony
has been hurried a little, for the officer has to
return with all speed to headquarters.
“ A divorce suit now costs little more than the
other kind of suit in which man proclaims him-
self. A prominent solicitor and barrister adver-
tises in the daily press that his advice is to be had
gratis, and that he takes divorce cases through
the court for £6. He doesn’t head his ad., ‘Why
remain married, when you can be happy if you
come to me.’ But I see that ad. looming in the
near future.
“A sunstroke laid Mr. Peter Tyson low a few
weeks ago, and so seriously was he affected that
the news of his unconsciousness spread, and the
hospital ’phone was ting-a-linging all day long
with inquiries. The doctors pulled him through
the ‘ stroke.’
“ One of those nice, agreeable bachelors of the
order that boils the billy at the ant-hill picnic,
and turns over the music-leaves in the drawing-
room, and flirts alternately with mother and
daughter, has got his particular self into trouble
by being inquisitive after the handmaid of a Lady
——
Modish. It transpires that the afternoon look-in
was always timed at the precise hour the ladv of
the house was out, and Suzette did the enter-
taining. One day the mistress, while at the
telephone in a friend’s house, heard the bachelor
and the parlormaid exchange greetings and ex
pressions of joy at the chance of a ‘call’ hi
the ‘old girl’s’ absence. The ‘old girl’ and
bachelor arrived simultaneously at the door from
different directions. Suzette is now looking for a
new place.
“George Smith, the shrewd young man who
gave up tinkering in tapes, etc., in a shop, top ur
sue the mummers’ calling, and who fell in for the
managership of Wilson Barrett’s co., has married
one of the promising artists of the co. Having
taken the Barrett co. Transvaal wards, he went in
for management on his own, and now runs four or
five shows in S’Africa.
“In the beautiful ‘Dream’ show Oberon
Rignold sings two or three song snatches with
special charm—soft, deep notes of ’cello stoutness.
An Oberon, hers, of sweet strength and gentle
mastery. The Titania of Miss Nora Kerin is very
lovely, full of tender woofulness and fairy grace.
The pair are exquisite in their forest scenes'
Misses Cynthia Brooke and Maud Milton chase
their lovers in classic drapes and with much poetic
fury. The former, as Hermia, dresses her
generous form in the green of marble veins ; the
latter, as the distracted Helena, clothes her blonde
pleasantness in blossom pink.
“ The Melbourne Women Writers’ Club assem-
bled in force at Port Melbourne last week to bid
goodbye to Miss Katie Conway, niece of Mr.
David Watterston, of the Australasian. She is
going to the cold country for six months. Pre-
viously, the club spread itself at an evening in
honor of the parting guest. Men were permitted
to come, but only two members ventured to bring
their male belongings, who gazed on each other
with rapt sympathy. The evening, however,
passed off well, and another is projected, when
every member vows to bring either a man or his
scalp.
“ It is becoming the thing to carry fencing foils
instead of a golf kit or croquet mallet through
Collins-st. A smart dame, out St. Kilda way, is
coaxing her smart friends to form a fencing-class
at her house for the cold season. Various Toorak-
ites have started, and now try their paces in
theatrical brown velvet knickers and white tunics
or shirts. The bandy-kneed hold back, and others
who refuse are at once suspected of ‘bunkers,’ to
use a golfing term, on the calf. Even if these
fashionable patrons of the foils never seriously
learn to thrust and parry with any alertness,
they’ll have the satisfaction of shaping in the
tricky garb of the fence. By the way, lovely
woman abandons the long, tangle-heel sleeping
robe for natty pyjamas. A she-visitor to a Toorak
house recently, finding this novelty under her
pillow, took alarm, cast the shocking things away
and slept in her bath wrapper. But now she is
initiated and rejoices.
“In ‘Ma Mie Rosette,’ the palace sleeping-
arrangements are unique. When Henri rings the
alarum-bell in the watches of the night to show
Rosette and Vincent that he’s not the sort of king
to submit to blackmail, the servants march in,
entering the room in serried ranks, men in front,
women behind, all dressed in nightgowns, but
orderly as drill-sergeants. It looks as if, in
France, male and female all snoozed together
somewhere on the palatial pavement, in regimental
array. Perhaps Henri, knowing the kind of king
he was, thought there was safety in numbers. If
Rosette, for instance, had camped on space sin
the second-last row of a sleeping army of scullions
and soubrettes, she might not have been troubled
with that mystic feeling stealing over her that
raised such a riot in hospitable Henri’s chateau.
“Last two winners of Melb. Art School’s
travelling scholarships have been Jews—the Alt-
son brothers. Next winner, ’tis said, will be a
member of the same race, in the person of young
Cohen.
“Secret marriages are becoming the vogue.
Smart girls say it’s an excellent and commend-
able thing to get the parental blessing in pri-
vate, and then ‘elope,’ thus saving the tedious
wait of a trousseau and the expense of the joy-
bells.
“ The deft diving of Annette Kellermann is only
one of many accomplishments. The sturdy brown
mermaid has feet of the lightness of fluff and spins
them into a pas de fascination with the readiness
and ease of the professional tripper. She sings,
recites, and fences well, and may yet turn her
voice and Trilbies to account on the stage.
J.C.W. has his eye on her. The wet, brown maid
is attending to one or other of her qualifications
when not diving among the scared fish of the
aquarium or prowling along the shelly beach of
Mentone. One drawback only—Annette is short.
All the portraits are true as to arched insteps and
bold curves, but exaggerate her height. The ordi-
nary elevators would hardly lift her to the mark
of stage distinction. She ought to be rolled out a
bit.
Yours affectionately,

PERSONAL ITEMS.
Henry Lawson is now in Prince Alfred
Hospital, suffering from nerve break-down.
An Adelaide girl, the other day, died from
the effects of the bite of a cockatoo, received less
than 24 hoilrs previously.
Varney Parkes employs some of his
present leisure in touching up that promised
volume of anecdotes about the late Henry.
The recent shuffle of State Premiers
leaves only one—See—with a title in Australia.
See is English, Irvine Irish, Philp Scotch, Jen-
kins Yankee, and James and Propsting Aus-
tralians.
Miss Miriam Vigar has been licensed as a
local preacher in the Yorketown (S.A.) Methodist
circuit. The report of the parson who examined
her in theology and such is that she “ acquitted
herself very creditably.”
“Johnny” Hines, who knew the favorite
tipple and liquor capacity of every South Austra-
lian legislator for the past 47 years, during which
long time he had been Parliamentary caterer,
retired from the service at the beginning of April.
A North Sydney woman visited Sydney
during Show week for the first time in 24 years,
although she had lived all these years within one
mile of the harbor, on the ridge above Neutral
Bay. This is the sort of material out of which
oldest inhabitants are made.
It has taken James Fraser, the new
Engineer-in-Chief of N.S.W. railways, just 25
years to travel from the bottom to the top of his
professional ladder. He began at Redfern as a
youth of 17., He was born in Braidwood, and
received all his training in N.S.W.
A Roman Catholic pioneer priest died at
Ryde (N.S.W.) recently. Dean Hallinan arrived
in Sydney in 1843, and in the following year was
ordained by Archbishop Polding. He was 91
years of age and for 10 years resided at the home
for aged priests established by Archpriest Sheehy
at Ryde.
A FAIRY STORY.
CHLORIS : “ Yer orter come to the mission, Bill.
The tart wot teaches was tellin' us ’bout
fairies. Ever hear ’bout ’em?”
BILL : "Fairies, yers; didn’t one of’em that plays
in the panto, get took up from our lane fer
nickin’ a mug’s purse.”
Mr. “Reggie” Black, M.L.C., who is off
on a tour of Europe, is a director of almost every-
thing big, from the A.M.P. down to the Perpetual
Trustees, in Sydney. He was for many years a
member of the Sydney D.T. board, and was
known as “ a good man for the staff ” ; there was
much sorrow among the reporters when he left
that board to go into the somnolent politics of the
Upper House.
William Martin, originally of Scotland
Yard, but for nearly half a century resident in
N.S.W., and for a good many years a senior-
constable in the Sydney Water Police, went home
for a trip last week, though 77 years of age. He
was instrumental in the arrest of Thomas Scource,
the waterman, who, on the night of Feb. 2, 1873,
murdered Mrs. Bedelia Lee while conveying her
in a boat from Circular Quay to Milson’s Point.
Scource was executed for the crime.
Governor-General Tennyson will remem-
ber his latest visit to Sydney by reason of the
query of a gushing young woman, in voluminous
attire: “Is it really true, my Lord, that you
threw yourself down in the grass and wrote off
that long poem about Locksley Hall ? The part
about marrying some kind of a mulatto woman is
awfully touching. But there was one character in
it I never could make out —‘the individual
Withers,’ I think he was called.”
“Fluffy Joe” (as people used to call
Joseph Fisher, once proprietor of the Adelaide
Register, when 20 years ago he occupied a seat
in the local Legislative Council) has just distri-
buted £3400 among Adelaide institutions because,
as he expresses it, he objects to the iniquitous law
under which the Government deducts 10 per cent,
from charitable bequests He intended to leave
the money by will, but the temptation to get the
better of the Treasurer was too strong for him.
Mr. Spruce Myth, M.H.R., is taking a
freat interest in the Inter-State Commission Bill.
’ossibly Mr. Myth thinks he might adorn that
Commission, possibly he doesn’t; anyway he does
his best to convince members of the beauties of
the Bill. “You don’t understand this,” said he
to one member (there are few people in the world
who understand as much as Mr. Spruce Myth
thinks he does) “ I’ll show you.’! And he drew a
handful of coins out of his pocket and proceeded
to explain! "‘This ” (putting down a half-crown)
“is the large State of N.S.W,; here to the north
(putting down a shilling) “ is Queensland ; to the
south” (putting down a florin) “is Victoria, and
here to the west Heavens! who passed off that
bad shilling on me ? ” And the lesson there ended
With Mr. Spruce Myth lamenting his cronk
“bob.”
J. W. Fleming, Melbourne’s boss unem-
ployed agitator, has become quite a sassiety fad
since the departing Hopetoun entrusted him with
the task of distributing £lOO in cash and 50 dozen
of champagne among the deserving poor. Chief
Justice Madden now regularly forwards to
Fleming sums handed to him for the unemployed,
and the people who, a few years ago, looked upon
J.W. as a bomb-thrower and a dangerous incen-
diary, now regard hirn as the source of all reliable
information for the guidance of the charitably
disposed.
Premier Propsting, of Tasmania, was once
a school teacher in S.A., and when acting in that
capacity at Kapunda fell in love with a daughter
of Speaker Jenkin Coles. The father did not
consider the young man’s prospects good enough,
so Propsting resigned, returned to Hobart, studied
for the Bar, was admitted, and then tried again
for the girl he left behind him. Seven years had
passed, but she remained true and they were
married. But for that episode Propsting might
possibly be still whacking the backward and un-
regenerate kid in the Crow-eater’s Land.
Died near Perth, early this month,
Thomas Chipper, till then the oldest surviving
native-born Westralian. The foundation of the
colony arrived a day ahead of him. For the best
part of his life he was a considerable link be-
tween the Swan River settlement and the
rest of the world : he carried the mails from
Albany to Perth for 30 years without a break.
With his son-in-law, ex-Minister Piesse, he got
hold of the finest country in the rich Katanning
district, where he died.
On April 21, 35 years ago, O’Farrell was
hanged at Darlinghurst Gaol for the attempted
assassination of the late Duke of Edinburgh at the
Clontarf picnic. All the prominent actors in the
drama and its surroundings are now dead. The
Judge, the Premier, the Ministers of the Crown,
the barristers and the court officials directly con-
nected with the case have all gone. Mr. Sam
Cook, of the S.M. Herald, was connected with it
as special reporter for his paper of a prison inter-
view between Parkes and O’Farrell, and lives to
tell the tale, but his connection was merely that
of a phonograph.
Customs Minister Kingston was at one
time a private, and later on a corporal, in the
military forces of S.A. He was still in
that humble military position when he be-
came Premier. At autumn encampments
when the military Commandant wanted to
see his boss, Premier Kingston, he called
respectfully at Kingston’s tent; when he
wanted his humble military inferior Cor-
poral Kingston he sent for the corporal
and that very subordinate person waited
on the Commandant and saluted. It says
a good deal for Kingston’s ta,ct that he
managed to keep liis two capacities separate.
Apropos the news that King Ned
has increased the number of members of the
order of St. Michael and St. George.
Frederick Treves, the King’s surgeon, tells
a story :—“During the Boer war N.S.W.
Government sent to S’Africa two expert
surgeons to do the operative work for the
ambulances. One of them was recom-
mended for a decoration, and had con-
ferred on him a C.M.G. There was also
at the war another doctor, a young ’un
getting a pound a day, whose name was the
same. In gazetting the decorations the
War Office by mistake gave the C.M.G.
to the wrong man, and the right one was
left out. The War Office never admits its
mistakes, and so things remained at that.”
The late Chief Detective Inspector
of Scotland Yard holds that Klosoffski, the
Polish publican, of Southwark, recently
hanged in London for poisoning three
women, is identical with “Jack the
Ripper,” of Whitechapel notoriety during
’BB. More places have contended for the
honor of having given birth to the Ripper than
was the case with Homer.
An English man-o’-war’s man was, when
the last London mail left, under court-martial for
writing a pamphlet describing the incidents of the
naval trip of Mr. Chamberlain to S’Africa.
“ Gross insubordination ” and “ publishing ser vice
secrets ” are the main charges against the sailor-
man, whose book related how Mr. Chamberlain
was sea-sick and how “ the ship was stopped at
meal times to enable him to take a little food.
The collapse of Brummagen Joe’s stomach was
evidently a State secret not to be revealed with
impunity.
The death is announced at Wellington
(Maoriland) of Dr. Morgan Stanislaus Grace,
C.M.G., M.L.C., Count of the Holy Roman
Empire (so-called because it is a kind of disturbed
and unholy Austrian Federation), and some
other frills. He was 67, and did nothing, and did
it very badly, in the M.L. Legislative Council for
33 years prior to his death. Wellington re-
members him, because at the time of the tramway
strike he waved a banner from a balcony and
called on God to defend the right—by which it is
presumed he meant the tramway co.

Professor G. B. Hal-
ford is hale and hearty
at the age of nearly
80, on his farm at In-
verloch (Vic.) Among
his reminiscences is one
concerning the re-
ceipt, at the Uni.
versity, in ’63,
from the cap-
turers of bush-
ranger Mor-
gan, of that
desperado's head for the
purpose of phrenological
examination. The result,
if any, was never made
known, much to the dis-
appointment of the en-
thusiasts who despatched
the parcel. Halford
likewise recalls being
knocked up at 2 a.m. in
the late 60’s by Police
Magistrate Drummond,
who was dying from
snake poison, having
foolishly allowed
himself to be
bitten by a tiger
snake in the
possession of one
Shires, with the
object of deter-
mining if that
impostor’s anti-
dote was of any
value.
Norfolk Is-
land proudly
claims to have
given one peer
to the House of Lords. A wild young
scapegrace named Pery, of the house of
Limerick, landed in Sydney in Bourke’s day with
letters of introduction to the Governor. Bourke
sent him to a clerical position in Norfolk Island.
He married there and had a son, and that son, 26
years after, became Earl of Limerick and took his
seat in the House of Lords.
John Abbott, one of the ancients of the
Bendigo Beehive, where they deal in shares
and other things, used, before he died the other
day, to tell of the old times when he and his mates
were breaking 600 oz. stone from the United
Johnson’s. In the present sdwt. days a memory
like that is refreshing to look back upon.
Grandfather, father, and son, the Stephens
have occupied seats on N.S.W. Supreme Court
Bench for 58 years. John Stephen, 8;
Alfred, 34; and Matthew Henry, the
.present Judge, 16.
J. V. Balbirnie, an old Yic.
colonist who died at St. Kilda a few
years ago, was said to be the lineal
descendant of Lord Balmerino, exe-
cuted in 1746 for rebellion. Any
money that was attached to the title
vanished long ago, for when the last
Balmerino was executed he was even
short of clothes. A good many efforts
have been made to recover the lost
dignity, but without success.
Edward Spicer, the S.A. Wes-
leyan Methodist thousandaire, who
every now and then builds half-a-
dozen cottages for retired Methodist
meenisters, donates muchly to the
building funds of churches. He was
one of the State’s early pastoralists
and importers. That axiom, “The
early bird catches the worm ” is true
in his case as it is in the cases of so many
other early settlers. They left mighty
little undigested worm for those who
came after them.
Bishop Camidge, of Bathurst, is
probably the only professional man, lay
or clerical, in Australia who ever climbed,
steeplejack fashion, to the top of a church
in England, and successfully fixed the
vane, to the great surprise of the local
“ jack ” who “ had missed the ’bus.”
The Bulletin would pay that appallingly
good man, Speaker Holder, of the Reps., half-a-
crown for a paragraph (Holder is a journalist)
describing his feelings while the circus-tricks were
proceeding at Sydney Government House on
Saturday night. Holder is an unassuming man
who has lived a singularly retired life, on a
most modest scale ; and this paper wants to
know what he felt like when he was haul-
ing the knee-breeches over his painfully lean
shanks, and afterwards, when surrounded by “a
blaze of color,” he had to bob and balance before
a “Society” crowd. Apparently he didn’t feel
well over the silly business, because, next morn-
ing, he was out at the Glebe, preaching a sermon
in a Methodist pulpit, and trying hard to forget
that he hadn’t been behaving as usual.
Macdonald Patterson, the Queensland
M.H.R., makes it known, in connection with a
Customs case, that for the last two years he haa
had none of the profits of the Queensland firm of
Macpherson, Patterson, and Macpherson. Mac-
donald P. has many entertaining ways. Some
time ago he explained his waving of hands in the
frenzy of oratory by saying he had no pockets in
his trousers, and, on examination, the garment
was found to have been made without those recep-
tacles. He had ordered his tailor so to make it.
Query : Had that anything to do with his ceasing
to draw profits from his law business ? A lawyer
without pockets is an anomaly.
The late Rev. Charles Clark was by way of
being an athlete, and prided himself thereon.
During his first lecturing tour, he addressed the
students at Melb. University on the horrors of
drink and other current evils, and laid it down as
an axiom that no man could train on beer.
Waxing warm, he offered to beat over a mile op
the flat any beer-drinker amongst the students.
An undergraduate took him up, and the match
was arranged. The students christened their
beer-drinker “Mammon,” and Clark “the other
fellow,” and the betting was on “Mammon.”
When the race came off, “Mammon” let Clark
lead for a little way, and then, catching him up,
began to talk affably to him —the very surest way
to make an opponent lose his head. Clark made
a few valiant efforts to answer some searching
questions on the higher life while he ran, and got
badly rattled. The youth of beer won by a street.
Re a dead ironmonger :
Mr. George Scarfe, the head of the great firm of Harris,
Scarfe and Co., who died in Adelaide on Easter Tuesday,
aged 76, was reputedly worth £BOO,OOO, hut his personal
expenses did not exceed £4OO a year. He was a bachelor,
and until recently lived at the York Hotel. For more
than a quarter of a century before his final illness (which
only lasted ten days) he was never absent from his office
for one working day. He was so punctual in arriving
that he was called “ the peripatetic clock ” of the neigh-
borhood, while he was usually the last to leave. He came
to Adelaide a poor man over half a century ago and started
a general ironmongery store, subsequently becoming
associated with George P. Harris, who predeceased him.
These two men, emptying their lives of everything but
ironmongery, built up between them the biggest hard-
ware business probably in Australia There was not a
country town in their own State where they did not do
business, and very few enterprises of a payable kind in
which they had not some sort of interest. George Scarfe
was a man who devoted all the time he could spare from
his business to attendance on directors’ meetings, and
within a fortnight of his death, although then very
feeble, he was at a gas company’s meeting looking after
his fee. He never took the slightest interest in political,
municipal, philanthropic or other public matters,
except when taxation day came near. In short, he was
the Complete Ironmonger.
THE APPEAL TO THE PHILISTINE.
Sydney Art Society is holding an exhibition of
small, low-priced pictures with the hope of
inducing the public to buy.
Maoriland supplied Hector Macdonald
with his most importunate autograph-hunter.
Travelling from Wanganui his train was hailed at
St. John’s by a woman with a parasol. The train
was pulled up, according to regulations, and the
woman then coolly hunted for her lion. She
found him; and her sublime impudence succeeded.
Then she told the guard he might go on.

Aboriglnalities.
To Contributors. — ln the case of ethnological and
natural history items, etc., always give date and
place of experience ;or as nearly as possible. Thus
many interesting observations will have scientific
value.
A letter received from a place between the
Darling and the S.A. border, in the region that is
bounded by the crow on the east and Sheol and
lead-poisoning on the south, and Jenkins Iscariot
on the west and the ghost of Cain on the north :
Dear Bulletin.—The murmuring breeze alluded to
below assays 97J per cent, red dust and 21 per cent, half-
bricks. There has not been an opening flower to woo
since God knows when. The rippling brook is a drought
dream. I believe it did go babbling on once, but as it, is
80 I will not swear it ; and every rush and every
reed was long since devoured by sheep, who have since
been devoured in turn by crows and ants. But it is a
change, anyhow, to dream of something other than hides
hanging on a fence, and starving animals, and grassless
earth, and boiled water, and scraggy meat, and preserved
vegetables and rice every meal, and the sheep that are all
away, and the dead trees I have for company. So we
write verses sometimes to the imaginary girls we may
have when the drought is over :

“D. Harum ” • Was along the Overland
Telegraph route, a little north of Oodnadatta,
some time back, on a Govt, waggon. Just before
we left Oodnadatta I observed the driver diligently
gathering stones and placing them under the seat.
Enquired what they were for and he told me to
wait. After going some way the mules dropped
into a lazy walk, and the driver, seizing a stone,
let drive at the liind-quarters of the leader and off
we went again at a fair pace. Every few minutes
the driver would shake the mules up in this
maglner, though he never used his whip. Ap-
parently, from long experience, he knew it was of
no use.
“ Jaybell ” on a cat :
The dearth of dead cat in the Australian bush is abso-
lutely alarming, and no amount of poisoned rabbit or
defunct marsupial can fill the long-felt want, from an
educational point of view. Bush people actually don’t
know a dead cat. Nephew Bill, aged 18, paid me a visit
in the city the other week ; he’d never been down before.
Bill was no flat, and he played the concertina like an
angel (a guarantee that he was of a studious turn of
mind). I used to take Bill out sight-seeing and explain
things. We got on well together till one day, in our
strolling, we bisected a horizontal column of well-
developed odor. “ A rabbit,” said Bill, on the unsup-
ported testimony of his nose. Then I sighted the base
of the column aforesaid, and conjectured deceased cat.
It was a cat. Bill looked at it and touched it with his
foot, and called it “ poysoned rabbert." I stuck to “cat,”
and drew Bill s attention to its maximum tail
and minimum ear. Bill only said, “That’s nothin’; it
ain tno cat.” I told him that if it was a rabbit it would
be hanging up in a fish and game shop for sale. “They
don t allow dead rabbits to waste their fragrance on the
circumambient atmosphere like that in the city,” I said.
Bill said “ garn,” and I said it was a fact. Bill said,
“ Which—the eireumdentiimmustophere ? ’’—you see I’d
been explaining things to Bill and teaching him a bit. So
I said, “No, the cat.” Bill said, “You’ll do, but you
don’t have me, you know.” Then we went down Queen-
street and Bill bought three mouth-organs dirt cheap—
BUI can play two at once. When we got home Bill told
my landlady how I’d “ been trying to pull his leg, but it
didn t come off.” She, poor creature, being an emotional
Seventh-day Adventist, chided me for my cruelty to Bill,
and asked Bill if I made it bleed. Poor Bill was only a
Bush Baptist himself, so he actually thought that the
gentlewoman in reduced circumstances had ulterior
designs upon him, and became so scared that he cleared
homeward next morning by the westward train. The
last letter I had from Bill he was laughing over my
“dead cat” joke, and said all the blokes out there
laughed, too, when he told them.

W. T. Goodgo on a political matter :
“ Brother electors,” said the orator on the platform at
Bushman’s Flat, “ if the Old Country asks us to pay part
of the cost of the British Navy, we must do so, unless
we are prepared to maintain a fleet of our own. We
must remember, gentlemen, that blood is thicker than
water! ”
“I’m damned if it is!” interjected the man from
Nevertire.
“ What’s that?” fiercely demanded the orator.
“ You say,” said the man from Nevertire, “ that blood
is thicker than water?”
“ I do,” said the orator.
“Well,” said the man from Nevertire, “up our way
there ain’t a waterhole where the water ain’t thicker than
pea-soup, and if blood’s any thicker than that, it must
be like porridge.”
And that was the last time that a silly old phrase
was heard at Bushman’s Flat.

Dear Bulletin. — How some of our parsons
love the Ghow ! Last Saturday morning, was
riding with one (parson) to Newcastle,
when two men in a vegetable cart came
through the sliprails, a quarter of a mile
on, and travelled ahead of us. As we
drew up to the shandradan, the sky-
pilot said : ‘ ‘ Celestials ! And how
quiet and well-behaved they are ! Alto-
gether different from Europeans, who
would most likely be smoking filthy
tobacco, or indulging in obscenity!
Let us,” said he, “ wish them the time
of day in passing, and say a kindly
word to the poor fellows.” By this
time, we had got close behind the cart,
and the monkey-shaved gentleman re-
marked how nice and clean the vege-
tables were. So different from what
we see with Europeans. Just here, not
getting the usual whiff of manure from
the cart, I began to have doubts about
chinkies. When we got abreast of the
cabbages, we turned to say the kindly
word, and found the men not Chows at
all, but white as ourselves, and decent-
looking men, too. The parson didn’t
even ask how they were ; and there was
nary a “kindly word” from him to
“the poor fellows.” It sickens me.—
Yarrum.
“ Quad ” : Richmond River (N.S.
Wales) has boasted of some large vege-
table marrows, but one scaling 591bs.
tops the list. This stands at credit of
a Lincolnshire (Eng.) grower, who from
American seed produced the monster.
It was not artificially fed with sugar
and water by means of a string and
bottle —a favorite practice when rear-
ing vegetable monstrosities—or forced by the
application of an electric current, but was merely
well nurtured. A London daily asked for a
larger specimen but could not get it.
• * * •
“ Chasselas ” : Bulletin writers are fond
of picturing Vic. cockies as grasping, “soulless”
men. What about this for white ? A cockie em-
ployed a total stranger on the farm, and, after a
few weeks’ work, the man’s leg was caught and
badly crushed in cog-wheels, necessitating ampu-
tation. Cockie got best surgeon available to assist
hospital doctor at the operation, paid all expenses,
and often visited his man during the time he was
ill, and then presented him with the best cork leg
procurable. Cockie is only a struggling share-
man, and had his house and chattels burnt a short
while before. Remonstrated with by a richer
neighbor about his generosity, he said, “Ah, well,
he’s only got one leg, I’ve two.”

Classic us ” says : Touching your par.
(4/4/03) on the Queensland swagman who pro-
fessed to have discovered an antidote to snakebite
by means of a nostrum compounded of decom-
posed snakes’ bodies, it is just possible that dead-
beat may have once been a Master of Arts;
certainly he could claim the credit of classical
antiquity for his mixture. The ancients firmly
believed that the best antidote to the bite of the
viper was a confection of its own flesh. The
Greek word “theriac,” or “flesh of the viper,”
was first given to such a delicacy, and then to any
antidote of poison, and lastly to any syrup, until
it finally came to be corrupted into our present
•' reac ? e • ,, Chaucer has a line which runs :
Christ, which that is to every harm triacle.”
Milton speaks of “the sovran treacle of sound
doctrine ”; and Edward Waller, in a verse which
~a - f £ rave^e d many a modern reader, wrote:
n/rXi 1 fi ers . * reac^e yield.” Furthermore, in the
Middle Ages, a stuff called “Venice treacle’’was
considered an antidote to all poisons. Archbishop
Irench was the first to connect treacle with vipers.

Though splendid rains have fallen on the
country between Goulburn and Hay, the prospect
for sheep-feed even is very remote. In other
years if half the pris-nt rain had fallen there
would have been a wonderful growth of herbage
Ihe reason assigned for this lack of growth is
that the rains which fell first were so heavy as
to wash away the light soil and natural manure
(there being now practically no trees to retard or
prevent the washaway), leaving the earth quite
hungry. The young grass shoots, but the earth
now hardens so quickly that it dies off again
Mackay (N.Q.) has a curiosity in the shape
of a “ wild boy ” named Stewart, who has been
roaming the bush for months. Recently he was
chased by a constable and tracker, who came on
him while he was swimming. He beat them,
leaving his shirt, trousers, blanket, and billy!
What offence there is in being a wild boy doesn’t
appear very clearly, but the police are pursuing
this one with great energy. Possibly Philp wants
to put him on the electoral roll
“C.L.”: Going North lately, met a
prudent squatter, who foresaw the drought and
sold out profitably, and took his family to civilisa-
tion to be educated. Being a prudent man he
invested his money in buildings at Townsville!
When I met him he was going up to look at the
ruins, and see if he could raise enough money on
them to repair his properties. Also met the impru-
dent squatter who didn’t sell out. He recently bor-
rowed a horse and started to muster. At the end
of a week he had seen 27 extremely fat iguanas
and some very thin cockatoos, but that was about
all. He had 4000 head of cattle once upon a time.

Junee (N.S.W.) has produced an egg-
laying rooster. He has already laid a dozen, and
appears to be still going strong. The hens with
which he had hitherto consorted, howe'er,
resent his conduct strongly as that of a
non-unionist, and the owner has now to
keep him shut up for pro-
tection.
“ Eller ” : Recently saw a curious happen-
ing between a rabbit and an eagle-hawk. When
I first sighted the pair the hawk was sweeping
rapidly towards the ground, and bunny was lying
motionless ; but, as the bird made its pounce, the
rabbit leapt fully oft. into the air, and the hawk
swept harmlessly past beneath, while bunny made
full speed for cover. Ere he could reach it, how-
ever, the hawk came at him again, when the
rabbit flopped down as before, and repeated the
high-jump and get-away, this time making good
his escape.
MY LOVE IS TWENTY-TWO.
My Love hath locks of sunny brown, with which the
breezes play ;
They flitter-flutter round her brow, as if they’d fly away.
And she hath smiles like beams of light, and eyes of
dreamy blue,
And dimpled cheeks; but best of all—My Love is Twenty-
two.
So, twinkling Stars 1 glide on your way. Go, Sun ! your
course pursue;
Bid every gleam you shed proclaim—My Love is Twenty-
two.
My Love can stay the lowering storm, and chase the
clouds away ;
The magic of her glance can turn dark night to radiant
day.
And though she’s coy, demure and shy, whene’er I come
to woo,
I dare not murmur, for you know My Love is Twenty-
two.
So murmuring breeze, where’er you go the summer flower
to woo,
To every rosebud whisper low, My Love is Twenty-two.
And she hath moods that move and change, and ways
that do beguile,
With every frown she makes a wound, then heals it with
a smile.
So nought care I how rivals rage, or what they choose
to do,
My Love, I know, loves only me—and she is Twenty-two.
So, rippling brook, go babbling on the summer meadows
through,
Tell every rush, tell every reed, My Love is Twenty-two.
N.S.W.

“ San Remo ” on the fruit-eating bat: —
Gan support “ Wiljar’s ” statement in “Aboriginalities ”
(11/4/’O3) re the flying fox pest. One has to visit their
camping grounds to get an adequate idea of their immense
numbers. One of their breeding grounds is on Central
Island, in the Fitzroy River (Q.). I have witnessed the
foxes evening flight from there to their feeding grounds
inland, and I can assure The Bulletin that if it were
interested in fruit growing in Central Queensland, and
saw a flight, it would more than “ clear its throat
violently.” I have seen a dense black cloud of flying
foxes, 200 broad, literally blot out the sky, and this
stream of smellful bats fled across the landscape for two
hours before the last fox had left the trees. “ Wiljar ”is
quite right about the way the pests swarm on the
branches of trees, and his estimate of their breeding
ground as being a mile square is quite reasonable. I
should say IS killed with one cartridge is nothing to
boast of. Given a 15-pounder cannon and canister shot I’d
6ug a g' e to bag about 1000. But lam not taking on the game
for sport. They smell too bad. If in leeward proximity
of a haunt when a flight begins one fancies he has boarded
a 5000-ton guano ship just struck by lightning. The
damage done in Australia by these pests must be serious.
Fortunately their habit of forming huge camping grounds
will make their extermination easier when steps are taken
tOjCombat them. They bid fair to be classed with the
rabbit pest, but without the rabbit’s redeeming feature.
Even the devil wouldn’t eat flying fox. A month
ago a deputation of South Queenslanders waited on the
alleged Minister of Agriculture requesting the Govt, to
take steps to wipe out the pest. It was suggested that,
as in I iji, disease might be introduced among the foxes
either by inoculation or contact in their breeding trees.
The pest has thus been wiped out in Fiji, so it is said. I
would like to hear something on this matter from a
Fijian correspondent. The Minister promised, etc., etc.
but the Queensland Govt, is a crew without sufficient
collective brains to run an up-to-date pie-stall on success-
ful lines ; and the flying fox is quite safe in its hands.

“ J.H.”*. In support of his contention
that Murray cod will not take the spoon bait,
bi ym ” says that he has fished in the Murray and
other rivers with artificial bait and has never
caught a Murray cod. That is no evidence that
such fish cannot be caught with spoon bait. The
people here (Deniliquin) fish with nothing else
and think that the spoon bait is the best with
which to catch Murray cod. I belong to a party
of five who go out fishing almost every Sunday
during the season and our last trip yielded 32
Murray cod weighing 1871bs. collectively, and
from 401bs. down to 21bs. individually—all caught
on the spoon bait.
* * * *
“ Burglar Bill ” : An instance of the utter
dislike of the growing Australian to get on the
land. In a “first-timers” prison 12 acres of cab-
bage garden outside the walls are cultivated by
prisoners who are within six months of their
discharge. The work isn’t heavy; they are out
all day in the free air, away from the choking
sensation produced by walls, and in my time they
were under the charge of a warder who was con-
sidered very lenient. But they weren’t taking
any. They had to be pulled away from the tailor-
ing and shoemaking, and the wood-heap. Pri-
soners due for the gang, although doing heavier
work, would be planted behind stacks of wood
when the chief overseer came recruiting for the
cabbage garden. The only exceptions would be
occasional bank clerks, who, thinking all other
avenues of employment closed, wanted to get
agricultural experience and strength cabbage-
growing. The majority hated it. They couldn’t
dissociate it from the old man’s bare paddock and
starving stock. To their minds it was a farm,
and they had seen more farms than enough.
* • * •
Be dreams. A few years ago a Botanyite,
named Dowling, went for a sail with one Bucket,
a darky. There was a big sea running at the
time, and the black man didn’t care to face it.
The white asked if he were frightened. Said
Bucket : “It’s not that, Mr. Dowling, but me
dream lars night me and you went out, and you
no come back.” It was as the darky dreamt.
Bucket was cast on the rocks, and had to tramp
six miles, through rough country, before he got
assistance. Dowling didn’t come back.
MISS NORA KERIN.
MISS DORA RIONOLD. Photos, by Talma, Sydney. MISS DOROTHY MARSDEN.
SOME MEMBERS OF GEO. MUSGROVES NEW DRAMATIC CO.
("MIDSUMMER NIGHT'S DREAM.")
Miss Norah Kerin and Miss Dora Rignold,
whose photos appear herewith, sport the ornate
wings of fairy monarchy in “ A Midsummer
Night’s Dream,” just now being played at Mel-
bourne Princess s. Miss Kerin is a blonde young
Queen Titania, whilst Miss Rignold (daughter of
our old friend “ Falstaff ’’ Rignold and niece
of Henry V.) appears as Oberon, her King.
Charming artists to-day, when their united ages
are little more than 40, they look likely to achieve
great things hereafter. In the meantime these
two bright young ladies can be congratulated on
the fact that their wings seem mere details in the
illusion. They are fairies down to the feet. These
same kind remarks mostly apply also to the third
lady—Miss Dorothy Marsden.
Somebody in Belgium has become in-
terested in Australia, and writes to the A.U.S.N
Co., Adelaide:—
Dear Sir,—l intend to go in Australia. I desire to
have some informations concerning the land and his
economical position (Kingdom animal, vegetable, and
mineral, industries and commerce)—Aspect of the Citys—
Please send me as soon as possible all these informations
so as the prices for the travel and the baggages. I desire
to have these books in French if it possible is. P.S.—The
morals of the inhabitants. Yours truly, ."

“ Burglar Bill ” writes :
Trial by jury received a severe handling during the
hearing of a case by Judge Gibson, at the last Sydney
Quarter Sessions. One Jones was charged with “ assault
with intent to rob,” and the jury, after a lengthy retire-
ment, returned with a verdict of guilty, with a strong
recommendation to mercy. The foreman, in answer to
Judge Gibson’s question as to the grounds for the
recommendation, replied that “a minority of the
jury are not satisfied with the strength of the evidence
as to the prisoner’s intent to steal. They con-
sider the evidence of the Grown on that point
weak.” His Honor refused to accept the recommenda-
tion on the ground that it was not reasonable, adding “ if
that is the recommendation you should find the accused
not guilty." After his Honor had again pointed out the
evidence on both sides as to intent, tne jury again retired,
and in two hours returned with a verdict of guilty. The'
inclusion of a recommendation to mercy in delayed
verdicts often tells a tale—a tale of a weak-minded
minority accepting a sop to convict the unfortunate
against their reason. The present jury system is defec-
tive. Why not box each juror separately, and thus stop
the bullying tactics of the bull-necked, unthinking, loud-
mouthed majority, whom the sentimental minority are
keeping from their butchers’ blocks and counters?

address must appear on MS. No liability is under-

no case will MS. be returned unless stamps (of any
State) accompany.

Charles Bright.
Few men of 70 keep a sprightlier step on the
pavement or a cheerier face to the world than did
Charles Bright, who died last week. He came to
Australia 42 years ago. First start in life was on
Melb. Argus— Edward Wilson, editor; Aspinall
leader of staff ; staff consisting of Willoughby and
Bright. The Argus connection lasted for some
few years, and was only broken when Bright
began to foregather with Freethought, as did his
colleague and senior, James Smith (still living),
with the spirits.
In Melbourne, in his best days, Bright was
pre-eminently one of the elect—as much at home
at a high shivoo of the University as in Mrs.
Hegarty’s bar, next door to the Argus. A bril-
liant after-dinner speaker; a debater with tongue
as well as pen. But he knew always when to
debate, and when to talk. A man needed
to be all a man to hold his own in those
times, for pressmen then and there held
their own, and with cordial welcome, on
all the heights of intellectuality and commercial
and social success. Most of that good life was in
some way associated with the Argus. The old office
was the intellectual centre of Australia. Judges
graduated there, the best of the old University
professors were regular contributors. Hearn (of
the ‘ Aryan Household ’) wrote one article a
week fer many years. Dr. Neild was a flaneur.
Ma-rcus Clarke dropped all the best of his whimsi-
calities into the box, and amid such men a» these
Charles Bright held up his head.
About middle life a hatred of humbug took
Bright in charge, or otherwise lie became strangely
obsessed. Then began the Freethought lectures.
An iron-grey man, he was at that date (1870-1-2)
square-jawed, level-headed, uncompromising.
There was no money in the lecturing business.
Bright was not built on the Charles Clark lines.
Perhaps, also, he was too much in earnest just
then. He got into the inky way again as leader-
writer on the Age, and worked with Windsor
for the best part of a decade. Had the
usual little difficulty with “David,” and walked
out. “David” would have whistled him back
within a month, but the Mutual Life of New
York had intervened. “Don’t know what you
want with me,” said Charles, “I’m one of the
worst business men in Melbourne. Couldn’t
canvas enough to keep my pipe going.” But the
’Murikan man knew, and the old journalist and
Freethought lecturer, was shortly installed,
with a few thousand dollars in furniture about
him and a comfortable screw. He ran their
advertising, and edited the monthly journal.
That connection lasted till recent days It was
the restful period of his life, which many old
friends hoped might be fruitful in good literary
work. But he had spent all power of that sort
on David’s treadmill and in fruitless centroversy.
Death came in the familiar grey of influenza,
and did not invite a particularly unwilling guest.
Not a few of Bright’s colleagues, those Trojans of
the fifties and sixties, survive him; Willoughby,
James Smith, R. S. Smyth, Haddon, Henrv
Gullet, Windsor and David, are amongst them.
The next crop in that field did not last half as
well. A curious fact—and easily established.

VICTORIAN MATTERS AND EVENTS.


The provident man was limited in his aspirations for a family’
while the improvident classes bred apace. If these latter were
catered for at the expense of the general community, the result
would be that the ratio between the provident and improvident
classes would become much worse. The provident would lie made
\to take a fresh step towards further providence. i

THE ROYAL BANK OF AUSTRALIA,
LIMITED.
HEAD OFFICE - - MELBOURNE, VICTORIA.
THIRTIETH REPORT presented to the Shareholders at the Ordinary General Meeting: on 29th April, 1903.
rrHE directors have pleasure in submitting: to shareholders the annexed balance-sheet and statement of accounts
J- for the half-year ending 31st March, duly audited and certified.
The net profits (including £5965 7s. 3d. brought from last half-year) amount to £13,344 18 11
which it is proposed to deal with as follows, viz.
To pay the usual dividend at the rate of 5 per cent, per annum (free of income tax).. £3,750 0 0
To transfer to the reserve fund 7,500 0 0
11,250 0 0
and to carry forward the balance .. .. .. .. .. .. • • .. .. .. £2,094 18 11
From the profits as above will have to be deducted the income tax now payable by the Bank under the new Act,
the assessment of which has not yet been made, but the tax will probably amount to about £460.
On 16th February the Bank entered into occupation of the building recently purchased and situated at the
corner of Collins and Elizabeth streets. The necessary alterations in the portion used by the Bank itself have been
completed, and further alterations to the upper portion of the building are in progress, which will considerably
improve its letting value. The new premises are found to be very commodious and convenient, and the improve-
ment in business consequent on the change has been most encouraging—the increase in every line of the balance-
sheet figures being highly satisfactory. ...... , . . „ „ ,
If shareholders will continue to assist the directors as in the past by introducing new business to the Bank
wherever possible the results cannot fail to be gratifying to all concerned.
By order of the Board,
H. T. WILSON, Manager.
BALANCE-SHEET OF THE ROYAL BANK OF AUSTRALIA LIMTED,
Dr. For the Half-year Ending 31st March, 1903. Cr.
CAPITAL AND LIABILITIES.

Democracy’s Opportunity.
Victorian M.L.A. Prendergast lately went up
Rodney way and in very short order organised the
navvies on the Waranga Basin works into a solid
body 500 strong—each with a vote for the State
House. This was a State matter only and was
mostly done for the benefit of the Kyabrama-
pootra, but the lesson taught should be promptly
digested—and acted on—by every Labor body and
party throughout the Commonwealth. For
the Federal elections are almost at hand
and at these elections Labor will have the
best chance it probably ever will have
to sweep the polls and get an absolute majority.
At the first Federal election the rolls used were
the existing State rolls—eliminating in all cases
the plural vote. But thousands of citizens were
not on those rolls —for a variety of reasons. The
Commonwealth rolls are now being made up, and
they include practically every adult. Be it
remembered that on the old State rolls Fatman’s
name was always present; mostly it was there
many times over, but it was always there in one
list or another ; it was the name of the lean man
that was so conspicuously absent. That is not
the case to-day. Be it also remembered that these
rolls may seldom again be quite so all-embracing
and representative as they are to-day. The Com-
monwealth can’t afford to make np fresh rolls
every year or so, and there will also be thousands
who will be too careless or too tired to get their
names placed on the list. The figures already
published as to the Federal franchise show
that there will be a large increase of voters
for the next as compared with the last
election ; and most of these are democrats, for, as
stated, Fatman’s name was there before. Omit-
ting, for the purposes of comparison, the female
vote, a few instances will show how the mere
enfranchisement of numberless nomads and
others has altered the balance of electoral
power : Hawthorn (Vic.) on the State rolls used
last election had 3880 voters; on the Common-
wealth rolls it has 5193. males. Jolimont
had 2,329, to-day it has 3176. Per contra, Mel-
bourne city, where the Fat Man’s plural vote was
strong, had 4474; to-day it has 2486. Melbourne
East had 5010; to-day it boasts but 3973, and so
in other cities. The plural voter disappeared last
election, but he still possessed the useful right to
vote where his vote would do most good ; to-day
he has lost that power and must vote on his resi-
dential qualification only; and, in addition to
that, the democratic vote, as shown by the returns
for such electorates as Richmond and Colling-
wood, has largely increased. Wherefore it is the
business of Labor to get itself to work organising
—and at once—all over the Commonwealth ; then,
instead of a balance-holding party of a score, it
may form an absolute majority of the House of
Representatives. And two out of the three new
Senators in every State should be Labor men. If
that be done, when Barton retires, the recon-
structed Ministry would begin with Kingston—
the rest of it would be mostly Turner and Labor.
A Country that Can’t Afford to ha
Inhabited.
Some extracts from the rather too lengthy letter
of “ A Working Woman ” on the small v. large
family question :
Some more or less reliable person tells me that the
population of Australia is decreasing [lt most assuredly
isn’t. —Ed. B.] Well, I don’t intend to increase it any
more. I have done my share by being the mother of six
children, and would sooner die than be the mother of
seven. Have been fined in the local court for not sending
them regularly to school, when they had no boots to go
in. Now what is the use of bringing children into the
world when you cannot even keep them in boots ? You
can’t complain of the working classes not populating the
country— they generally have too many children. It is
the wealthy people that ought to have the children,
because as soon as they are born they can be handed over
to a wet-nurse.
One woman writing to the Argus makes servants the
excuse. Don’t see it. Never had a servant, but would
not mind having one if someone would pay her wages.
Met a man the other day whose wife had just had her
ninth child. He was going from pub. to pub. “ wetting
the baby’s head.” Sympathised with him having so many
to feed and clothe, but he said he didn’t care if he had a
dozen—" bless their little hearts—let ’em all come ! ”
Asked him if his wife was of the same opinion. He
scratched his alleged head and admitted that “it was a
bit rough on the missus.”
I was a patient in the Women’s Hospital once. The
woman in the next bed had just had triplets and was a
bit fretful. The doctor, by way of consolation, said :
“ God, who has the ruling of these things, must have
known you were a good mother when he sends you three
babies, and” (glancing round the ward) “these other
mothers only one.” “Oh ! ” said she, “ I don’t think God
had much to do with it.”
No true woman, I think, would like to be without
children, and married couples that have no family seldom
live happily. But there is a medium, and it is nothing
short of a crime to bring children into the world if you
cannot afford to keep them properly clothed and
fed. One of my neighbors has just had her
fourth child (No. 3 cannot walk yet). “ Puppa,” she
said, “ loved to have a large family round him.” Now,
the said puppa goes off to the theatre on his own a
couple of times a week. Can’t take mumma on account
of the children. Mumma, in fact, goes nowhere—hardly
sees the light of day, and is worn and old-looking at 24.
What I want to know is what mumma is living for ? Will
the children repay her when she gets old ? I say No !
At one time mumma was a nice, natty little woman.
How can she be neat and tidy now ? She has no time to
think about such things, and by-and-bye puppa gets tired
of home and its constant worries—is the lover no longer
—has not been for some time —and spends his evenings
in the nearest billiard-room. But mumma struggles on
for the sake of her little ones, rears a large family some-
how, and then all she has got to do is to peg out. And
there isn’t so very much recompense in pegging out, is
there ?
Which all seems to boil down to these two propo-
sitions. (1) Some people don’t want children.
(2) Many people want them but can’t afford to
have them, for mining is precarious and often ill-
paid ; and agriculture is more precarious, and, for
the mere worker, always ill-paid ; and Govern-
ment relief work is transitory and ill-paid; and
life in the cities is mostly a precarious existence of
odd jobs, the Freetrade or low tariff craze for
importing things having prevented any great,
Drofitable and reliable industries arising. In
short, the theory is that Australia can’t afford to
be inhabited nowadays.
4
“ Leila ” on a theological point :
Talking over the baptism business, once asked our
Anglican vicar if he really thought a child was doomed !o
hell if it died unchristened. “ Well, you know,” he said,
“ we don’t like to say that any soul is lost ; but it would
start in eternity with a very serious handicap.” And
then my brother asked him : “ How many yards behind
scratch ? ” There has been a coolness ever since.
In Hyde Park, Sydney.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“What mean those pennons flying ?”
A stranger said to me.
“ Like colored dishcloths drying,
About that huge marquee ? ”
I said : “ Those flags elate that
You see—blue, red, and chrome—
Are meant to indicate that
The Harmy is at Home.”
Quoth he : “I used to fancy
This Park the People’s own,
The property of Clancy,
And Smith, and McElhone.
“ I also thought that no man,
Whate’er his creed or clan,
Or Anglican, or Roman,
Or Presbyterian,
“ Had any special claim here
To camp on public ground ;
Put, since to-day I came here,
My logic seems unsound.
“ And yet a small suggestion
I’d like to make to you,
For settling of this question—
If what you say is true.
“ There’s folk enough, I guess, go
Around who have a bent
For worshipping alfresco,
Or in a circus tent.
“ Then let each free Reserve go
To make some sect rejoice,
And that which has most nerve go
And take first pick and choice.
“ Where old Macquarie’s Lady
Sat in her chair of stone,
Are spaces green and shady
The Wesleyans might own.
“ The rest of the Domain should
Contain the other lot—
But still I think a chain should
Be stretched between each plot,
“To keep the fold from danger
And saint from saint aloof ”
I gazed upon the stranger,
And saw—a cloven hoof.

THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-st., Melbourne, is the largest buyer of
Opals, Sapphires, Pearls and other Australian
gems, and consignors will find it to their advantage
to send their parcels direct to him. The firm has
a reputation for straight-dealing, extending over a
period of 40 years, which is a sufficient guarantee
that they will be honorably treated. A trial par
cel, which will be followed by direct remittance
is solicited
Truly this world is full of strange things.
Joseph Chamberlain has said this in South Africa:
I leave this shore more convinced than ever that the
forces—the natural forces—that are drawing you toge-
ther are more potent than the evil influences that would
tend to separate you.
With these words as a text, Rudyard Kipling
prints a poem in the Times, a verse of which runs
as follows :
Where my fresh-turned furrows run, and the deep soil
glistens red,
I will repair the wrong that was done to the living and
the dead ;
Here where the senseless bullet fell, and the barren
shrapnel burst,
I will plant a tree, I will dig a well against the heat and
the thirst.
The war is over now, and even Kipling may
allow himself to write a little truth about it.
