POLITICAL
POINTS
“Two hundred thousand men went to
South Africa to fight for the Uitlanders’ votes.
How many had votes themselves ?”—Tom Mann.
Wanted an amendment of the Factories
Act to apply to all “charitable” institutions.
Sweating in the holy name of charity is becoming
an evil of giant proportions.
The rush of American settlers into Canada
suggests two things—the British immigration into
the Transvaal and the camel’s entrance into the
Arab’s tent. The British immigrants took the
Transvaal and the camel took the tent.
It looks as if Sydney’s North Shore Bridge
was about to die a natural death. It served
Parkes’ turn on many occasions, but that wily
politician knew better than to build a bridge, and
so lose the votes that he got by promising one.
The very latest Experience of Tommy
Bent’s comic-opera Vic. railways happened when
a number of men arrived at Warracknabeal
shoving Tommy Bent’s engine, which had run out
of water and had been compelled to give up busi-
-oess and leave the train 1-g miles back.
Tasmania will shortly have a small, quiet
centenary on hand. A hundred years ago this
year Lieutenant Bowen was sent by Governor
King from Sydney to settle on the island. The
party, consisted of the Lieutenant, Dr. Mount-
garrett, three soldiers, four female prisoners, and
six male ditto.
A Supreme Court Judge and Minister of
the Crown met in Sydney, the other day, and
the Mercadool cases accidentally cropped up.
Both were intimately acquainted with the matter
five or six years ago, but both now admitted they
didn’t ;know what happened or is likely to happen
to anyone implicated.
On the Westralian fields they are dis-
cussing the introduction of a worn-out miners’
fund, more or less on the lines of the “ Watson
fund,” at Bendigo. But that, good as far as it
goes, is not the ideal. The N.S.W. miners’ acci-
dent relief fund is better; but its weak point is
that it only makes provision for the miner who
goes under through accident, leaving to his misery
the equally deserving and equally helpless miner
whom “ miners’ consumption ” or lead-poisoning
has knocked out. The ideal fund will save both
lots from depending entirely upon their wives’
wash-tubs. !
THE NAVEE BUSINESS.
SIR JOHN FORREST: “Here! Come on! —stick your
head into this! ”
Mr. Bagnet, in Dickens’ “Bleak House,”
when asked for advice, used to turn to his wife
and say, ‘' Tell them what my opinion is, old
girl.” Attorney-General Gordon, of S.A., is
Premier Jenkins’ old girl. Gordon even comes
over to the Premiers’ Conference in Sydney,
bringing Jenkins’ opinions along with him.
S.A. Government has studied frugalities
to the extent of making one of its shorthand
clerks do the secretarial work of the big Railways
Royal Commission He has to attend to his
ordinary duties as well and to write up in his
spare time. The Ministry objected that outside
reporters charged too much for this kind of work..
The glowing accounts given in Melb.
Argus of the conversion of whilom horizon-fenced
sheep-runs in the Western District into aggrega-
tions of tenant dairy farms, should burn into the
sluggish Victorian brain like a hot poker the fact
that the second stage in that chronic general
paralysis of old nations, landlordism, has been
reached.
B >Aftfer all the vast sums of loan money that
have been poured out in the “permanent and
reproductive work ’’ of improving the entrances to
N.S.W. northern rivers, it is officially reported
than both the Clarence and Hastings bars are in a
worse condition than before the permanent and
reproductive expenditure commenced. More of
cur blessed assets gone astray!
Dry Dog Reid is going to deliver a series
of Foreign-trade addresses in Tasmania during
May. Doubtless he is going to explain at last
why he protected sugar, jam, biscuits, wine,
beer, cigars, tobacco, Foreign-trade newspapers,
and all the other things while he was Freetrade
Premier of N.S.W., and why he preached a creed
which he always found too dirty to practise.
The N.S.W. importing interest is getting
up a special magazine to plead for the freetrade
cause at the Federal elections. Mr. Spruce Myth
is to write an article (on the “Philosophy of
Freetrade”), Mr. M'Millan (on the “Texture of
Freetrade”), Mr. George Reid (on “the Humors
of Freetrade”). Mr. Robert Reid, it is under-
stood, will contribute a disquisition on “Freetrade
in Practice—with some Hints on Invoices.”
A correspondent from W. A. :
There is no Crown land within 50 miles of Perth except
small pieces on timber concessions among the Darling
Hills, and they cannot be got until expiration of timber
lease. There is plenty of private land in big estates to be
got at a fearfully high price. People in the east and
elsewhere should be warned against those rapacious land
sharks who have grabbed all the good spots about Perth,
and want to sell at a splendid profit.
The same curse, East and West—the landlord.
N.S.W. Arbitration Court is gradually
getting up with arrears ; the excellent plan of
encouraging employers and employed to come to
a voluntary agreement and then register it before
the court, has been responsible for many happy
settlements. Coal-miners, hairdressers, bread-
carters, restaurant employees, undertakers’
assistants, butchers and many other trades have
received substantial advantages from the court.
Westralian Worker lias fallen foul of
Labor member Johnson, and the language on both
sides has been free and numerous. Finally, John-
son, his vocabulary giving out, plaintively asked
the local Labor party for protection. There is a
big breach between two sections of the Labor
movement in Westralia, and it is probable that
the Labor party will be engaged in internecine
conflict when the general elections happen along.
Thomas Bent begins to weaken on his
policy of cursing the Victorian railways into
better order. When he took hold he was going
to put a prompt stop to late trains; also, the
speed was to be accelerated quicker than immedi-
ately. T’other day an inquisitive reporter asked
him when he thought of tackling this latter job.
For a moment he glared with his accustomed
vigor as though he would shift the universe in one
whirling blast —then he mopped his forehead
wearily, and said, “That’ll have to wait till Tait
comes.” Poor Tait! Pretty near all Sheol is
waiting till Tait comes.
Sydney merchants have proposed a
scheme of a “commercial board” to decide,
in a nice, quiet, friendly way, questions
that arise between fraudulent importers and
the customs. Why not extend the idea and
let the same board adjudicate in a nice, quiet
friendly way between the importer and his
embezzling clerk ?
Sydney daily papers are very hostile
to the proposal that industrial legislation
should be under Federal control. There is
the awful possibility to be faced of encoun-
tering, under such a system, a Minister
like C. C. Kingston, who might hale a
newspaper proprietary before the low,
common police court for keeping an insani-
tary- factory or for sweating. In the State
administration of laws, the “influence” of
the rich goes a long way; it doesn’t seem to
be able to move things a yard in Federal
administration.
How little Protection Australia has
got as yet, and how much it needs a
M‘Kinley tariff! A railway engine costs
about £4OOO ; of which £2400 goes for labor,
£l6OO for raw material. Very little of the
£2400 and just about none of the £l6OO is
spent here, most of the material being
imported ready made up; e.g., boiler plates,
copper plates, steel and iron sheets, bars and
angles, brass tubes, tyres, centres, axles,
Westinghouse brakes. Even the paint and
varnish are often imported. Australia
merely puts the thing together—and does
not always do that. Yet the raw material is all
at hand ; efficient Protection would mean the
employment of ten men for every one at present
engaged.
“ Jay bell ” : The late T. J. Byrnes at the
height of his political career failed to rally such
large and interested audiences as those which
have met Kingston at each of his stopping-places
throughout Queensland. Everywhere the interest
displayed in “ the large man from Adelaide” was
enormous. Kingston’s short, pithy sentences
soak well into the memory ; and remain there long
after the caretaker has turned the gas out and
growled at the lateness of the hour. Neither
Griffith nor M‘llwraith in his best days stormed
Queensland as Kingston has done.
Canada closes its financial year with a
surplus of £2,670,000, and pays off part of its
public debt. And the record for Australia’s last
financial year was an aggregate deficit of over
£1,000,000 after using about £2,000,000 of loan
money as revenue. The fact that in Australia
the States build their own railways doesn’t affect
the comparison, for all the railway-building money
was borrowed in addition. And the drought
doesn’t affect it, for this sort of thing was going
on long before there was any drought. How does
the high Protective system of Canada show here
against the half-hearted semi-revenue-tariffism of
Australia ?
THE KNOCKING AT THE DOOR.
“Many members of the Council feel that the House cannot long retain its old character under the new
Constitution.”— Melb. news item.
THE LEG. COUNCIL: “He may be only knocking, but it sounds dreadfully like an attempt to
break in.”
When it closes an hotel, Maoriland doesn’t
compensate the owner; but another claimant for
compensation has arisen—the local Council, which
fets the license fees. But that ought to be easy.
f, as is alleged, it makes hardly any difference to
the total consumption whether there are 40 pubs
or 50, and if by the closing of some pubs the
others’ trade is thus improved, the obviously fair
and proper thing to do is to put up these other
pubs’ license fees.
Legitimation of children born out of wed-
lock by the subsequent marriage of their parents,
a humane principle which has for ages found a
place in the laws of all Christendom except
England, has at length found its way in a slightly
maimed form on to the Victorian statute-book.
Persons interested, whose children were bom be-
fore the Act was passed, will do well to note that
they have only six months in which to take
advantage of its provisions.
Now that the Victorian Government has
ordered the railway employees to sever their con-
nection with the Trades Hall, and the employees
have declined to do so, the Victorian Government
has a large problem before it. It can’t make the
men leave the Trades Hall; and it can’t sack the
whole body of railway men for not doing so ; and
it can’t leave things alone without great loss of
dignity. Probably this is another problem that
will be left over till new Commissioner Tait
arrives.
The news from the East is that Japan feels
very sore over the recent Australian and American
alien-exclusion laws. “It has been suggested to
retaliate by shutting out of Japan citizens of those
countries which will not admit the Japanese.”
Well, if it will make him feel any better the Jap
ought to be told that a little thing like that won’t
wound our precious dignity a bit. What Japan
does with Japan is Japan’s own business, not
Australia’s; what Australia does with Australia
is Australia’s own business, not Japan’s.
Stephen Barker proposes to petition
against Robert Reid’s election as an alleged
Senator. The law says clearly that no man,
while sitting in a State Parliament, is eligible for
election to the Commonwealth Legislature. That
law, if honestly administered, clearly rules out
Robert Reid who was elected, and Peacock who
came second. Barker, who icas eligible, came
third, so Barker naturally claims the seat as the
only qualified candidate, and it is difficult to see
any earthly reason why he shouldn’t have it.
Justice Cohen (N.S.W.) objected to take
a case the other day on the ground that he
was interested as a trustee on one of the
sides. He pointed out that on one occa-
sion the Lord Chancellor gave a decision in
an estate of which he was, unknowingly, a
trustee, and on appeal the House of Lords ruled
the judgment could not stand. In this connection,
should guinea-pig Cabinet Ministers rise from the
directors’ table of a private insurance co. or pri-
vate banking co. and take part in Parliament in
determining whether there is to be a State Bank
or State Insurance Department ?
Premier See, of N.S.W., after dinner
(also, by the way, after Waddell)
The man who didn’t borrow when he had plenty of
assets to cover the borrowing was not a commercial man
to his way of thinking.
Depends. If John pawned a ship to back a horse
that didn’t win the Cup, he wouldn’t be anything
of a commercial man to speak of. Or even if John
pawned a ship to pay an instalment on another ship,
and if he didn t make those ships work off the
loan, he would presently find himself without any
ship at all. Anyhow, John’s theory (for political
consumption only) that a good commercial man is
known by the encumbrances he keeps, and that he
is open to suspicion if his assets haven’t a bill of
sale oyer them, is not the theory that he regulates
his private business by.
The importer seems to be the most
unanimous and unscrupulous lie, swindle, fraud
and steal in all Australasia. The Shoddy party
in the Representatives urged that goods coming,
say, from Manchester via London should pay
duty only on their value at Manchester —not on
their value when leaving London with the cost of
freight to London and re packing there added to
the original price. On this point the importer
promptly proceeded to be a liar and a swindler as
usual. In the end he became so outrageous that he
bega,n to claim that out of £6O, value of goods when
leaving London, £4O should be deducted for the
cost of bringing the goods from, say, Manchester to
London, and that the value of the goods on leaving
Manchester (the value on which duty was to be
paid) was only £2O. Then Kingston’s too-patient
Department began to move, and some more emi-
nently respectable and Christian firms are likely
to undergo “persecution.”
The tale of a lost politician :
Outtrim, ex-M.L.A. for Maryborough (Vic.), after
gorging on the loaves and fishes for about 17 years in
Parliament, had the hardest kind of luck in the end.
When there was talk of attacking the Peacock Government
two men were mentioned as likely to lead the attack;
Irvine and Outtrim. Now Outtrim had held office in
three Governments, but never as Premier, and probably
wishing to crown his career with that office, he refused
to serve under Irvine. But he had something up his
sleeve. Outtrim’s idea, it is said, was, when Irvine had
got his vote of want of confidence under way, to move an
amendment which would draw votes from both sides and
be carried. Then he had a reasonable hope of being sent
for and obtaining the coveted Premiership, and of being
called upon to act the part of the Strong Man of Politics
since usurped by Irvine. But anticmating (reasonably
enough) a long debate he left the chamber
to take a look down the street, and when he came back
the debate was over and his chance was gone. Irvine
carried his motion, was sent for, and the rest is known.
Had Outtrim chosen to serve under Irvine, in the attack
on Peacock, he would have received a portfolio, sub-
scribed to the Reform policy, become a shining light of
Kyabramapootraism, and have won his seat for Mary-
borough triumphantly. But, as things were, he had, of
course, to oppose the Irvine policy, and he therefore lost
his seat to new man Field, and with the revision of
electorates consequent on the reduction of members it is
doubtful if he will ever recover it. And now he practices
auctioneering again at Maryborough, and meditates, on
the ingratitude of the railway station and other institu-
tions which he helped to rear in Maryborough with public
money.
THE UNBORN.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Ah God ! for those who are coming-,
The millions who yet must be !
Thine Earth like a hive has been humming
So long with anxiety.
Such a lot of confusion and trouble,
Thousands so poor and unfed . . .
They are coming to starve on the stubble
Where hosts of the ages are dead !
R. CRAWFORD.

The old whaler Chance, made historical
by Bullen’s “Cruise of the Cachalot,” and which
has been lying at the Bluff (Maoriland) for many
years, is to be broken up. The old ruin is half
covered with sand, and has long been very much
in the road.

VERSUS
VITADATIO
Another Victory for the Great Herbal Remedy.
READ MR. RUDDOCK’S TESTIMONY,
192 Elizabeth-street, North Richmond,
Melbourne, April 22nd, 1901.
Mr. S. A. PALMER.
Dear Sir,—lt affords me very great pleasure to let you
know what Webber’s VITADATIO has done for me. Two
years ago last January I was brought home to my wife
very ill; a very peculiar feeling came over me, which
caused me to become quite helpless. My wife sent for a
doctor ;he ordered me to be painted with iodine. This
was done for seven weeks, and he (the doctor) then
ordered me to the Alfred Hospital. After being there
for two months, the doctor sent me home, stating that I
was too weak to go under an operation, and said that I
must go for a trip in the country. I did so, and returned
home slightly better; but after a few days. I became
much worse with pneumonia, and after suffering six
months between life and death it was decided that I
should go to the Sanatorium for Consumptives at Echuca.
My wife interviewed the' lady secretary of the Sanatorium,
stationed, at Kesv, and, after examination by the doctors
they pronounced me a fit patient for that institution, as
I was full of consumption, but that I could not get in
for a fortnight, as there would not be a vacant bed till
then. At this time, Mr. Campbell, grocer and wood' and
coal merchant of Vere and Nicholson-streets, Abbotsford,
and now of Fairfield, urged me very strongly to try VITA-
DATIO, which I did. The first bottle upset me very
much, but by the time I had finished the second bottle I
was gat out of bed, and on taking the third bottle
I felt so improved that I decided not to go to the Echuca
Sanatorium. My wife took a message to the Secretary
to that effect. I continued with VITADATIO, and after
taking nine bottles I was able to go to work, so I called on
Mr. Wall bridge, .carrier, Lincoln-street, North Richmond
(my last employer) and started at once, and have been
there ever since. Ido very heavy work delivering coal.
I feel strong and healthy, and can truthfully say that
VITADATIO has saved my life, and the least I can do is
to hand you this testimonial. It is one year and eight
months since I took the last bottle of VITADATIO. I
have livqd in this locality for many years and am well
known. ' l will give further information to anyone calling
at my above address as regards my illness and cure by
VITADATIO, and you can refer to any of the under-
mentioned names, who are quite prepared to substantiate
my statement.—Yours faithfully,
THOMAS O. RUDDOCK.
E. Ruddock, 28 St. Phillip-street, Abbotsford.
A. Starr, Varian and Hunter-streets, Abbotsford.
Samuel Eadley Hambleton, Bootmaker, 382 Smith-street,
Collingwood.
Alex. C. Kennedy, Family Grocer (45 years’ standing),
390 Drummond-street, Carlton.
J. Campbell, Baatins-street, near Fairfield Station.
Tiios. Wallbridge, 38 Lincoln-street, Richmond.
For further particulars,
■S. A. PALMER,
184: Pitt Street, Sydney.

Joseph Kirton, who gives up his thankless
honorary job in the Vic. Iceberg Ministry to earn
a few pounds a week as chairman of the Ballarat
Water Commission, is not likely to remain in Vic.
politics much longer. Joe entered Parliament in
the same year as Peacock (1889), and was at first
supposed to be the bigger man of the two; but
Peacock became a Minister in his second year in
Eolitics, and out of his 14 years in the Assembly
e spent nine in office, while Kirton was left in
the cold until, a very few months back, Irvine took
him in as a legislative makeweight without salary.
He hasn’t found politics profitable, still he comes
away with the title of “Hon.” and a free railway
pass.

Then compare the values. You will think
differently.

DO YOU WISH TO
BELIEVE IT ? . . .
Miss
Marion
W

Y
T
E
¥
5 Campbell Street, Darlinghurst,
Sydney, November 13th, 1902.
Dear Sirs Suffering constantly
from indigestion and rheumatism,
and hearing a good deal about the merits of
Scott’s Emulsion, I tested it. Before I had
finished the first bottle I felt considerably
better, and continuing the treatment for a
while both indigestion and rheumatism left
me entirely; it has undoubtedly improved
my general health as well. Faithfully yours,
(Signed) Miss Marion Whyte.
Messrs. Scott & Bowne, Ltd.,
483 Kent Street, Sydney.
DO YOU WISH TO BELIEVE
that there is relief and cure for indigestion
and rheumatism ? It is to be supposed that
you do, if you suffer from indigestion or
rheumatism. Or if any member of your
family is afflicted with these misfortunes. It
is perfectly safe to assume that you do wish
to believe it —believe, that is—that for
rheumatism and indigestion Scott’s Emulsion
is a cure. Has not one who has seen the cure
performed just proved it to you? It is nob
required, of course, to explain what rheu-

HUMAN POISONS
Continuously being made in each of us! Their fatal nature!
Why they are made! The diseases they cause! How to
render them harmless! Their antidote!
Life is maintained by a process of waste and renewal. It is as essential to life that the tissues of
the body should continuously die as it is that they should be continuously renewed. To the un-
initiated this may sound strange, but any doctor or scientific man will tell you that the tissues of the
body are in a constant state of decay, and that the decayed or dead matter must be removed, or it will
accumulate and cause disease or death.
THE KIDNEYS AS CLEANSERS.
It is the function of the kidneys to remove, in the form of uric acid and urea, a large proportion
of the waste matter caused by the decay of the tissues. It is the failure of the kidneys to efficiently
remove uric acid and urea which causes people to suffer from Rheumatism, Gout, Neuralgia,
Lumbago, Backache, Sciatica, Anaemia, Gravel, Stone and Bladder Troubles. The
most common indication that the kidneys are not doing their work properly is
PAIN IN THE BACK.
A much surer test as to whether the kidneys are in thorough working order is an examination of the
urine. If some urine passed the first thing in the morning is placed in a bottle or covered glass, and
is allowed to stand for twenty-four hours, and it is then cloudy, or there is a brick-dust like sediment in
it, or if particles float about in it, then you may be sure that the action of the kidneys is impeded by
weakness or disease, and no time must be lost in restoring them to effective vigour, or uric acid
poisoning in some of its many forms will be the result. When you suffer from Rheumatism, Gout,
Neuralgia, Lumbago, Backache, Sciatica, Anaemia, Gravel, Stone or Catarrh of the Bladder, you are
suffering from
URIC ACID POISONING.
But there is no reason why you should continue to suffer from any of these complaints. It is only
necessary to restore the kidneys to health, and all uric acid and other urinary poisons will be duly
expelled from the system in a natural manner. Since the discovery was made (about thirty years ago)
of the remarkable power possessed by
Warner’s Safe Cure
to cure all disorders of the kidneys and to restore to vigorous health those vital organs, any person
who now suffers from any form of disease caused by failure of the kidneys to efficiently perform their
office, suffers needlessly, because a course of Warner’s Safe Cure will speedily restore him or her to full
blooded health. Warner’s Safe Cure is an antidote to all poisons formed in the human body, owing
to incapable action of the kidneys or liver.
Mr. Murray L. Allen, of 4 Marsden Terrace, Clifford-street, Goulburn, writing on the last day of
the old year, says :—“I have had a bad attack of inflammation of the kidneys and calculus (stone),
both of which yielded to the power of Warner’s Safe Cure.”
Remember that pains in the back, painful passing of urine, inflammation of the bladder, torpid
liver, cloudy urine, pains in the back of the head and neck, rheumatic pains, swellings of the body,
all are Nature’s means of indicating to you that your kidneys are not able to do their work properly;
and also remember that Warner’s Safe Cure will speedily and permanently restore your lost health.
Warner’s Safe Pills are a gentle cathartic, and assist in accelerating the cure.
A treatise containing full particulars of Warner’s Safe Cure and the diseases it cures will be sent
post free on application to the Australasian Branch, H. H. Warner and Co. Ltd., Melbourne.

The Police Paralysis.
In perfunctory, slipshod N.S.W., the land where
things slide, the State has almost given up pro-
fessing, so far at least as large centres of popula-
tion are concerned, to keep order and provide for
public safety. Sydney, for example, is carefully
mapped out in constabulary beats, each one repre-
senting about the largest possible area of ground
over which a solitary “cop” can even profess to
keep guard. But the force is shamefully under-
manned, and what force there is has to spend so
much of its time making up jury lists and elec-
toral rolls, acting as dancing dervish at the door
of the State Governor or some other functionary,
killing dogs, and “inspecting” this, that or the
other, that one policeman has often three beats
to look after—perhaps 20 miles of streets and lanes
—and he can’t inspect the whole of his district
even once in a night. The writer, who lives in a
main street in a rather thinly-populated suburb,
has only seen his local policeman three times in 18
months, and probably many others are similarly
situated. A policeman every half-year isn’t
‘ ‘ police protection ” —not by a long way. As a
result a great part of Sydney now hires private
watchmen to do the duty that the police can’t do.
The writer’s share of a private watchman costs
him £6 a year, and he is only one case in many.
Very soon it seems probable that the private
watchmen of Sydney will be a more formidable
force than the police—in other words, that a
private police force will grow up which will,
to a great extent, take over the job of the
overworked, under-manned, and generally para-
lysed State police force, and it seems a toss up, in
view of the habit of the State judicial func-
tionaries in the lower courts of fining murderous
ruffians in a small sum, if the privately-owned
petty judiciary doesn’t rise some day as the
natural complement of the privately-owned police
force. If the State Government isn’t ashamed of
haying brought an allegedly civilised city to this
pass, it must be because it is so ignorant of
civilisation that it doesn’t know the article, or the
absence of the article, when it sees it.

“Euroa”: Met a young lady of my
acquaintance, t’other day, with her baby in her
arms. I stopped and asked how he was. “Oh !
he’s so bad,” she said; “the doctor lanced three
teeth out of his gums, and he’s not eight months
old yet. He was so bad last night, and the doctor
said he couldn’t live, so I had to rush with him
this morning and have his photo, taken.’’ The
baby ist still alive, and I’m wondering if she
regretted the expense.
At Poverty Point.
“ Ludo ” : The only Victorian country districts where
there is any money at present are Gippsland and the
Western district, and these have a monopoly just now of
travelling theatrical companies. Gippsland is a region
of bad roads and big distances, so the bulk of the
mummers drift westwards. There are so many of them,
and they travel so close on each other’s heels that, on the
whole, they are having a hard time. Some of them travel
by train, and get out to adjacent villages per cab or
brake ; but a lot have their own shaky turnouts, in which
the women folk doss at night, while tbe men sleep in the
anteroom of the hall. Without exception they denounce
the company that has preceded them as consisting of
shady, unprincipled rogues who should be in gaol for
obtaining money under false pretences; and they are
right. They all placard their show as being “free from
the faintest taint of vulgarity,” or as “ superior and re-
fined entertainments,” which are a set of breezy, healthy
lies. llow on earth is it that a travelling company can’t
fix up a night’s entertainment without references to
someone’s torn pants, or somebody sitting on a tack, or
twins, or such wretched pot-house drivel. It isn’t witty,
and it isn’t obscene ; it’s simply inane, soul-depressing
vulgarity. There is a big opening in the Vic. Western
district for a bright, clean, and wholesome variety co.,
which could play all the year round between Geelong and
Portland. Nearly every village has a handy little hall,
and a £lO or £l2 house could be generally depended on.
The first trip would be an up-hill fight, as the company
would have to meet the unsavory record of former
troupes ; but once it established a reputation its future
would be assured.
“ Cockaigne ” : London Gaiety is about to be pulled
down. The last musical comedy is running there. It is
called “ The Linkman,”'and is a musical hash of “The
Forty Thieves,” “ Little Jack Sheppard,” “ Monte Cristo,”
“ Ruy Bias,” “ Faust-up-to-Date,” “ Carmen-up-to-Data,”
“ Cinder-Ellen,” and sundries.
The late Rev. Chas. Clarke memorised his lecture,
“ Westminster Abbey,” thuslv, according to his own
account: —“ I went daily to the abbey and took in details
re the tombs of bygone heroes resting there. Then I dug
up their stories in the libraries and top-dressed with
elaborate and ornate language, after which I learnt tbe
day’s work parrotwise. Each day added more, until I’d
bagged the lot, and I have been letting it loose on the
public ever since with advantage to all parties.”
“ Melb.” : The most distinguished guest received by
the deceased Castella at St. Hubert’s was Sarah Bern-
hardt. She shot a Bengal tiger there—at least the event
was probably so described afterwards, but in reality she
popped off a pea rifle at a native bear on a gum tree.
‘ ‘ H.” : Shakspeare is supposed to have been inspired
to the composition of “ Midsummer Night’s Dream” by
seeing the masque given by Leicester to Queen Elizabeth,
at Kenilworth. Ambroise Thomas composed an absurd
French opera entitled “ Midsummer Night’s Dream ” re-
presenting Queen Elizabeth in love with Shakspeare.
Miss Nora Kerin, who takes the part of Titania in “ A
Midsummer Night’s Dream,” in Melbourne, is, like most
actresses (why ?), a profound disbeliever in woman’s
suffrage. She works hard at her profession, but is of
opinion that women in general should do no work,
except act as man’s guardian angels, and live noble,
womanly lives. Yet the manly Rosalind is her favorite
Shakspearian role. Miss Kerin’s alleged ignorance of
Australia is rather behind the times. She confesses to a
Melbourne interviewer that she even brought a store of
sewing cottons with her in case she should not be able to
get them here.
Titheradge and Henrietta Watson were engaged by the
Stage Society (London) to play the leading parts in
Ibsen’s play, “ When the Dead Awaken.” The drama
was produced on a Sunday evening, arid repeated at a
matinee next day, with the usual Ibsenic result. Accord-
ing to the furious Believers, it was an enthralling work
of luminous mystery and inspired vagueness, whilst de-
graded scoffers —like Carados of the Referee—found the
work of the Norwegian genius to be “ not only distress-
ingly obscure, but deplorably mean as well.”
Dear Bulletin.—The best that can be said for the Hill-
Adams creation, “ Tapu,” is that it is a gallant attempt
to idealise a hopeless subject. After seeing the piece, I
am convinced that the stage has no use for the Maori.
The Maori is an admirable creature in books ; and a nice
neighbor a long way off. He used to be a valiant animal
in battle, and a “ jolly good fellow ” at a cannibal feast.
But on the stage, he and his whares, his pahs, his kaingas,
mats, meres, moko, utu, tapu, and all the rest of it, aren’t
worth 4d. as operatic assets.—A. Spence.
“ Jongleur ” reflects thus :—History repeats itself. But
that is because it is growing old and garrulous, and is
losing the gift of originality. Poor Kate Vaughan, the
once worshipped skirt-dancer, is dead, and London, at
least, experienced something very like public mourn-
ing. Many centuries ago there died at Rome a most
beautiful woman, whose name was Pyrrha. She had
mixed in the highest and swiftest circles, but her move-
ments were of a kicking character. Had she lived in our
day, Society leaders would have alluded to her freezingly
as “ a person.” But she lived in the days of the Caesars,
and was virtually mistress of the world. When a Csesar
died it was fairly easy to find a successor. But
when Pyrrha died, the people felt as though the sun-
shine had gone out of their lives—“ and there came
a mist and a blinding rain, and the world was never
the same again.” And so it came to pass that poor,
dead Pyrrha was honored with a funeral such as no
Csesar ever had, and on her tomb the people inscribed
the simple but eloquent words “Saltavit; placuit” —
“She danced and delighted.” The woman who delights
us by her graceful evolutions on the stage may do us
more real good after all than the sour-faced frump who
thrust a ridiculous tract into our hands and bids us
“ Beware lest we are cut off in the height of our
iniquity i"
When “ Henry V.” was produced in Berlin, last
February, the irrepressible Kaiser Bill gave “ hints and
instructions regarding the staging of the play.” It is not
stated whether he made any improvement in the other
Bill’s text.
At last we are improving on the melodrama story of
the young man who gets wrongfully convicted of murder
at 9 o’clock, and escapes from penal servitude in the 3rd
act. An up-to-date melodrama introduces the hero in
the Ist act as an innocent convict, out on the loose with
a good-hearted burglar. This arrangement permits of
much more sorrow for the hero than he could possibly
suffer under the old system, which gave him a lot of hap-
piness at the outset. He starts with “ the bloodhounds
of the law ”on his track. Then the villain comes along
and begins to plot against him, so that he gets convicted
of a new murder, and has two sets of bloodhounds on his
track.
Somebody remarks that the Early Christians in “Sign
of the Cross ” sing at the top of their voices for the pur-
pose of escaping observation.
A number of new dramas adapted from Charles
Dickens’ novels are coming on for production in London.
Fresh versions of “The Old Curiosity Shop,” “Bleak
House ” and “ Oliver Twist ” have been arranged. Given
a well-chosen cast the best play from Dickens is “ Nicho-
las Nickleby,” wherein the schoolroom scene offers lots of
opportunities for smart stage management.
In Britain they are bound to swallow Barrett as “The
Silver King,” for he holds the local rights in the famous
melodrama. Hence the truthfulness of the local critic,
who remarks that “ While Mr. Wilson Barrett lives to
impersonate Wilfred Denver the piece will never lack
supporters.” Australia remembers the “ Silver King” as
a play which was seen to least advantage when Barrett
filled the name part.
Jingo dramatists, who twine their dull stories around a
tableau representing the invariable triumph of the British
Flag, might take a hint from the latest Parisian melo-
drama, “Les Derni6res Cartouches.” The author has
arranged his battle-scene so as to realise De Neuville's
famous picture—drawn from the alleged incident of The
Last Cartridges at the Battle of Sedan. Then the scene
goes on to show the hoisting of the white flq,g and the
surrender of the French. Thus far no English drama has
allowed the Enemy to win a battle. It is an article of
the Bulldog Creed that the enemy never did win a battle.
The directors of London’s boss music-hall, the Empire
Palace, Ltd., declared a dividend of 40 per cent, for year
1902. All the same, the principal theatrical managers in
the orthodox line of business are making good money.
Irving is said to be recovering his lost financial position,
and the plays in which Ellen Terry seemed to share his
popularity have not suffered through her retirement
from the co. Courtneidge sa.vs that Irving is a finer
actor than ever he was in his best characters, and a
greater theatrical personage by virtue of his high
mindedness and generosity. When he was performing at
Manchester, last year, he spoke publicly in glowing terms
of the “ Midsummer Night’s Dream ” production which
Courtneidge was running in opposition to his (Irving’s)
show.


An Ex-Mayor’s Opinion.
The opinion of a man is often useful, especially
when the man is well-known to be a master of the
subject upon which he speaks; then his state-
ments are more than mere opinions, they are
absolute facts. Such for instance is the statement
recently made by Mr. P. A. Phillips, ex-Mayor
of Auckland, who, after spending many useful
years in publio service in Maoriland, has now
come to permanently reside at Woollahra, Sydney.
His opinion of Dr. Morse’s Indian Root Pills is
one founded upon the most substantial grounds,
for he himself has personally tested them, and
found them, to use his own words, of “inestimable
value.” _“ I suffered,” he says “from flatulence,
dyspepsia, and indigestion, together with their
usual train of horrors. In my search for a
remedy, I tried every known medicine, and I
must have taken a cart-load of drugs and patent
medicines. All of these were useless. None
removed the cause of my trouble, and it was only
when at last I took Dr. Morse’s Indian Root
Pills that I found relief and a permanent cure
for my ailments.” Dr. Morse’s Indian Root
Pills are a perfect blood purifier, and a positive
cure for biliousness, indigestion, constipation,
headaches, sallow complexion, liver and kidney
troubles, piles, pimples and blotches, and for
female ailments. Put up in amber 'bottles and
the full name blown thereon.*

SPORTING NOTIONS.
A forefront Victorian jockey attributes unusual
number of falls during four days of A. J.O. Autumn meet
to persistent fighting of lightweight riders for places on
the rails; they go for these blind-eyed, and without
remotest thought of possibilities in the way of aocidents.

• • • •
■ The late F. F. Dakin, who followed that youth-
ful old sport, E. T. Barnard, as handicapper for the
V.R.0., was a frequent target for complaint when he first
took over the job. But Dakin outlived the animus of his
critics. He knew his business about as well as any man
on the Australian turf, and the few seeming blunders
which he committed proved to be quite harmless, as a
rule. A knowledge of individual sharpers and a keen eye
for their tactics are, of course, as essential to a handi-
capper’s success as the mere study of “ form,” in the
running and of book results. Dakin showed all the
necessary vigilance as regards what the horse-reporter
calls “a rod in pickle.” Also, he discriminated intelligently
between probable stayers and probable non-stayers. As
a tipster on the day of the race, the late P.F. was not
very deadly. A handicapper naturally gives lots of the
Candidates a chance, and is sceptical about good things.
The sanguine outside punter and the enthusiastic stable
connection are the only positivists in turf matters. Their
hopes mould their predictions. Men who deal with gee-
gee performances as a matter of business are disposed to
back anything rather than something—shake up the
names of the starters in a hat and back the one that
comes first to hand. The writer once got a successful
20 to 1 "tip” for the Caulfield Oup from J. F. Miller,
who publishes the Australian Racing Chronicle. It
came from information received in Miller’s domestic circle
by means of the dough-nut system. This system is free
from all suspicion of prejudice, or unconscious bias. The
name of each probable starter is written on a small piece
of paper, the paper is concealed in a lump of dough, and
all the dough-nuts are baked in a pan. The first one that
breaks open and reveals its paper is the “ tip.”
* • • *
A.J.C. stipendiary stewards, who were to have
abolished stiff running and crooked turf work generally,
have, beyond an occasional abortive inquiry, failed to
give the slightest indication of their existence, and the
things they were created to stop are as rampant as ever.
Their solitary move was when Chippy Norton, after
failing badly in Flying Handicap, Easter Monday, over
his favorite distance, 6 furlongs, came out on Wednesday
and mopped up Mile Welter, making rest of field appear
hacks. Someone picked up a fortune through the win.
A big commission was worked in Leger and paddock
simultaneously—the nag being backed from long prices
down to fives. Stewards “inquired exhaustively” and
expressed themselves “satisfied that there was something
wrong, but evidence wasn’t sufficiently clear to justify
punishment of the parties concerned.” So they might
just as well never have moved at all.

Another scheme for manipulating scales was
disclosed on Newcastle course on Monday, and method
proved exactly on same lines as discoveries at Moorefield
and Warwick Farm, with the difference that while at two
places last named there were indications that the thing
hadn’t been worked for a long time—perhaps years—a
slabby-formed youth, provisioned for the day and thickly
coated with dirt and cobwebs, was caught red-handed
at Newcastle. Beyond the statement that he was “ a
man with a black moustache a bookmaker, perhaps—
nothing could be squeezed from the lathy one regarding
the identity of his employer, who placed him in position
previous midnight, with a stock of fruit and confec-
tionery. Holes in the floor were evidently of ancient
date, but 291 b. of lead, which the youth says he had to
attach to the scales and remove at understood signals,
was newly cast.

In All-aged Stakes (one mile), at Rand wick,
last week, Emir redeemed his reputation as the best two-
vear-old this season, which his running in the Cham-
pagne Stakes had made doubtful. Newmarket Handicap
winner Ohantress lined up first choice. Sir Leonard was
next, and Emir only backed half-heartedly. He won
easily in particularly fast time—only a second behind
Australian record—l.3BJ. Emir will be hot during
winter for V.R.C. Derby, and already he has been backed
for that and Oup double. One Mo., at settling Thursday,
is said to have laid £lOOO to £4O to some juggins—won-
derful what a big crop of them there is in the racing
game. Emir, by Wallace from Emmie, was bred at St.
Albans, and, at that stud’s dispersal, went with his dam
to W. and F. Moses, of Combadello (N.S.W.). At last
year’s yearling sales he was bought by Mr. E. E. D.
Clarke, of Victoria, for 1000 gs., best part of which came
back when the colt appropriated V.R.C. Ascot Vale
Stakes.
• * • *
A. J.O. Plate (3m.), Saturday, demonstrated
that Bewitcher is a real good colt. The Victory,
upon whom odds of 7 to 2 were laid, just struggled home
barely a length best, with Bewitcher catching him every
stride. Only two named and Cyanide started, but latter
had enough four furlongs from home. Time (sm. 25sec.)
beat the limit fixed by 20sec., and is a record for this
event, mainly through the pace set by The Victory from
barrier rise to judge’s box. Success of Victorian reps, in
A. J.O. Plate is remarkable —it went their way in ’Bl and
’B4-5, and every year from ’B9 to ’97, and 1900 to present
year inclusive.
• • • •
Fatuousness of general public trying to pick
Winners when trainers are unable to size up relative
merits of their charges was well exemplified at A.J.C.
Autumn meeting. Bassini and Kilfera—stable chums
and by the same sire—started in two-year-old Maiden
Stakes, and while latter was a scorching favorite at 2 to 1,
tens were easily obtainable about former, yet Bassini
won easily, with Kilfera third. Another pair (Gratia and
Ilderim), from one stable, figured in Rous Handicap.
Gratia was well backed, but there was hardly a shilling
on Ilderim (Mo. yelped 100 to 4 right to the death). Yet
Ilderim won, while Gratia failed to make even a decent
showing.
* * • •
Cumberland Stakes (2m.) was contested by five.
Abundance (stale looking) was, despite close Leger finish,
made a warm first choice at 6 to 4. Great Scot, The
Victory, and Patronage, were in about equal demand (4
to I>—Patronage half a point longer than the others.
Pace was comparatively slow till a mile had been covered,
then Abundance went up to Great Scot and things
hummed. Locked together the two swept into the
straight clear of The Victory and Cyanide. Great Scot
forged to the front, while punishment caused Abundance
to swerve, and the money seemed Great Scot’s until The
Victory, coming down the centre* while Patronage,
Abundance, and Cyanide had gone towards the judge’s
box, finished with a paralysing rush and won handily.
Abundance and Cyanide were done to a turn—they
knocked up completely in the last furlong. The Victory’s
was the third successive success of Victorian reps, that
day.
• • • •

had three winning mounts, while Frank Fielder, Phillips,
McOrimmon, A. Richardson, and Hickey credited them-
selves with two each.

Spite of his failure previous Monday, F.J.A.
(initials generally supposed to be those of an ex-member,
now in London, of Williamson’s Comic Opera Co.) started
hot for Easter Stakes, and easily settled 11 others, in-
cluding Champagne Stakes winner, Kilfera, and Duke of
Grafton. F.J.A. belongs to Rupert Clarke, and was bred
at St. Albans ; he cost present owner llOgs. at yearling
sales. F.J.A. is by Wallace from the Robinson Crusoe
mare La Tosca, full sister to Wakeful’s dam. Though a
little ’un, he is a beauty, and prior to latest, which was
his first set weight success, had annexed several handi-
caps.

that recent four days’ meet at Kandwick attracted record
attendances—gate was £l7OO ahead of anything ever
previously known.

Notable thing of A.J.C. Autumn week was

anxious to own a nag up to winning Viceroy’s Cup.

• • * •
Big Sydney Ikey concern—Tattersall’s—con-
tinues to flourish. In 10 years it has paid £14,000 off
a big mortgage, leaving £19,000 still due. Club’s invest-
ments aggregate £4500 (all in N.S.W. funded stock), and
bank balance is £l5OO.
• * • •
The latest aspirant to champion honors is the
Victorian-owned trotter Almont, who trotted two miles
in 4.36 2-5, at Epsom (Vic.), on Saturday, 11th. Almont
is closely related to the N.S.W. champion Fritz, being by
Rothschild-Prunella. The dam Prunella is full sister to
Fraulein, dam of Fritz. A cable has been sent to Christ-
church (M.L.) challenging the winner of the Ribbonwood-
Fritz match to meet Almont in a match at Melbourne.
Almont was bred by the late Henry Mace, at Christ-
church (M.L.), and was purchased for his Victorian
owners at the breaking-up sale, in Nov., 1902.

£lOO on that account) near Mt. Gambier, S.A.

Race), £3 25., £7 45., £lB 10s. (Great Eastern Steeple-
chase), £23 11s., £l2 18s., and £6 Is. A pound put blindly
on every horse that started would have meant a profit in

of nearly £3O 1

Admitting that Newhaven can leap some, he
will have to tuck his hind legs very far under indeed to
maintain Fitzgerald Bros.’ boast that he will exceed by
an inch the best record at the Agricultural Show’s jump-
ing contest. Desmond, Strathmore, and Tancred, ridden
by M‘Gee, Grady, and Atkins, all cleared 6ft. sin., thereby
lowering the Society’s record. This will take some
getting over.
• * * •
Mrs. O. Elliot’s win of the ladies’ high jump at
the Agricultural Show on Skylark over 6ft., was probably
a record woman’s jump. Allowing for the difference in
the saddles, which is all against the lady lepper, it was
far superior to any made by the male ‘riders. Worth
noticing the firm, determined, tightly-compressed mouth
worn by all equestrienne jumpers, caused, probably, by
practising the firmness necessary to obtain complete
mastery over the noble animal and the cultivation of the
nerve necessary to take the big risk with equanimity.
Note for young men about to marry : Never contradict a
horsey damsel if there’s a riding-whip within easy reach 1
• * * *
Concerning Street Arab’s win of Sydney Cup.
Numbers of people backed him because he was 13th in
the race book, and race was run on 13th of the month in
a year the figures of which add up 13. Strangely enough,
first two numbers on Street Arab’s ticket (13037) in
Tatt.’s No. 2 sweep further emphasised the “ office.” No.
13 in the list (Rose Petal) won the other big thing (Don-
caster Handicap) of the meeting.
* • • *
White Australia up North ! At a recent meet-
ing of the Townsville Trotting Club nine of the licensed
jockeys petitioned the stewards to stop an aboriginal
boy from riding in one of the events, and threatened, in
the event of their request not being acceded to, to strike
and so make the meeting a frost. Luckily, those stewards
had a backbone somewhere, and not only put the pro-
testing ones outside on their brassy cheek, but, for that
meeting, disqualified them in a bunch, and successfully
filled their seats with amateur riders. If the alleged
“ white” person up North had as much healthy contempt
for the many-colored imported agony as he has for the
local nig. from whom he stole the land, White Australia
would be in reality a White Australia much sooner.
• • • •
Evidently there was something the matter with
The Victory when he got so badly beaten (twice) by Great
Scot, at the V.R.O. Autumn meeting. His trainer has
the distinction of having captured two Melbourne Cups
with horses—Patron and The Victory- -that didn’t possess
a 100 to 1 chance, judged by their form at a petty
suburban meeting a few days previous to the big
handicap.
• • * •
“T.T. ”: It is rumored in racing circles that
Simile, the riderless winner of the Sydney Cup, had a
ticket on himself in “ Tattersall’s ” sweep. Also that
when he reported himself at the weighing enclosure and
learned that the race had been awarded to Street Arab
he wept bitterly and solemnly swore to abjure the turf
and take to a hansom cab. Of course, this may be an ex-
aggeration.
* * * •
There’s real grit in the proposal of the Kensing-
ton Racecourse proprietary to introduce a high standard
of coursing. The ground has been laid out with a view
to comfort—the Plumpton being of well-turfed sand, not
injurious to the feet of the dogs, while the hares are
paddocked in a manner that requires no handling—and
altogether the cruel pastime is getting a big boom.
Since the present co. has taken the grounds over about
£30,000 has been spent in improvements, and an annual
rent of £9OO is shelled out of its coffers. ’Twould be far
better if such a co. were to purchase grounds of its own,
when all improvements would become an asset, and the
huge rent tax a thing not to be contended with.
• • * *
At Coolgardie, recently, Ali Khan bet that his
black camel Selim could beat Abdul Phaman’s brown
camel Hazoff over two miles for £5O, both owners
up. Both brutes were well-known fliers, and the
wagering was heavy and the excitement high. A
big crowd rolled up, and a good start was
effected by a multitude of swarthy swabs arming them-
selves with palings and giving each camel a sounding
smack on the rear when the starting signal was given.
To the great dismay of his backets Selim soon fell a long
way behind, and Hazoff had the game all to himself till
someone ran round the back of the course and threw a big
armful of camel fodder in front of him. Hazoff immedi-
ately flopped and remained munching till his rival had
got about 20 lengths in front. Then, by dint of much
whacking and insulting language, Hazoff was at last in-
duced to get a gait on him again, but he had a terrible
lot of ground to make up, and the case appeared hopeless
till Bagdad Damascus or some such noble Arabian, who
had bet against Selim, ran half-way down the course
and shouted out “Hooshta!" whereupon the smellful
animal at once hooshed. It took several minutes’ per-
suasion with a rail to get him going again, and by the
time he got fairly started Hazoff had scooped the pool.
• * • *
N.S.W. reps, for inter-State 8-oar race, to be
rowed over 2J miles of the Yarra, next month, leave for
Melbourne present week. Father of Victorian rowing,
George Upward, has devoted his holiday coaching the
visiting crew which, though light comparatively (the men
average 11.2) is a huge improvement on any put forward
for years. Work is now very even and faults in style
have nearly all gone. Great advancement has been
made in leg drive, while recovery is steady. Craft moves
very freely between strokes and a trial up to 38 per
minute found the eight equal to it. Coach’s tip is that,
when he delivers them, though they may not win, they’ll
give Victorians a torrid time all the way, which is more
than a N.S.W. crew has got within a big margin of doing
for many a day.
• • • •
In one of the Easter cricket matches at Adelaide,
a Southern District batsman was given not out when
clean bowled, on the square leg umpire pointing out
that the wicket-keeper had a portion of his body in front
of the wicket. The fielding team (a visiting XI. from
Broken Hill) took a good deal of convincing, more es-
pecially as the two batsmen happened to be the sons of
the two umpires (!), but Rule 42 was successfully upheld.
To make it still more curious, the bowled man, being a
deaf mute, naturally walked away when his stumps went
down, and had to be brought back almost by force.
• » • •
There is no cricketer in Australia who has met
with such adversity and bad fortune as Harry Trott.
One-time he was one of the best trundlers in the States,
but when at his zenith an unfortunate illness kept him an
invalid for a couple of years, and caused a carefully-put-
aside credit balance to rapidly diminish. That was a
prelude to a sequestrated estate, and those to whom a
trifle was owing let the matter drop. The ex-Australian
Eleven skipper’s trials were many—all his trophies of the
cricket field being disposed of to gain the simple neces-
saries of life; and if any athlete deserves a “benefit”
and a “ lift " from a sport-loving people it is Harry Trott.

is 0. Wellington, who has an average of 167. Wellington
is a member of a well-known family of burly South Aus-
tralian cricketers—mostly parsons.
• * • •
“ Sprocket ” dives down into the misty past and
drags to light ancient records made on bone shakers,
liver jerkers, and other weird progenitors of the modern
bike, for comparison with the latest watch-breakers made
by century gears on banked tracks and pneumatic tyres.
On July 31, 1880, at Leicester (Eng.) one Phillips did a
quarter mile in 41 secs., and, as the old account has it,
“ started from a stool unassisted.” About the same date
J. Keen covered 6 miles at Cambridge University
Grounds in 14 mins. 42 secs. Aunger, at Adelaide,
recently did the same distance in 8 mins. 45 3-5 secs. In
1879 H. L. Cortis was declared champion of England up
to 50 miles, his records being for 1 mile, 2 mins. 42 2-5
secs. ; 4 miles, 11 mins. 53 secs. ; 25 miles, 1 hr. 26 mins.;
50 miles, 2 hrs. 56 mins. 54 4-5 secs. In 1883 the cham-
pionship of N.S.W. for 1 and 3 miles was held by
Lee, and for 10 miles by Bennett. In 1883 O. Hazel-
ton did a sensational mile and 30 yards at Melbourne in
2 mins. 43 4-5 secs., which was understood to have broken
all then previous records. Rolf won the six days’
championship of Australia in 1884 with 910 miles 11 laps
in 59 hours. There were good men Jin those days con-
sidering the awful jiggers they bestrode.
• • * •
The “injured racing cyclists’” sports meeting
in Melbourne recently realised but a paltry £22o—and
that is provided certain outstanding items are “realised.”
A few falls, together with a like number of heavy claims,
and the fund will be no more. Still, Victoria has done a
little. Where are the other States ? Every branch of
sport should be compelled to initiate a fund for the
benefit of injured athletes, and a percentage deducted
from the gross takings for such a purpose.
* * * m
The imported Major Taylor shook the Aus-
tralian dust off his feet last week, and left to fulfil Euro-
pean engagements. The colored flier certainly opened the
eyes of Australian riders, and taught them a few wrinkles;
but, considering that he has raked in well nigh £3OOO
during a four months’ sojourn, he hasn’t been under-
paid. Taylor, owing to his religious faith no doubt, con-
sidered ’twas best to run “ straight,” and, accordingly
he was always out after the boodle—something which
cannot be said of all the oversea visitors that have hap-
pened to afflict Australia.
• » * .
Major Taylor’s popularity was worth its weight
in gold to the cycling bodies of the various States, and
was the means of re-establishing many bodies whose finan-
cial position was sadly in need of a lift. The S.A body
profited to the extent of nearly £5OO ; and the amassed
shekels raked in by the various State leagues should be
spent on a general reconstruction of the racing tracks
Followers of the game like to see first-class riding, and
the riders need first-class paths on which to show their
speed ; so the promoters should see that the easily-
acquired cash is laid out in a satisfactory manner At
present Australia hasn’t got a decent track.

Victoria (where there are at least twice as manv
bowling clubs as in any other State) for the first time on
record sent teams to N.S.W., S.A., and Tas. during Easter
week and, through spreading out too thin, buffered
defeat all round. N.S.W. won all three matches of thp
rub by 38, 7 and 13 ; and Saturday last Randwick and
Waverley Clubs combined tackled the visitors in a 6-rink
tussle, and gave them the biggest drubbing of all-win
ning with 40 to the good. Wheat State howlers won first
and third matches, and lost second. Tas. reps gathered
first and second and made a draw of third. Latest was
27th meeting between N.S.W. and Victoria, and first win
N.S.W. has scored in three years. Since initiation of
these contests in 1880, 27 have occurred, 17 of them
going Victoriawards.

“Kink”: A country racehorse (!) owner told
me the other day that the ownership of a couple of
neddies was worth a clear fiver a week to him. He enters
them for all the races in the district, in one or two
events, and, although he hasn’t much show except
in very poor company, he often gets his “ex’s” paid
him to run a bye, and at the same time gets the “ office”
as to what is going. So far he has only a “walk-over"
to his credit, after four years at the game, yet he clearly
lives on it somehow or other, having no other means of
support.

The report of Victoria’s Parliamentary
Committee on the alleged cures of inebriety by the
various “ cure ” institutions is out. It is neither
enthusiastic nor respectful. Of the four institu-
tions which submitted themselves for approval,
two declined to divulge their particular specific;
the third breathed its information into the com-
mittee’s ear in strict confidence; the
fourth, run by an Adelaide clergyman, didn t go
in for any particular nostrum, but relied on moral
suasion. The three ‘‘ specific” dosers all admitted
having produced amazing results, and brought
forward any number of grateful “cures, who
were ready to vouch for the efficacy of this or
that method. But the committee could get no
evidence to show that the cures were in any way
permanent, and there appears to have been
often a lack of evidence as to how lost
the saved drunk had been. Also, the committee
had no chance to inspect those saved drunks who
had become lost again, or to find out what per-
centage was lost again. Practically the only
things the committee was quite sure about when
all was over were these :
1. That provision should be made for registration,
under Government supervision, of all institutions for the
treatment of inebriety. _
2. That all such institutions must be placed under Go-
vernment supervision and inspection. .
3. That a special place of confinement and treatment
must be provided for criminal inebriates, where offences
may be directly traced to inebriety, or where ground for
detention is habitual drunkenness itself.
4. That a Government institution under a board of con-
trol be established in some locality suitable for isolation,
classification, and opportunity for employment, to which
inebriates can be sent voluntarily or compulsorily, in
which different methods of treatment may be adopted.
5. That a Bill be immediately introduced by the Go-
vernment determining the conditions under which in-
ebriates can be dealt with on the lines of the New South
Wales measure of September, 1900, and making provision
for the foregoing recommendations.
SHAKESPEARIAN THERAPEUTICS. it
anything can “ minister unto a mind diseased ’ it
is assuredly Hunyadi Janos, which will “ cleanse
the stuff’d bosom of the perilous stuff which

Apropos “Ooloo'” tales of horror (B.
14/3/’O3). At Sydney Benevolent Asylum, many
years ago, a doctor operating on a pauper patient
sewed up a bull-dog forceps and some other
luggage in the patient. The woman died.
N.S.W. Arbitration Court’s next big case
will be in connection with the contract conditions
and rates of pay of Broken Hill miners. Present
terms date from the last big general strike, or
lock-out, or whatever it was, in 1892 ; for 11 years
the men have chafed under them, but the unions
have at no time felt strong enough to kick. As
soon as the Arbitration Court was created they
started on their claims; but it was probably
a stroke of very good luck for them that their
case was hung up for 12 months, otherwise it
might have been dealt with at the bottom of a
very bad slump in the silver-lead mining business.
At Maryborough (Q.) the President of the
Chamber of Commerce hysterically declared, the
other day, that “ no man in Austria, Germany, or
France had exercised the autocratic powers which
Mr. Kingston had exercised.” It is curious how
frantic these importers get. Kingston never
hanged, or imprisoned, or fined in as much as one
penny a single importer on his own responsibility;
m fact, the alleged autocrat never did anything
to importers on his own responsibility beyond
refusing to suppress his knowledge of their
swindles. Every one of them who fell in did so
because he couldn’t convince a magistrate or a
judge and jury (with none of whom Kingston
had anything to do), that he wasn’t a degraded
swindler and a rogue. A French autocrat of
the Napoleon I. type would have gaoled some of
these importers for life without trial.

Parson Dill-Macky claims the result of the
Tamworth election as a victory for his sectarian
organisation, which gives a slight air of credibility
to the yarn that Opposition candidate Nankervis
was forced out of the contest because he wouldn’t
run on sectarian lines, and that Garland got the
support of the strife-mongers by pledging himself
to help on their policy of bigotry and public
disturbance. Garland, if he is wise, will promptly
repudiate the Dill-Macky patronage—that is to
say, if he can do so without too much damage to
the immortal truth.
Sydney D.T. daily approximates more
closely to the Tory Argus policy. April 17 it
hinted at a disfranchisement of the public servants
on the lines of the recent Victorian measure,
saying:
In New South Wales there is a section of the people
about as large as the whole population of Tasmania
directly dependent on the public exchequer for a liveli-
hood. And all the men and women so circumstanced
have votes, for which the Government charged with the
management of the finances has to cater the same as for
those of other electors. No one can doubt that this is a
serious position for any State to be placed in, and as the
proportion of Government employees and Government
dependents of one kind and another is on the increase,
the situation arising out of it threatens to soon furnish,
if it has not done so already, one of the most perplexing
problems connected with the politics of the State. This
vast, compact, and now organised army of voters has its
first interest in keeping up public expenditure to the
highest limit, for therein lies the prosperity of their
trade.
Nowadays, when one S. Australian Minis-
ter goes out visiting at Cup time the man who
stays at home has to be a lot of things. Butler,
who has a farm in the lower north to look after, has
this month been also acting Premier, Treasurer,
Minister of Health, Minister for the Northern
Territory, Chief Secretary, and Minister of
Lands; and Fisher, a country storekeeper, is
Attorney-General, Minister of Education, Com-
missioner of Public Works and Minister of In-
dustry.

Melbourne Age, with its new fad for inter-
viewing and publishing the opinions of all manner
of hardshell Tories, gets some curious items of
alleged information. Here is one :
I think that much of the depopulation is caused by the
interference in trade and industry by Parliament. By
this I mean particularly certain features of the Factories
Acts. We know that large numbers of young men have
been positively sent away by these Acts. They were
thrown out of work, and there was nothing for them to
do but to emigrate.
There is something magnificent about the cheek
of the person who talks about the “depopulation”
of Victoria. The population of Victoria only
increased at the average rate of 2400 a year from
1892 to 1896, when there was no Factories Act.
After that the increases were : 1897, 2380; 1898,
280; 1899, 6300; 1900, 7720 ; 1901, 11,320. There
is something extra about the gall of the Tory
who calls that steadily increasing growth of popu- :
lation a process of ck population.
It was reported a few days ago that a
private had been seriously injured at the recent
Bathurst military encampment through the brutal
horseplay of his comrades, who tossed him in a
blanket and let him land on the ground when he
came down. There has been a great silence on the
subject since, and the matter seems to have
dropped as well as the unfortunate private—who
is said to have damaged his spine in falling.
Australia doesn’t want its little military show to
get the same sort of smellful reputation that the
British army has acquired, so further information
would be thankfully received.

Sydney sixpenny hash-shops which, a little
while ago, raised prices, “owing to the dearness
of meat,” have now come down to the sixpenny
level again.
“H.” : Upon a deceased person of some
note in his day :
It surprises me to get hold of an autobiography of
George Francis Train, dictated by him in 1902, and just
issued by Heinemann, London. There are steep things
enough in it, but yet a good deal that can be verified
otherwise. He was born in Boston, 1829. His father and
mother shortly took their family to New Orleans, where
the yellow fever raged frightfully in 1833. It slew the
whole family, except little G. F., who had been sent away
to Methodist relatives in Boston. They reared him
well, and as a young man he made a great business
mark. After doing well in Boston he went to Liver-
pool, England, and did better. Returning to America,
he gathered up a lot of agencies for Australia. He
arrived in Melbourne in 1854, when 600 ships were in
the bay. He and his partner, Caldwell, built a large
bluestone warehouse in Flinders-street, a little east of
Elizabeth-street. The cost was £16,000. He claims to
have originated the Hobson’s Bay railway, and put up
Freeman Cobb to the idea of the coaches. When Lola
Montes came he interviewed her behind the scenes of the
Theatre Royal, and was enveloped in a cloud of lace,
through her kicking over his head (they always do). A
book Train published about 1858, entitled “ Young
America Abroad,” gives you a better notion of his
Melbourne experiences, and is more sober. He has
been off his onion since. His most astounding
story is that the Eureka Stockade insurgents wanted to
make Train President of the Australian Republic. The
real leader, as the police well knew, was James M'Gill.
Train alleges that M'Gill, badly wanted by the authorities,
walked into his Melbourne office a week or two after the
affair, and there ran against Capt. McMahon, Chief Com-
missioner of Police, afterwards Speaker of the Assembly.
Train alleges that he beguiled M'Mahon away before he
discovered M'Gill, and shipped the latter off to San
Francisco. In fact, from his own report, Train was
pretty well running Victoria just about that time, and
few things happened without his supervision.
All the public clocks in S’Africa were put
on half-an-hour at midnight on February 28, and
all Africa (so far as the British have a say in it)
will now have a standard time from the Cape to
Cairo.
“ Burglar Bill,” in reply to “ Darkholer ’
(4/4/’O3): —
“ Darkholer ” and myself were evidently residents in
the one gaol, but from his remarks I judge that he occu-
pied the position of a warder, while I was a convict. That
makes all the difference. A warder never hears the truth
from a prisoner. When I wrote “ It’s in gaol you hear
the truth about a case,” I wrote as an ex-prisoner, not as
an ex-warder. I never knew a prisoner to tell a warder
the truth. The prisoner whom “Darkholer” mentions
as admitting his crime is an idiot that is
why he admitted. Although a prisoner, I am
better informed than “ Darkholer.” The “ ex-
phrenological lecturer of Sydney Domain ” did
not get the run for “ not rousing on the prisoners,” but
got 48 hours notice from the Deputy Comptroller of
Prisons for asking leave to practise his profession (sic) as
a phrenologist in conjunction with his warder’s duties.
This mis-statement of “ Darkholer ” backs up my former
remarks that the “ screw” is an indolent know-nothing ;
the smart prisoner with an eye to every move generally
knows more about the workings of the gaol than the
ordinary warder. “Darkholer” says the town and
settled farming districts contributed 18 revolting cases
out of 22. I doubt it. When I came out I wrote 15
letters to prisoners’ friends. Five of the prisoners were
lifers, and three were in for horrible offences, and
I know the addresses of their friends were pretty far
back. In fact, the two most revolting cases in the gaol
came from the dark Never Never. And a well-informed
warder must know there is only one “ first-timers’ ” gaol
in N.S.W. His opening sentence saying that my “facts
from one gaol cannot warrant an inference of general
applicability ” leads one to believe that he has found
another. I distinctly mentioned “first timers’” gaol.
Perhaps a prisoner told “Darkholer” where there was
another. The ordinary “ screw ” receives his education
in gaol.

Dwellers in one Australian suburb were
horrified t’other week to find that a needy identity
of the neighborhood, regarded as a convenient
consumer of broken vittles and wearer of old
clo’, had succeeded in getting himself officially
appointed to the joint post of hangman and public
flagellatonT jHis sometime patrons shuddered to
reflect that, while leading his master’s kine in the
green pastures of X , and calling regularly at
hospitable backdoors for household flotsam and
jetsam, the scragger had already attended one
execution ‘in a subordinate capacity,’ and had
done several chores in the flogging line. The
charitable woman who gavethehangmansome neck
of mutton last week can’t think of that dish now
without a shudder. The new hangman is a chin-
less little person, with an eerily feminine voice
(seemingly pitched somewhere up in the top of
his head) and is the son of respectable parents in
the cold country, whence a fond mother occa-
sionally forwards to responsible persons small
sums of money for his benefit. He brought with
him from ’Ome a comely wife and really pretty
and winsome children, but, as he soon became a
widower, he took the advice of charitable persons
and handed over his motherless family to the care
of a religious institution. When it was rumored
that the bereaved man was advertising for a new
wife, householders surmised that in future they
would be called upon to pan out crusts and cold
tea for two, little suspecting that the expectant
bridegroom had in view a regular source of income
at the gallows and triangle.

It is not often that a man runs into gaol
to get away from a policeman, yet such actually
occurred a few days ago in Sydney. A wanted
person, after a long run, made a final effort to
shake off the pursuing bobby by jumping a fence,
whereat the bobby eased up and shed a benign
smile across the enclosure. The man had jumped
into the yard of No. 4 police station !
“M'Quaid” writes from Queensland
about Victoria’s long-lost hangman, Gately :
The man who wrote up Gately in your columns stopped
short with his sudden disappearance from the hangman-
ship and floggership of Victoria. Probably the detectives
who searched for the vanished official strangler confined
their search too exclusively to the Yarra banks. My
acquaintance with Gately commenced some 23 years ago,
where your correspondent’s left off. He was then doing
time (a short time) at St. Helena, Brisbane—a case of
assault and battery only. My duties brought me in
touch with him daily. He was no better and
no worse than thousands of those who affect to
feel shuddersome if compelled to sit in a tram-
car with a Nosey Bob or a Gately, yet would
deem it a lifelasting honor to be even noticed by
the “hero of Omdurman.” While Gately was on the
“ island ” he was kept apart from the other prisoners.
He was excessively vain, and dyed his hair and beard a
jet black. He used to make his dye from material
growing on the “ farm ” ; said material was a trade
secret. After his discharge from the “ island ”he applied
for and got a pass to the Mitchell extension of the S. and
W. railway. He was not long on the works ere he was
recognised as the Victorian ex-hangman, and unpopu-
larity set in. Every pay saw Gately very much in
evidence ; for him to have a dozen fights in a day was
nothing unusual. He was no coward, but, being old, his
wind used to fail him, and it was a common occurrence
for him to sit or walk round until he got a fresh stock of
oxygen, and then resume his battle. The Muckadilla
rum proved more fatal in the end than the combined
hostility of the navvies, and he was counted out, and all
that was left of Gately, alias Bourke, was thrown into a
shallow grave—unwept, and all the rest of it.

Deaf persons need no longer despair,

OBSERVATIONS.
If some people were only half as clever as they think
they are they would be a great deal cleverer than they
are now.
Don’t judge a man by the company he keeps. The
company may keep him.
When I see a rich man surrounded by his friends I
think of me and fleas.
A man often finds his close friends close.
If love is blind it doesn’t long go begging.
What paradoxes some women are. They support
themselves by falling.
An acquaintance with the “ cat ” is sometimes the
result of going to the dogs.
Love is blind, and is therefore eager to see a match
struck.
J.J.O.
“ The fatal opulence of bishops ” —a good
phrase, by a parson too.
“ Burglar Bill ” : Till lately six freemen
and six prisoners constituted a coroner’s jury for
an inquest on a prisoner who had died inside.
Quite recently an old man died in a N.S.W. gaol,
and, after the six most ignorant prisoners had been
selected as jurymen, they were suddenly dismissed
and twelve free men hooked from the rag-and-bob-
tail of the town. What has brought about the
change, and who, and why ? The prisoner gener-
ally knows something that should be asked, but
which he is afraid of asking ; but the outside man
knows nothing and doesn’t care. In the case
to which I am referring it would seem that it was
only the priest’s threat that induced the authori-
ties to put the unfortunate old man of over 70
years of age in gaol hospital. The temperature of
the cell in which he was confined was 30 degrees,
and he was given a black draught an hour before
he died. Black draughts are a panacea for many
ills in gaol.

Willoughby of “ Wrong Mr. Wright ” fame dealt a blow
to piracy when he succeeded in restraining Henrv from

“T.D,”: Edward Lloyd broke down, through frov-in.
the-throat, in one of his songs at his second Melb con-
cert, and with a semi-jocular nod to the audience began
all over again—certainly singing far better the ’second
time than the first. Were a minor artist to do so he
would simply vanish from all leading concert programmes
permanently, and singers are many a time kept from
actually breaking down, through cold or nervousness or
failure to remember the words, by this knowledge They
may sing very badly in consequence, but it is the “ break
down ’’ that audiences regard as the unpardonable sin.
Pinero, in picking his cast for the first production of
“ Dandy Dick,” decided that the man to impersonate the
rural policeman exactly to his liking was W. H. Denny,
whose forte, as an actor, is a natural assumption of infal-
libility. Noah Topping, the policeman aforesaid, was
played by Brough in Australia, but Brough, when bossing
the situation and commanding his “darling” wife to
“ shu-u-t oop,” didn’t strike one as being to the manner
born. Denny has the autocratic voice and instinct
strongly developed. This accounts for his recognised
lien on the character of Bottom the Weaver, in “ Mid-
summer Night’s Dream.” No performance of “The
Dream ” can be considered quite complete nowadays
unless Denny plays Bottom. “ Yet my chief humor is for
a tyrant : I could play Ercles rarely, or a part to tear a
cat in, to make all split.” The audiences at Melbourne
Princess’s accept Denny’s delivery of those lines as a
satisfactory personal explanation.
“ T.F.D.” : Once knew a girl who was nursemaid to the
only child of a lately deceased actress. She told me the
actress would spend hours in front of her cheval-glass
admiring her bare legs. “ She asked me often,” said the
girl, “if I wouldn’t like a pair as good, but mine ain’t so
dusty, so I said I was satisfied.’’
About the most interesting thing said at the annual
meeting of the Hannan’s Land Co. in London, after the
announcement of a 10 per cent. div. and the writing-off of
£613 for depreciation, was that the directors proposed to
invest £20,000 in building a theatre at Kalgoorlie. There
are 40,000 reserved shares, which will probably be issued
for the purpose.
“Melb.”: Died, April 14, John Leon Jones, aged 83,
ex-circus proprietor, known as Mons. St. Leon, father of
Gus St. Leon and Walter St. Leon, etc. Had a long and
honorable career, in rivalry of Burton, Ashton, and the
rest. Not to be confounded with Billy Jones, of Fitz-
gerald’s.
Chicago papers are enthusiastic in their praise of Maud
MacCarthy as a violinist“ Miss MacCarthy is delicate,
refined, almost fragile, but she draws a larger tone than
any woman who has ever played here, and one which
startles many a masculine opponent. She has an- unyield-
ing authority and rhythmic sense that are as firmly
grounded as a rock, and she is about as quiet, aaunmov-
able. Miss MacCarthy’s playing has emotional quality
and pure sentiment stamped indefinably upon it.”
Young Maurice Kellermann, eldest son of Madame
Charbonnet-Kellermann, promises well as a violinist.
Though but 18 he is one of the first violins of the Phil-
harmonic orchestra, has just been appointed second
violin at the Princess’ for the Shakspearian season, and
conducts and trains on his own account a very efficient
little orchestra at Cheltenham.

Talk about hard luck ! Yankee papers
tell of a man who had served 25 years for murder
being liberated in a street where the electric
trams ran. Twenty-five years before, when he
went through the front-gate of the gaol, the trams
down that street were drawn by horses, and he
had never seen or heard of an electric car. When
they showed him out, an electric car was standing
a few yards above the gaol gates, and seeing no
horses attached to it he concluded they were at
the other end and that the tram was going in the
opposite direction. Then there was a ringing of
bells, a sudden shouting, a quick stopping, and a
dead body was carried back into the gaol.
