A shocked citizen sends this :
The landlady of a popular Westralian hotel was relating
her matrimonial miseries to some of her most favored
customers, myself among the number : “ I left my first
husband immediately after the wedding breakfast"
“Why?” asked one of her auditors. “ Because ” ex-
plained the lady, “he got drunk and kissed all the
bridesmaids, and wanted to cut up my garters for wed-
ding favors, and then started chasing the barmaids all
over the shop. And since then,” she sighed, “ I’ve never
set eyes upon him.” “ But,” chipped in a most incon-
venient citizen, “you had two children when you
married your second husband in Kanowna.” “Yes ’’the
lady admitted, with a pensive sigh, “ but those children
happened after I came to Westralia.” And, as the editor
of a pious paper over there remarks, “ A great many
things happen after a great many people come to
Westralia.”
Hearne

From the Satukday Review (London) :
Under our policy of free-trade our agriculture has been
ruined, our manufactures have for years been struggling
for existence, our working classes are calling out for em-
ployment, whilst we are paying to the foreigner more
than £400,000,000 a year in the purchase of produce,
much of which might, under a common sense policy,
have been furnished at home, giving employment to our
own working classes. The United States and Germany
are pressing us hard, and taking away our trade, even in
our own markets. Our imports now exceed our exports
by about £180,000,000 a year. The industries of the
United States have developed by leaps and bounds under
a policy of stringent protection, which the Cobden Club
prophesied would be their ruin ; their special exports in
1898 increased by £76,000,000 when compared with those
of 1890, whilst those of Great Britain decreased by
£30,000,000.

The Turn Off.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
As the waggon came alongside the swaggie threw
his “bluey” aboard, and followed it with an
alacrity surprising for one of his profession.
When comfortably seated he ventured to address
Terry for the first time.
“ Keepin’ very dry, boss. The ’eat seems to be
playin’ up with yer team. Wonder when this
drought’s goin’ ter break up ? ”
The man from the “ Devil's Bit ” didn’t approve
of suoh free-and-easy methods ; but, as he knew
the remarks were true, he nodded his head.
“’Ow far from’ere to Windeyer, boss? I ’ear
they’re gettin’ a little gold on the Meroo and
Long’s Creek way.”
“About twinty-three moiles ’ll land ye at
Windeyer,” said Terry, coughing to prevent him-
self from choking with indignation.
As this was all the information the swagman
wanted, and as he could plainly see his imposition
was not approved of, he said no more.
From then till nearly sundown the only words
heard were those addressed by Terry to his
bullocks, but when the scattered huts and would-
be farms indicated that a town was not far distant,
the stranger said —“ S’pose we’ll git to Windeyer
by sundown, boss ? ”
“ Windeyer’s thirty-foive moiies from here” said
Terry calmly.
“Wot? Didn’ you say this mornin’ that 23 mile
’ud see me there? ”
“Oi did. An’ so it wud —but ye should have
turned-off at Guntawang ! ”.
“Then, why the hell did’n yer say so before,
instead of havin’ me perched in this dam furnace
all day?”
“The ‘ perrchin in this dam furnace’ was yer
own doin’; an’ Oi hope the nixt toime ye want a
roide, ye’ll have the dacency to ax for it.”
The swagman threw his “drum” into the road
with as much vim as if it had been the carcase of
O’Shaughnessy himself. Then he got out, shook
as much of the road as he could out of his bruised
swag, and made tracks back to the turn-off —a
wilder but wiser man.
PADDY MELON.
What to Carry to Newcastle.
In England the expression “carry coals to New-
castle ” has for centuries been used to denote any
absurdly unnecessary act, such as gilding refined
gold, painting the lily, or bidding a boy to whistle
and interfere with whatever is within his reach.
With regard to carrying coals from Newcastle,
that has been done in England for a vast
number of years, and the process is still going
on as merrily as ever. It is no more neces-
sary to carry coals to our own Newcastle,
here in Australia, than it is to carry coals to
the English Newcastle. Indeed, we Australians
confidently expect to have a few tons of the dusty
diamonds on hand after the English Newcastle
has been cleared of them and has only a memory
of former wealth. What is necessary to carry to
Newcastle, N.S.W., is good health for her
miners, and this is best done in the form of bottles
of Mother Seigel’s Curative Syrup. A great
many people know this to be true, and one of
them is Mr. J. Thompson, of Edward-street,
Merewether, near Newcastle, N.S.W. Writing
on October 10th, 1902, Mr. Thompson says
“In 1899 I suffered very much from indigestion
and derangement of the stomach. The food I
ate was productive of much pain. Frequently I
was obliged to hurry from the table to reject all
that I had just swallowed. This wretched con-
dition compelled me to restrict myself to the very
plainest of fare. My complaint greatly disturbed
my rest at night, and often I have turned out of
bed and paced the floor for hours before I could
obtain a wink of sleep. I procured medicine
from the dispensary, but it did me no good. It
was the dispenser himself who advised me to try
Mother Seigel’s Curative Syrup. My landlady j
procured a bottle of it from Mr. Allinson’s stores,
and I used it with the best possible results. The
first doses conferred upon me immediate relief,
aud by the time I had consumed a bottle and a
half (half-crown size) I was thoroughly cured and
able to enjoy my food again. It is now more
than two years ago since I was restored to health
by Mother Seigel’s Curative Syrup; and I am still
well and hearty—pretty good evidence that when
the Syrup cures it does so very thoroughly. I can
now eat at any time, and to any reasonable extent,
whatever I have a fancy for; and that, too,
without any dread of after-consequences.”
South Australia has had singularly dazzling
luck in getting hold of other people’s good things.
South-west Queensland, Broken Hill (N.S.W.),
and Kalgoorlie (Westralia) have been worth mil-
lions to the State, which hasn’t much of its own to
feel good about; while up to now the other States
have not had a chance to get anything back.
Practically, the only South Australian thing
very well worth picking up, is what all the rest
of the State refers to vaguely as “our South-
East ” —a little patch of volcanic soil which grows
very fine potatoes and onions, but isn’t large
enough to grow very many of either. While the
border duties lasted Adelaide held the trade in
this district without worrying much ; but since
those went Victoria (which has rather the advan-
tage, geographically) has made the pace so will-
ing that Adelaide is getting red in the face and
wants to relieve her feelings by kicking some-
body. At present the toe is pointed towards the
Railway Commissioner, partly, perhaps, because
the South Australian Commissioner is a very meek
person who wouldn’t kick back.

THE CASE OF PLUG.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Ranelagh was second suburban and block-signal
station south of Romahapa, on the main southern
line. It was stuck about quarter way down the
big incline below where the line comes out of
Ranelagh tunnel, and the back portion of the
platform and station buildings was supported on
thin, spidery piles.
It “blocked ” with Ballantyre on the south and
Kenilworth Signal Station on the north. Kenil-
worth communicated with Romahapa, the head
station of the section. At Ranelagh were inter
locking starting signals, and, owing to the steep
grade and the lack of brake-power on Maoriland
railway engines at that time, it was understood
that under no circumstances was any shunting to
take place at Hillgrove workshops, between Kenil-
worth and Ranelagh, at any hour that might make
it necessary to try and stop a heavy goods train
on that terrific incline. This was absolutely
necessary, as no heavy goods train could stop
under any circumstances.
The stationmaster was one Plug. His name
ought to have been Tank, for his capacity for
absorbing beer amounted to genius, but having a
gigantic wife and a large family (14, I think), he
was a sad example of genius denied opportunity.
Still, he did very well, Laver’s Brewery was
only twenty yards from the end of the platform,
and it was unusually liberal with its liquid. The
whole of the goods traffic at Ranelagh con-
sisted of coal and grain coming in and beer
going out. But for that brewery Plug had
assuredly died of broken heart and cracked throat.
There was also Jimmy, the cadet: mostly
known as “the blanky kid.” His life was a
misery. He sorted tickets all day long and was
the target for all the bad temper of the overworked
sub and drunken stationmaster.
I was sub-stationmaster. ,Plug was entirely
innocent of any knowledge of book-keeping, and
belonged to the old school which manned Maori-
land railways when it was hard to get men to
work for wages; when Mullocky and Gabriel’s
Gullies were yielding up fortunes in a week, and
the roaring Vogel policy had swamped the land
with borrowed cash, and all Maoriland, including
its railways, was on a mad drunk. The old hands
never quite got over that drunk.
It was pay-week when I arrived at Ranelagh,
and I was given the morning shift. The lucre was
due any time between ten a.m. and noon. About
half-past nine a large, aggressive-looking female,
with four chins, appeared in the doorway, squeezed
through it, waddled across the office and subsided
into my chair, s.
I turned on the apparition angrily, but remem-
bered a recent admonition of the District Traffic
Manager’s to cultivate tact. So, with anger in
my heart, I smiled pleasantly, bowed, and begged
to be informed what I could do for the lady.
She seemed taken back by my extreme polite-
ness. She was not accustomed to it. She made
several starts, and finally made me understand
that she was Mrs. Plug. I bowed again, and said
that I had not had the pleasure of seeing the
stationmaster that morning.
“No,” she said, “I don’t suppose as how you
have. He’s down at Laver’s Brewery, starting to
make a hog of himself. It’s a terrible thing, Mr.,
this drink. There’s Plug, a good husband and
father, but he do make an animal of himself every
pay-day; an’ if I wasn’t to come up here and draw
his pay he’d have it all spent afore he came home.
My Gawd ! it’s terrible. Don’t you marry, young
man, if you’re inclined to lush.”',
I felt sorry for this great ungainly woman with
four chins. It was so pathetic! Plug’s salary
was £156 per annum, with a free house, and out
of it that unfortunate woman had to clothe and
feed 14, and still spare enough for Plug to go out
and make a beast of himself once a month.
A few minutes later Mr. Plug staggered into
the office, hiccuped, and gravely introduced me to
his wife. Then he sat down on the cold stove and
commenced to swear at the cadet. Whenever the
block bells called he would try to get across to
take the signal, but I generally managed to antici-
pate him ; he was handicapped by having to walk
round his wife.
The trains and went. Through “passen-
gers” roared past. People called for letters and tele-
grams, and the hours dragged along, but Mrs. Plug
sat in my chair patiently, while Plug reclined on
the cold stove, his head against the pipe, cursing
the traffic office in such language that I got angry
and asked him with a snap if he was aware there
was a lady within hearing.
He looked startled. “Where?” he asked.
“Anyhow, why don’t they sit in the ladies
waiting-room and keep away from this end of the
platform ? ”
He evidently thought I referred to someone
outside, and I did not enlighten him. He swore
no more, and a few minutes later the bagcontaining
the money arrived. Mrs. Plug grabbed it, un-
locked it, counted it out, glanced at the pay-sheet,
gave me mine, handed his to the cadet, pocketed
Plug’s, and turning to him, handed him half-a-
sovereign with the admonition :
“There yer are ; go and make a hanimal of
yerself and get it over ! ” .
Plug looked at me with a most pathetic ex-
pression.
“That’s all I get out of it ! ” he said ruefully,
showing me the half-sovereign. Then he went
with alacrity.
The gigantio woman waddled away. At the
door she turned.
“ I’m sorry to have to poke me nose in where
I’ve no right to, Mr., but you can see for yer-
self how I’m just druv to it. Yer the first gentle-
man we’ve had at this statioiv. * I hopes yer’ll stay
for ever. ”
She meant it kindly too, but the thought of it
threw a gloom over me the rest of the day.
At 1 p.m. Plug failed to materialise, and I
found myself let in for a double shift. At mid-
night Mrs. Plug arrived at the office and informed
me she had discovered Plug in a state of insensi-
bility. “ He took ten shillin’s worth of draught
rum down to Laver’s, and the whole Brewery s as
tight as a lord. They mixed it with the beer,
God forgive ’em. I’ll have old Plug up here for
the first train right enough. Don’t you trouble
coming down for No. 1 unless I sends for yer.
Good-night!”
She was good as her word, and Plug worked the
morning shift for the rest of that week. 1 began
to develop an admiration for Mrs. Plug.
• » • *
Next pay-day, before I let Plug go, I wanted
guarantees that he would return by 1 p.m., and
after a long argument we compromised. I left
the station immediately, and was to return at 8.30
p.m., so that Plug could get away for the usual
purpose and have time to do it thoroughly.
He tried hard to persuade me to return at G 30.
The Lavers objected to him taking the rum to the
brewery before 6‘30. They had no objection to
Plug and the brewer getting howling drunk on
brewery beer as long as it was done after hours.
Romahapa chimes were sounding 815 when I
strolled down the long platform. I heard the
Ballantyre block call twice, and wondered if
Plug’s thirst had tempted him to leave the office.
The bell sounded a third time, and I ran for my
life. The office door was wide open. I rushed in
—and stood petrified.
Plug was down on his hands and knees on the
floor, looking in wonderment from one block to
the other. He couldn’t make out which block
was calling. The block called a fourth time, and
the man at Ballantyre gave his strokes slowly and
emphatically.
A sudden smile of recognition illuminated the
face of Plug ; he discovered it was the Ballantyre
instrument that was sounding. He went across
the office on all-fours, hauled himself up the leg
of the block table, and propping himself against it
in a kneeling position, cheerfully sounded, “ Line
clear,” and took the signal. There might have
been a dozen landslips on his own platform for all
he knew.
Once more he progressed across the office, like a
troglodyte, to the Kenilworth block, and signalled
the train through ; then he progressed some more
outside, down to the signal-levers, pulled down
the starting-signal, put a white light near the edge
of the platform, crawled inside, slammed the
door, and then the silence was only broken by a
gurgling sound.
So far he had done everything quite correctly,
but it was a purely automatic process. Suddenly
the Kenilworth block sounded “obstruction.” I
rushed in. Plug stared stupidly at the block.
There was no reflex action of the brain now, for
it was only occasionally—once in a year perhaps—
that a signal-box sounded “obstruction” after
having accepted a signal.
I rushed to the Ballantyre block and sounded
‘ ‘ obstruction ” twice, in clear sharp rings. Then
to the Ballantyre telephone, and the Scotch voice
of Macdonald trembled as he asked, “ What’s
oop ? ’ ’
“ Can’t you stop her, Mac. ? There’s something
wrong at Hillgrove, I suspect —Kenilworth’s just
‘ obstructed ’ after giving clear line.”
“She’s half-way up the hill, oot o’ sight. She
haes 39 trucks and a guard’s van, an’ me ain
brither’s brakesmaun —for the love o’ God, laddie,
try and stop her at the tunnel’s mooth.”
Kenilworth still rang “obstruction,” rang it
frantically. I stopped a second to acknowledge
it, and then, grabbing the lamp, fled outside and
up the hill towards the tunnel. As I went out of
the door, I saw Plug sitting on the floor with his
back against the leg of the desk and a puzzled
look on his bloated face.
I sprinted up that grade to the tunnel as I
never sprinted before. I could hear the train
snorting up the hill the other side. Each stroke of
the piston-rods was plainly audible, and in imagin-
ation I could see the long-bearded old driver
petting and cooing the big Tynesider as though it
were a horse, all unconscious that he would meet a
red light at the end of the tunnel, and perhaps,
half-a-mile below, Death waiting.
The train had entered the tunnel; the long,
regular piston strokes had become a roaring sound ;
there was a rhythmical sounding of rail joints ;
the three short, sharp whistles sounded for the
brakes, and then her head-light streamed oyer the
track and showed me wildly waving a red light.
There was a screech of brakes hard on. I looked
up at the engine as she thundered past above me.
The driver’s head was thrust out at the side win-
dow, his beard streamed behind him, and on the
hawk-like face there was a look of grim despair.
His eyes were puckered up, his brows were down,
and he was straining to see what was in front—all
in a flash the picture burnt itself on my memory
for ever.
Every wheel streamed with fire. Brakesman
Macdonald was climbing from one truck to
another, letting down the hand-brakes, and, as
the sounding of the rail-joints died away, I caught
a glimpse of the guard holding the wheel-brake at
the back of the van.
God help them ! they could no more stop than
they could leap over the obstruction, whatever it
was. My trembling legs almost refused to carry
me back to the office, but I made an effort, and,
pulling myself together, ran, in a weak, pitiful
sort of a way, to where Plug sat on the floor
staring stupidly from one block to the other.
“ Glad you’ve come, old man ; these dam blocks
are going fair mad. Both the somethings are
calling at once, and hang me if I know what it’s
all about. They’re waiting for me at Laver’s, so
I’ll just leave you to it.”
lie went out and waddled or crawled somehow
out of sight. I knew I ought to do something. I
felt that I must do something. So I rang up
Kenilworth. A wild yell of delight came through
the instrument, and then another voice with an
awful Scotch accent.
‘ ‘ Mon, that was the nearest shave I hae had in
thirty year’s drivin’. Ah, laddie, if ye had no
come to the tunnel we had a’ been renderin’ our
’count at the throne o’ grace the now.”
Then the Kenilworth man explained that a stop
block had been left off at one of the Hillgrove
sidings and a dozen trucks had run down the line,
and got off the rails, and were piled in a heap
quarter of a mile south of Kenilworth. They
had managed to stop the Goods just as she
bumped them.
* * * *
At Romahapa I was received cordially by the
Traffic Office juniors, and admitted straight to
the Traffic Manager’s private office. My engrossed
report was lying on the table in front of him. He
went on glancing over it as I entered, and I stood
waiting till he should be pleased to notice my
presence. It was fully five minutes before he
glanced up from the paper and said :
“You’ll get yourself disliked if you indulge in
this sort of thing.”
“ What —preventing smashes ? ”
“No, you idiot; reporting your station-
masters.”
“ I expect to,” I said.
He got angry.
“You’re a troublesome fellow. You seem to
be deficient, somehow, in judgment. Here you
go and spoil a real good action by this sort of
report. You just go back to Ranelagh and mind
your own business. Keep your accounts straight,
your books clean and your batteries fully charged,
and you’ll get on much better, be more popular,
and never in rows. Good-day ! ”
He touched the bell and a tall cadet entered. I
was too furious to speak, and the rusii of words
stuck in my throat. I heard the cadet requested
to show in Mr. J. Smith, and somehow, next
moment, I found myself out in the public office,
surrounded by an eager crowd of juniors.
I listened to them in a dazed sort of way and
without a word wandered downstairs and back to
Ranel igh, where Plug greeted me with the remark
that I hadn’t been long.
* * * *
The new District Traffic Manager was stark and
business-like. He made a big fight to stem the
tide of beer that was threatening to destroy the
efficiency of the service. He issued a special
circular to the effect that the wages of drunkenness
on duty was sudden sack. _
On pay-day I avoided the station as though it
was infested with the plague. I didn’t want to
see Plug drunk, and I felt certain he would be.
Apparently he made a big struggle, but at 10.30
he was properly screwed. Ballantyro. signalled
the southern Goods, which was running about
three hours late. Plug promptly signalled her on
to Kenilworth. At the top of the hill one of the
piston rods blew out, or something like that, and
as the engine couldn’t haul the train over the knot
of the hill the only thing to be done was to slide
back as quietly as possible to Ballantyre by
gravitation.
Under no conditions did it ever take a tram
more than 35 minutes to come from Ballantyre to
Kenilworth, and when three-quarters of an hour
had passed Plug’s professional instincts became
uneasy. Was Kenilworth never going to clear
that block ? He waited a few minutes longer, and
then called up Kenilworth on the telephone and
asked why he did not clear the block.
“ Because the train hasn’t passed, you crimson
idiot! ” said the angry man at Kenilworth.
Then that angry man grew suddenly alarmed.
The frightful possibility that the great train with
its crew might be lying at the bottom of the bank
somewhere between 'Kenilworth and Ranelagti
appalled him.
“Bay, Plug, has the Goods passed?” ()
“Passed —of course she has ; 12 minutes ago,
replied Plug, who took things for granted. For
that matter a megatherium might have passed or
stayed away without him knowing anything to
the contrary. The man at Kenilworth clutched
the wall, and then sprang to the Romahapa tele-
phone and howled to the traffic clerk that a train
had got lost. At the same moment the manager
was reading Ballantyre’s telegraphed report of
the mishap and the information that the Goods
was back at that station.
They quieted the man at Kenilworth, and then
called up Ranelagh to inquire what lie meant Vy
saying the Goods had passed his station when it
had never even reached there.
Mr. Plug merely hiccuped into the instrument.
Twenty minutes later I was hauled out of bed
to relieve Mr. Plug, suspended for drunkenness
on duty.
His political influence must have been mighty.
Even the D.T.M. couldn’t get him sacked ; but
he got him shifted to where there was no block.

ACUTE ECZEMA CURED
After Many Treatments had Failed.
“I gradually sank into a state of ill-health,” says Elizabeth Corkran, of No. 8 Milton
Place, Richmond, Melbourne. ‘‘My blood became impoverished, and unsightly 'pininl
and blotches appeared on my face and neck. The doctors whom I consulted pronounced
it to be an acute form of Eczema, and, although they treated me for some time, my health
gradually grew worse. I could scarcely eat any food, and what I did eat disagreed with
me, and the suffering I endured at times was dreadful. I also tried various medicines
advertised, but without result, and I was altogether in a very despondent mood until one
day, I received a pamphlet wherein I noticed where fellow sufferers had been cured of
similar complaints to my own by the judicious use of Bile Beans and Zam-Buk Ointment
and I decided to give them a fair trial. I underwent a course of the Beans, and at th'
same time applied Zam-Buk Ointment to the blemishes on my face and neck. After &
week of this treatment I noticed some improvement, which became more pronounced week
after week, until now—just two months since I began the treatment—lndigestion has
entirely left me, and my skin is now quite clear, and, as you may imagine, such a perfect
and speedy cure came as a great surprise to me, after so many other treatments had proved
unavailing.” v

Aroha.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
with the years, oh, what is won ?
At Bethel a memorial stone—
A grave at Cave Machpelah.
Hark ! X hear the south-wind straying
Through the leaf-twined lattice bars,
And the fronds upon the palm trees
Shimmer ’mid the glint of stars,
And the odor of the sweet vine,
Tall and slender,
Holds me silent, till the few words,
Free and tender,
You once whispered, come like spectres
From the long past, Aroha.
I am standing where, in far years,
Through life’s flood ways love was flowing,
Every touch of earth and spirit
Freshest gladnesses bestowing;
For our earthly boon seemed dearest,
Brightest ever,
With our promise in low musio
Not to sever,
O, a strength of good lay lurking
In thy sweet lips, Aroha !
Moan I that the gleams of joyance
Brief as morning’s glow have been ?
You were angel, I was earth-stained,
All the spans that rolled between.
When your spirit passed and left me
Deep in sorrow,
I grew weary of the march-roll
Of to-morrow—
Clouds of mystery have chilled me,
I am lonely, Aroha.
Long I’ve wandered and I’m weary
Of the hollowness and stress,
Of the grave’s life and the shadowed
Pain-gulched brow of bitterness ;
Weary of the axe-head’s dulling
By blow on blow,
Of the grinding and the mill-wheels’
Chafe to and fro;
Heart of tenderness ! 0 for thee
Death was safer, Aroha !
Yet withal thy spirit’s radiance
Hath had power to keep from stain
Summer years of red-flushed splendor,
Winter’s grimness dashed with rain.
Do the precious links that tied us
Still entwine thee ?
Among seraphs art thou restless
Till thou find me ?
From thine heights—from gulfs that drown me,
Love is calling, Aroha.
n.s.w.
JOHN MARDEN.
Bill’s Selection.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A PAR in the Bulletin (28/2/’O3) about a selector
whose whole year’s work consisted of putting in
12 posts, recalls to my memory a selector who beat
that record easily. He put in only one post,
although he put that same post in many times.
It was this way: Bill Baggs (I’d like to oall
him Reginald for a change, but his name really
was Bill) had saved £lOO or so by dint of “hard
toil,” as he called it, and rigid economy in the
matter of clothing. Toil, as Bill understood it,
meant creeping around a boundary fence, and
occasionally mending a broken wire.
His ambition was to retire to a selection, where
he could sit down with his pipe and watch his
wool growing. Agriculture had no place in his
scheme. “Only mugs went farming.” So he
invested a portion of his savings in an
application for the full 2560 acres of grazing
country in the Central Division. After survey,
which, as usual, was completed in about
twelve months —when the former lessee had eaten
it bare—Bill made a start on his selection. At
the end of a month he had succeeded in finding a
name for it —Laurel Park. He had got it from a
page of a penny novelette wrapped around his
tobacco from the store.
“It’s an orlright name, ain’t it ? ” said Bill, with
aglow of pride in the discovery. “And when
I’ve shifted a few of the ironstone boulders lying
around, and sown a few laurels, it’ll just fit it.”
Bill put in another month or so roaming around
his estate in search of a suitable site for the home-
stead of Laurel Park. In my rides abroad a
cheerful “ Coo-ee ! ” often guided me to the top of
an adjacent ridge, where I would find Dill seated
on a boulder admiring the view—whilst, in his
mind’s eye, his future homestead grew up around
him.
“Reckon I’ve hit on the right spot at last,” he
would exclaim joyously, “I’ll have the house just
here, with a wide verandah facing the west, so
that when I’ve grubbed up all those trees out of
the way I’ll be able to look right across the river
to Gainer’s pub. The woolshed I’ll shove up just
over there, where those two iron-barks are dead in
line. They’ve both got forks about the same
height, which’ll save me putting in a couple of
posts for the wall plate. The stable will be just
over there, and that big yellow-box on the ground
will make one side of my cattle-yard.” Bill
would be heaving sticks and rocks about all the
time to indicate the exact positions of the pro-
posed buildmgs, or drawing ground-plans with
his finger in the dust.
I noticed, in course of time, that any site pos-
sessing natural features that suggested the saving
of labor was always favored by Bill. If it hap-
pened to possess only a couple of saplings growing
close enough together to fix a grindstone on, he
would hold forth at length about its splendid
advantages as a building site with all the enthu-
siasm of a witness (witli land to sell) giving
evidence before the Federal capital commissioners.
I had ceased to meet Bill in my rambles for
some weeks, when I heard he had at last fixed
upon the ideal spot for his homestead, and had
actually commenced work. Someone had met
him shifting a roughly squared corner-post with
the help of his old saddle-hack. He had found
an abandoned collar and had made the rest of the
harness after his own fashion. Like Bill’s saddle
and leggings, it was a practical illustration of
what could be done with green hide and fencing
wire. Some days elapsed after the receipt of
this news before I visited Laurel Park, and when
I called I was surprised to find Bill still at work
upon the solitary post I had heard about.
“Just wanted an excuse for a ‘smoko,’” he
exclaimed, throwing down his rammer and draw-
ing his pipe from his belt. “ I’ve been hard at it
all day laying out the homestead.” Heproudly
pointed out the parlor, kitchen, and storeroom he
had sketched in the dust. At my suggestion he
proceeded to add on a bathroom and a spare bed-
room, and was hard at it when the mailman rode
up with some Land Board correspondence for Bill,
and anxiously inquired if he was expected to chase
that blanky post of his all over the country.
“This is about the fourteenth time I’ve had to
track it through the scrub,’’ exclaimed the
aggrieved postman to me, while Bill grinned
apologetically ; “ When he gets that dashed post
upended on a new pitch, he puts in so much time
admiring it that before he can get a mate for it
his waterhole dries up, or he finds it’s too stony
for a garden, or the sun shines too much through
the droring-room winder, or some other blanked
foolishness, and I come along with his mail and
find his address is nothing but a hole in the
ground, with the track of a post being drawn
away from it. The department won’t stand it, so
I tell him straight if I find only a hole at this
address the next time I bring his mail, I’ll just
drop his flaming letters into it and leave ’em.”
I left Bill assuring the doubting mailman that
this was his permanent address. He conducted
him through the house, and drew his attention to
the size and comfort of the various rooms.
A month later I met Bill riding the boundary
of a neighboring run with a straining fork on his
shoulder. “And what about Laurel Park?” I
inquired, as he filled his pipe from my plug.
“ Oh,, it’s there yet,” he replied cheerfully.
“ Fact is, I went into town for a week’s spell, and
when I rode out to the selection again, and saw
that post by moonlight, it looked so dashed lonely
sticking up there in the middle of 2560 acres that
I hadn’t the heart to go on with the contract, so I
just came back and got on here at my old billet.”
That was years ago, but the post is still known
as “Bill’s Selection.”
CRIPPS CLARK.
The Bush Dentist.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A few years ago I was sent from Sydney
to take temporary charge of the telegraph-
office in the little saw-milling town of B ,
on Myall Lakes. A cold caught on the trip
brought on toothache. After two nights’ agony,
I asked one of the mill-hands if there was
a dentist in the town. When I had made it plain
that a dentist was a man who pulled teeth, I was
taken down to Sandy M‘Gill’s, who, I was
assured, was “ d n good at it. On the way I
learned that Sandy was the local blacksmith, but
in addition filled the positions of horse-doctor and
dentist with much success.
Sandy, dressed in moleskins and flannel shirt,
informed us when we called that he was just
washing his feet to go to bed. I was sorry to
disturb him, “ but the pain was awful.”
“ Come in and let’s have a look. Which of the
s is it ? ”
Sandy’s fingers forced my jaws asunder. “ Oh,
it’ll come all right,” he observed, after a brief
examination. “I’d fetch it if its roots were
twined round yer neck. Here Joe! ’’—the whole
M'Gill family had assembled— “ run down to the
forge and get the little pinchers—the ones I pulled
them teeth from MTherson’s black mare with.”
Perhaps I had turned pale, for Sandy said
soothingly: “ Have a drop of whisky while he’s
away ? Good stuff—made on the Lakes.”
I was a teetotaller, but the dentist helped him-
self liberally. “I think yer better come out on
the verandah to have it out. I generally pulls
’em out there, unless it’s kids. Things gets broke
in here sometimes, and the missus don’t like it.
See that winder ? Broke three panes in the top
sash last one I pulled in here. Bring yer chair
with yer.”
Then Joe returned and we all went to the veran-
dah, the missus carrying a candle, Sandy the
Eincers and the bottle. After some engineering, the
eavy pincers were inserted in my mouth. Then
protest or resistance became useless, “Here,
missus, hold the light round here, till I make sure
I ain’t got his tongue. D n you, what are you
doin’ ?”—this to the missus in response to a mute
protest on my part when some of the hot tallow
from the candle, also “made on the Lakes,”
dropped in my eye. Sandy removed the tallow
with his pocket-knife and adjusted the pincers to
his satisfaction.
“Hello, boys ! Come to see the fun?” The
news that the “Lightning Squirt” was getting a
tooth out down at Sandy’s had attracted a small
crowd of bullockies and mill-hands. Sandy spent
a few minutes talking shop, to give as many as
possible time to arrive before he began. “ Well,
if there’s no more coming, we’ll start,” he an-
nounced at last. “Hold on to yer chair hard.
Alick and Joe ! get hold of the chair-legs. Now,
altogether ! ”
The boys held on to the chair-legs, I held on to
the chair, and Sandy held on to the tooth. Down
the verandah we went, making a running fight of
it. Sandy paused at the end of the verandah for
breath. “By George !” he remarked, as he wiped
the perspiration from his face ; “it’s a tough ’un.
Here, missus ! bring me a drink.”
I looked at my torturer. He had settled down
into his collar. Remonstrance on my part would
be useless.
“Now, then! Altogether, once more.” Back
again up the verandah we went, the chair creak-
ing, the boys outside cheering, and Sandy forging
ahead. Still the tooth was immovable. The
boys were beginning to indulge in pleasantries, and
suggested a team of bullocks, or getting steam up
at the mill winch. Sandy’s professional pride was
wounded, and I saw the look in his eyes which
meant do or die. I resigned myself to the latter.
“ Yer better twist yer legs round the verandah-
post this time,” he directed. “I think it’ll
stand the strain. Missis ! another drink.” Out-
side the fence I could hear the money changing
hands freely, but whether the wagering was on
the strength of the verandah-post, the boys.
Sandy or the chair, I could not make out.
“Now, once more!” The strain was terrific.
The verandah-post creaked, the galvanised-iron
roof rattled. Click ! click ! I thought I could
feel my vertebrae yielding to Sandy’s strength.
“ Something’s coming.” The dentist’s voice was
exultant. Something did come. It took me
five minutes to recover. Then I saw Sandy
triumphantly exhibiting a row of teeth in the
pincers to the admiring audience.
“Good Lord !” I cried, “how many have you
got.”
“Only four,” he answered; “four beauties
It’s always as well to get two or three on each side
when yer at it, yer sure to get the right one then
and yer can’t never trust the blessed things when
once they start aching.”

MEMORANDA
W. J. Lambie and H. H. Spooner, with ten
others, are reported to Eng. Institute of Journalists as
subjects of the projected memorial to newspaper corres-
pondents who died in the African war.
An article in The Eng. Illustrated stresses the
power of the Dead Hand over English journalism. The
writer says that the character of Bond. Times, Sta,ndard,
Daily News, Morning Post, and Daily Chronicle is made
up of traditions which succeeding editors and staffs
regard as inviolable. “ What have we said before on this
subject? ’’was one of John Walter’s regular inquiries.
And when The Standard, a few years ago, to the surprise
of its proprietor, its literary editor, and everyone else,
published a leading article depreciating Meredith, it was
privately explained that the article was written without
animus, did not represent anybody’s opinion, but merely
continued the judgment passed upon Meredith by The
Standard in'lB6l, soon after the appearance of Richard
Feoerel!
This faith in the Gospel of Mummy is doubtless a rem-
nant of that instinct of ancestor-worship fixed in us by
the age-long practice of prehistoric progenitors. Sunday
pilgrims to suburban cemeteries, wandering happily
among the tombstones, are often unstimulated by affec-
tion for a buried relative. Without knowing it, they are
satisfying lingering instinct. “ I am so happy among
graves,” a woman will say who never heard of the rever-
ence due to manes. And Maude Victoria, trysting in the
shadow 7 of shapeless statuary, feels that her lover’s pledge
is sanctified by the neighbourhood of the Dead. “ Eppur
si muove ! ’’—Maude Victoria’s name is a proof.
Mummy will not prevail, but Mummy is mighty. Con-
sider the enthusiasm for Shakespear’s bones displayed
by N.S.W. Shakespear society. Shakespear’s bones have
undoubted literary value. A man interested in literature
and Shakespear is" necessarily interested in the scrutiny
and comparison of Shakespear’s origins, in ascertaining
how dross became gold in the alchemy of Shakespear’s
brain. But Mummy is always persuading his worshippers
to neglect the substance for the shadow, the future for
the past. In finding the legend-root some of us are apt
to miss the poetry-flower. Then, a man should gaze
forward, not back perpetually. Lot’s wife was salted :
take care you are not petrified. Even a moderately
Chinese devotion to great-great-Grandfather may undo
you. Your parents were fine fellows : they made you ;
but look to your children.
And aha! . . . Hope! In N.S.W. military manoeuvres
at Easter “ some of the [Australian] Scotchmen ” —not to
be targets of opposing marksmen —“adopted the pre-
caution of wearing their hirsute tassel at the rear.” A
fine symbol. Put Scotland behind, put Ireland behind,
put England behind—not as sources of inspiration, but
as goals of aspiration—and go forward with Australia !
James Chalmers the missionary’s autobiography
and letters have human value as well as religious ; but the
sale-value is religious. The cheaper edition at 3s. 6 d. will
be circulated more widely than a corellican novel.
“ What shall I do with my boy ? ” Make him a religious
publisher ! The Sunday-school audience can enrich him
at a single volume. Buying “something religious” is
Laodicea’s automatic-heater. The warmth of The Land
and, the Book on a drawing-room table may even
bring kitchen-Bridget to the housemaid’s-knee of
recantation. Besides .. . visitors! Si sanctitatem
quseris, circumspice. Who could distrust the careful
duster of Life of Luther in full morocco? Under the
shadow 7 of John Knox the handicapped Christian may
emulate a Hebrew in the shadow of his nose. So pub-
lishing piety pays ; and the servants oi the bookselling
Achish say unto him, Is not this David the king of the
land ? did they not roll one to another of him in news-
papers, saying, Saul Caine hath slain his thousands, and
David Sheldon his ten thousands ? Once, a Bible w'as the
believer’s library, literature’s be-all and end-all. Every
book added betokens slackening of the single heart,
change of the single aim, and exemplifies the advance of
unbelief. All grist to the publisher, who finds that the
thinner the religious layer, like glue, the better it sticks.
“Another suicide in the Yarra ! ” The Yarra
is too accessible. Its use should be prohibited under
Section 3 of Vic. Poisons Act.
The joke about Marcel Prevost, analyst of the
only-half-a-virgins, is that just before the lady shot at bni
he had published Les Lettres a Framboise, in which he dis-
cussed the education of girls seriously and paternaiiy.
Prevost has passed the critical fortieth year at which
Dumas fils recommends us to give up w'ild oats, and it
seems wrong that Nemesis should hit him just when he is
commencing to cultivate small domestic potatoes. One
relapses so easily.
A.G.S.
